Al-Razi's Argument and the Refutation on Comprehensive Knowledge
Al-Razi argues against comprehensive knowledge; the author refutes and clarifies the issue.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's objection to comprehensive knowledge and the author's refutation that distinguishes between the object of knowledge and the mode of knowledge.

Core Idea
Comprehensive knowledge is about the mode of knowing, not the object known.

This passage presents a key debate in Islamic theology: is comprehensive knowledge a distinct type of knowledge or just a combination of partial knowledge and ignorance? Imam al-Razi argued the latter, but the author of the Mawaqif refutes him by showing that the difference lies in the state of the knower's knowledge, not in the object itself. Let us examine the argument and its rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage fully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall flow. Notice the three parts: the preliminary distinction about accident, al-Razi's argument, and the author's answer.
Pay attention to the technical terms like 'العلم الإجمالي' and 'العلم التفصيلي'.
2
Understand al-Razi's reasoning
Al-Razi argues that if something is known summarily, it is known from one aspect and unknown from another. Thus 'comprehensive knowledge' would be a combination of partial knowledge and ignorance, not a genuine type.
He thinks the known and unknown aspects are distinct, so no single knowledge encompasses both.
3
Analyze the refutation
The author responds that the object known (المعلوم) is the same in both comprehensive and detailed knowledge. The difference lies in the knowledge (العلم) itself – its manner of grasping the object. Thus comprehensiveness is a mode of the knower's knowledge, not a property of the object.
The author uses the earlier accident example to show that one thing can be known from one perspective and unknown from another without contradiction.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends by noting that the second branch of discussion (about knowing from one aspect without another) also presupposes the existence of comprehensive knowledge, confirming its validity.
This shows that even al-Razi's own objection inadvertently relies on the concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يشتبه عليك بما أسلفناه لك أن عارض الشيء قد يلاحظ في نفسه فيكون العارض معلوماً مع كون حقيقة الشيء مجهولة فيتغاير المعلوم والمجهول، وقد يجعل آلة لملاحظة الشيء، وحينئذ يكون ذلك الشيء معلوماً باعتبار عارضه ومجهولاً باعتبار حقيقته فيتحد المعلوم والمجهول لكنه معلوم من حيثية ومجهول من حيثية أخرى ولا استحالة فيه، وبمثل هذا الذي ذكره القاضي استدل الإمام الرازي على نفي العلم الإجمالي في المحصل . فقال : المعلوم على سبيل الجملة معلوم من وجه ومجهول من وجه والوجهان متغايران والوجه المعلوم لا إجمال فيه والوجه المجهول غير معلوم البتة، لكن لما اجتمعا في شيء واحد ظن أن العلم الجملي نوع يغاير العلم التفصيلي والجواب : أنَّ الإجمال والتفصيل، ليس حالهما على ما توهمه بل المعلوم فيهما واحد والمختلف هو العلم المتعلق بذلك المعلوم، فتارة يكون ذلك العلم في نفسه على وجه، وأخرى على وجه آخر كما تحققته، فليس الإجمال بأن يكون الشيء معلوماً من وجه ومجهولاً من آخر، وإذا قلنا : هذا الشيء معلوم من حيث الإجمال دون التفصيل، كانت الحيثيتان راجعتين إلى العلم دون المعلوم، وبما قررناه يتضح أنَّ الفرع الثاني أيضاً فرع على ثبوت العلم الإجمالي كأنه قيل: هل هو من قبيل العلم بالشيء من وجه دون وجه أو
And let it not be confused for you by what we have already presented: that the accident of a thing may be considered in itself, so the accident is known while the reality of the thing is unknown; then the known and the unknown are distinct. And it may be made an instrument for considering the thing; then that thing is known by virtue of its accident and unknown by virtue of its reality; thus the known and the unknown are unified, but it is known from one aspect and unknown from another, and there is no impossibility in it. And by something similar to what al-Qadi mentioned, Imam al-Razi argued for negating comprehensive knowledge in the detailed. He said: The known in summary is known from one aspect and unknown from another; the two aspects are distinct; the known aspect has no comprehensiveness in it, and the unknown aspect is not known at all. But when they come together in one thing, he thought that comprehensive knowledge is a type different from detailed knowledge. The answer: that comprehensiveness and detail are not as he supposed; rather the known in both is one, and what differs is the knowledge related to that known. Sometimes that knowledge is in itself in one way, and another time in another way, as you have verified. So comprehensiveness is not that the thing is known from one aspect and unknown from another. If we say: 'This thing is known in terms of comprehensiveness without detail,' the two aspects refer to the knowledge, not the known. By what we have established, it becomes clear that the second branch is also a branch of the existence of comprehensive knowledge, as if it were said: Is it of the type of knowledge of a thing from one aspect without another?
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage presents al-Razi's argument against the existence of comprehensive knowledge (al-'ilm al-ijmali) as a distinct type. Al-Razi claims that if something is known summarily, it is known from one aspect and unknown from another, so the 'comprehensive knowledge' would actually be a combination of partial knowledge and ignorance, not a real type. The author refutes this by explaining that comprehensiveness and detail are not properties of the object but states of the knower's knowledge. The same object can be known comprehensively (with an imperfect grasp) or in detail (perfectly), and the difference is in the knowledge itself, not in the object. Thus, comprehensive knowledge is valid, and the second branch (the discussion of knowing from one aspect) is actually a subcase of the existence of comprehensive knowledge.

العلم الإجمالي
العلم التفصيلي
العارض
الجواب
الوجه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think comprehensive knowledge is a separate type of knowledge that has a different object than detailed knowledge.
Correction: The passage clarifies that comprehensive and detailed knowledge have the same object; the difference is in the mode of knowledge itself.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the refutation against al-Razi's argument?
A) Comprehensive knowledge is impossible.
B) The difference between comprehensive and detailed knowledge is in the object, not the knowledge.
C) The difference between comprehensive and detailed knowledge is in the knowledge, not the object.
D) Comprehensive knowledge requires a different object.

Key Takeaway
Comprehensive knowledge is not a distinct knowledge about a different object; it is a mode of knowing the same object summarily, and the distinction lies in the knower's knowledge, not the known.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage shows that al-Razi's error was conflating the mode of knowledge with the object known. Where in your own faith do you mistake a comprehensive understanding for a detailed one—perhaps in your belief in divine decree or the nature of the Quran? What step will you take today to seek detailed knowledge in that area?
?
Know that the accidents you experience (blessings, trials, signs) can either be ends in themselves or instruments to know their Creator. Which are they for you? What must change in your daily reflection to use them as instruments rather than stopping at them?

Active and Passive Knowledge
Sanusi divides knowledge into active and passive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between active knowledge, which causes existence, and passive knowledge, which is derived from existence.

Core Idea
Knowledge is not always receptive—it can create.

This passage introduces a key distinction in Islamic theology: knowledge can be either active or passive. Understanding this helps clarify how divine knowledge differs from human knowledge. Let's explore the definitions and examples provided.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic carefully, noting the two categories.
2
Identify the two types
Find the definitions of active and passive knowledge. Notice how they relate to existence.
3
Reflect on the example
Consider the carpenter's bed example: how does it illustrate active knowledge?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد التاسع : العلم إما فعلي وإما انفعالي . العلم إما فعلي: وهو أن يكون سبباً للوجود الخارجي كما نتصور أمراً مثل السرير مثلا ثم نوجده، وإما انفعالي مستفاد من الوجود الخارجي كما يوجد.
The ninth objective: Knowledge is either active or passive. Knowledge is either active: that it is a cause of external existence, such as when we conceive something—like a bed, for example—and then we bring it into existence; or passive: derived from external existence, as it exists.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage defines the ninth objective: a division of knowledge into active (fiʿlī) and passive (infiʿālī). Active knowledge is knowledge that causes something to exist in the external world; the example is conceiving a bed and then creating it. Passive knowledge is knowledge that is derived from an already existing object; it is receptive rather than productive. This distinction is important in theology because God's knowledge is active, as He creates by His knowledge. Human knowledge is typically passive, but can be active in the sense of craftsmanship.

Key Terms
العلم الفعلي
العلم الانفعالي
الوجود الخارجي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All knowledge is simply a reflection of reality; it cannot create.
Correction: This passage shows that some knowledge (active) causes existence, such as a craftsman's knowledge of a bed leading to its creation.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of passive knowledge?
A) A carpenter conceiving a chair and building it.
B) A person seeing a tree and knowing it exists.
C) A mathematician proving a theorem.
D) An artist imagining a painting.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge is not only receptive; it can also be productive, and this distinction is vital for understanding divine and human knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that knowledge can be active—it can create something new in the world. Think of one area of your life where you have passively accepted things as they are. What would it look like for you to use active knowledge to transform that area, even in a small way, starting today?
?
The example in the passage is a bed—an object of daily use. What 'bed' have you conceived in your mind (a skill, a relationship, a good habit) but never brought into existence? What is the first concrete step you will take this week to make it real?
?
Consider the knowledge you have about your Creator. Is it mostly passive—derived from what others say or what you've assumed—or is it active, shaping your actions and your very existence? If you truly believed that God's knowledge is actively creating your life every moment, what would you do differently today?

Active and Passive Knowledge Defined
Sanusi defines active and passive knowledge with examples and their relation to multiplicity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of active and passive knowledge, their relationship to multiplicity, and the philosophical commentary on universals.

Core Idea
Knowledge that causes existence is active; knowledge derived from existence is passive.

This lesson introduces the foundational classification of knowledge into active and passive, which the philosophers use to discuss divine knowledge. Understanding this distinction and the examples provided is essential before applying it to God. The author also addresses a nuanced view about universals that accompany multiplicity.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions and examples
Carefully read the Arabic text and its translation, noting how active knowledge precedes existence and passive knowledge follows it. The examples of a bed and the sky clarify the concepts.
Active knowledge is like a blueprint; passive knowledge is like a reflection.
2
Understand the relation to multiplicity
Notice that active knowledge is a universal before multiplicity, while passive knowledge is a universal after multiplicity. This distinction is key to the application to God.
Active gives rise to particulars; passive is abstracted from particulars.
3
Consider the note about universals
The passage adds a nuance: some scholars speak of a universal that exists with multiplicity but is not knowledge in the ordinary sense. Reflect on how this relates to the definitions.
This note prepares for the divine context where knowledge is unique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد التاسع : العلم إما فعلي وإما انفعالي ] العلم إما فعلي: وهو أن يكون سبباً للوجود الخارجي ( كما نتصور أمراً) مثل السرير مثلا ،ثم ،نوجده ، وإما انفعالي ) مستفاد من الوجود الخارجي ( كما يوجد أمر في الخارج مثل الأرض والسماء ثم نتصوره فالفعلي) ثابت (قبل الكثرة والانفعالي (بعدها أي العلم الفعلي كلي يتفرع عليه الكثرة وهي أفراده الخارجية، والعلم الانفعالي كلي يتفرع على الكثرة وهي أفراده الخارجية التي استفيد هو منها، وقد يقال : إن لنا كلياً مع الكثرة لكنه ليس من قبيل العلم أو مبني على وجود الطبائع الكلية في ضمن الجزئيات الخارجية ( قال الحكماء : علم الله تعالى) بمصنوعاته علم (فعلي لأنه السبب لوجود الممكنات في الخارج، لكن كون علمه سبباً لوجودها لا يتوقف على الآلات والأدوات بخلاف علمنا بأفعالنا، ولذلك يتخلف صدور معلومنا عن علمنا وقالوا : إن علمه تعالى بأحوال الممكنات على أبلغ النظام وأحسن الوجود، بالقياس إلى الكل من حيث هو كل هو الذي استند إليه وجودها على هذا الوجه دون سائر الوجوه الممكنة، وهذا العلم يسمى عندهم بالعناية الأزلية وأما علمه تعالى بذاته فليس فعلياً ولا انفعالياً أيضاً، بل هو عين ذاته بالذات وإن كان مغايراً له بالاعتبار، كما سيرد عليك إن شاء الله تعالى .
The Ninth Topic: Knowledge is either active or passive. Knowledge is active: it is that which is a cause for external existence — as when we conceive a thing, like a bed, then we bring it into existence. And it is passive: derived from external existence — as when a thing exists externally, like the earth and sky, then we conceive it. So active is established before multiplicity, and passive after it. That is, active knowledge is a universal from which multiplicity branches off — namely its external individuals — and passive knowledge is a universal that branches off from multiplicity — namely its external individuals from which it is derived. And it may be said: we have a universal along with multiplicity, but it is not of the type of knowledge, or it is based on the existence of universal natures within external particulars. The philosophers said: God's knowledge of His artifacts is active knowledge because it is the cause for the existence of contingent things in the external world. However, His knowledge being a cause for their existence does not depend on instruments and tools, unlike our knowledge of our actions. Therefore the occurrence of what we know lags behind our knowledge. And they said: His knowledge of the states of contingent things in the most perfect system and best existence, in relation to the whole as a whole, is that to which their existence is attributed in this manner without other possible manners. This knowledge is called by them the Eternal Care (العناية الأزلية). As for His knowledge of His Essence, it is neither active nor passive, but rather it is identical to His Essence by essence, though different by consideration, as will come to you, God willing.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage defines two types of knowledge: active knowledge (الفعلي) which precedes and causes the existence of its object, and passive knowledge (الانفعالي) which follows and derives from its object. Examples: conceiving a bed then making it is active; perceiving the sky then conceiving it is passive. Active knowledge is a universal that gives rise to external particulars, while passive knowledge is a universal abstracted from external particulars. The author then applies this distinction to God: the philosophers consider God's knowledge of creation as active because it causes existence, without instruments, unlike human active knowledge which requires tools and for which the effect lags. God's knowledge of the entire system of existence in the best possible order is called 'Eternal Care' (العناية الأزلية). Finally, God's knowledge of His own essence is neither active nor passive; it is identical to His essence, distinguished only conceptually.

Key Terms
العلم الفعلي
العلم الانفعالي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is a characteristic of active knowledge?
A) It is derived from external existence
B) It is a cause for external existence
C) It is always particular
D) It follows multiplicity

Key Takeaway
Knowledge is divided into active (causing existence) and passive (derived from existence), with active preceding multiplicity and passive following it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think about a recent decision you made. Did you first conceive it in your mind (active knowledge) and then act to bring it into existence, or did you simply react to circumstances (passive knowledge)? What does this reveal about your responsibility in shaping your life?
?
Sanusi says active knowledge is a cause for external existence. Your knowledge of good deeds—like prayer or charity—can cause them to happen. Are you using your knowledge to actively create good, or is your knowledge merely passive, acknowledging truths without translating them into action? What one deed will you actively bring into existence today because of what you know?
?
If God's knowledge is purely active, creating and sustaining all that exists, consider your own act of reading this lesson. Is your knowledge of this truth active enough to change how you live before Allah? Or is it passive—information you consume but do not embody? What would it mean for you to have truly active faith-based knowledge?

God's Knowledge: Active, Unique, and Eternal Care
Sanusi explains the philosophers' view on God's knowledge as active, introduces Eternal Care, and distinguishes His self-knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers consider God's knowledge of creation active yet distinct from human active knowledge, the concept of Eternal Care, and the unique nature of God's self-knowledge.

Core Idea
God's active knowledge causes existence without instruments and is called Eternal Care; His self-knowledge is neither active nor passive.

Having established the distinction between active and passive knowledge, this lesson applies it to God. The philosophers argue that God's knowledge of creation is active, but unlike human active knowledge, it requires no instruments and has no temporal lag. Moreover, they introduce a special term for His comprehensive knowledge of the best system of existence: Eternal Care. Finally, they note that God's knowledge of His own essence transcends this classification.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim: God's knowledge is active
Read the philosophers' statement that God's knowledge of His artifacts is active because it causes the existence of contingent things. Contrast this with human active knowledge, which depends on instruments and results in a temporal delay.
God's knowledge does not require tools; His act of knowing is identical to the act of creation.
2
Learn about Eternal Care
The passage introduces the term 'Eternal Care' (العناية الأزلية) for God's knowledge of the most perfect system of existence. Reflect on how this knowledge encompasses all possible states of creation.
This is a key philosophical concept: God's knowledge of the best order is the ground of existence.
3
Distinguish God's self-knowledge
Note that God's knowledge of His own essence is neither active nor passive. It is identical to His essence, though conceptually distinct. This prepares for deeper discussions on divine simplicity.
This shows that the active/passive classification does not apply to God's self-knowledge.

Clarifications on the Division of Knowledge into Active and Passive
Marginalia clarifying the active/passive division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the active/passive division is not exhaustive and how it applies to God's knowledge and knowledge of conceptual things.

Core Idea
The active/passive dichotomy does not cover all knowledge.

The marginal notes we are about to read clarify a crucial point: the division of knowledge into active and passive is not meant to be exhaustive. There are types of knowledge that fall outside this dichotomy, such as knowledge of conceptual things and universal knowledge not derived from particulars. The philosophers use this division to argue that God's knowledge is active, enabling Him to specify one of two opposites. These notes also address issues about the nature of active knowledge and God's self-knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage twice
First read the Arabic and translation to get the flow. Then note the three separate marginal comments.
2
Identify the three clarifications
The passage contains three distinct marginal notes. Find each one and understand the specific nuance being addressed.
3
Consider the implications for God's knowledge
Reflect on how these clarifications affect the philosophers' claim that God's knowledge is active and can specify one of two opposites.
4
Check your understanding with the quiz
Answer the checkpoint question to verify you grasped the key point about knowledge that is neither active nor passive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : إما فعلي وإما انفعالي ليس المراد الحصر، فإن العلم بالاعتباريات ليس شيئاً منهما، وكذا العلم الكلي الذي لم ينتزع من الأفراد الخارجية، ولم يكن سبباً لوجودها، بل مجرد بيان انقسام العلم إلى القسمين، توطئة لما يقوله الفلاسفة من أن علم الله تعالى فعلي يمكن أن مخصصاً، لوقوع أحد الضدين بالوقوع لا انفعالي تابع للمعلوم حتى لا يمكن ذلك . قوله: (أي العلم الفعلي كلي يتفرع عليه الكثرة إلخ) أي أنه قد يكون كلياً يتفرع عليه الكثرة، لا أنه كذلك دائماً، فإن العلم الذي يتفرع عليه شخص واحد كالعقل الأول بالنسبة إلى علم الله تعالى فعلي قطعاً عندهم. نعم بقي فيه بحث وهو أن ما ذكره يدل على أن التصور الكلي كاف في صدور الجزئيات، وهو خلاف ما صرحوا به وسيجيء أيضاً في المقصد الرابع من مباحث الأين على رأي الفلاسفة. قوله : ( وأما علمه تعالى بذاته الظاهر أن علم كل أحد بذاته أيضاً كذلك.
His saying: 'either active or passive' — the intended meaning is not exclusivity, for knowledge of conceptual things (الاعتباريات) is neither of them, and likewise universal knowledge that is not abstracted from external individuals nor a cause for their existence; rather it is merely an exposition of the division of knowledge into the two sections, as a prelude to what the philosophers say: that God's knowledge is active, capable of specifying one of two opposites for occurrence, not passive following the known so that that would not be possible. His saying: (i.e., active knowledge is a universal from which multiplicity branches off, etc.) — meaning that it may be a universal from which multiplicity branches off, not that it is always so. For knowledge from which a single individual branches off, such as the First Intellect with respect to God's knowledge, is certainly active according to them. Yes, there remains a discussion: that what he mentioned indicates that the universal conception is sufficient for the emanation of particulars, contrary to what they asserted, and it will come also in the fourth topic of the discussions of 'where' (الأين) according to the philosophers. His saying: 'And as for His knowledge of His Essence' — it is apparent that everyone's knowledge of his own essence is also likewise.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
These three marginal notes provide crucial clarifications on the division of knowledge into active and passive. The first note stresses that the division is not exclusive; there are types of knowledge that are neither, such as knowledge of conceptual things (الاعتباريات) and some universal knowledge. The division serves as a prelude to the philosophers' claim that God's knowledge is active and can specify one of two opposites. The second note clarifies that the description of active knowledge as 'a universal from which multiplicity branches' is not always true; for instance, the First Intellect emanates from God's knowledge as a single entity, yet that knowledge is still active. A problem is flagged: the statement might imply that a universal concept suffices to produce particulars, contradicting other philosophical positions. The third note observes that God's knowledge of His essence is neither active nor passive, and the same holds for any being's self-knowledge.

Key Terms
الاعتباريات
العقل الأول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All knowledge is either active or passive.
Correction: Some knowledge, like knowledge of conceptual things or self-knowledge, falls outside this dichotomy.

Key Takeaway
The active/passive division is a useful tool but not exhaustive, as conceptual knowledge and self-knowledge show.

Actual and Passive Knowledge and God's Knowledge
Defines actual and passive knowledge and states God's knowledge is actual.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between actual and passive knowledge and why God's knowledge is considered actual.

Core Idea
Actual knowledge causes reality; passive knowledge derives from it. God's knowledge is actual.

This passage from the philosophers introduces a key epistemological distinction and applies it to divine knowledge. We will see how actual knowledge precedes and causes multiplicity, while passive knowledge is derived from it, and how God's knowledge is the cause of creation without dependence on external objects.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation once to get an overview of the content. Note the definitions of actual and passive knowledge.
2
Identify the two types of knowledge
Focus on the definitions: actual knowledge is a universal from which multiplicity branches off, passive knowledge is a universal derived from multiplicity. Understand the cause-effect relationship.
Actual knowledge is like a blueprint; passive knowledge is like a summary after seeing examples.
3
Apply to God's knowledge
Read how the philosophers say God's knowledge is actual because it is the cause of existence. Also note the concept of 'eternal care' and the special case of God's essence.
God's knowledge does not rely on instruments, unlike human knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثاني - المقصد العاشر : قول الحكماء في مراتب العقل
أمر في الخارج مثل الأرض والسماء ثم نتصوره فالفعلي) ثابت (قبل الكثرة والانفعالي (بعدها أي العلم الفعلي كلي يتفرع عليه الكثرة وهي أفراده الخارجية، والعلم الانفعالي كلي يتفرع على الكثرة وهي أفراده الخارجية التي استفيد هو منها، وقد يقال : إن لنا كلياً مع الكثرة لكنه ليس من قبيل العلم أو مبني على وجود الطبائع الكلية في ضمن الجزئيات الخارجية ( قال الحكماء : علم الله تعالى) بمصنوعاته علم (فعلي لأنه السبب لوجود الممكنات في الخارج، لكن كون علمه سبباً لوجودها لا يتوقف على الآلات والأدوات بخلاف علمنا بأفعالنا، ولذلك يتخلف صدور معلومنا عن علمنا وقالوا : إن علمه تعالى بأحوال الممكنات على أبلغ النظام وأحسن الوجود، بالقياس إلى الكل من حيث هو كل هو الذي استند إليه وجودها على هذا الوجه دون سائر الوجوه الممكنة، وهذا العلم يسمى عندهم بالعناية الأزلية وأما علمه تعالى بذاته فليس فعلياً ولا انفعالياً أيضاً، بل هو عين ذاته بالذات وإن كان مغايراً له بالاعتبار، كما سيرد عليك إن شاء الله تعالى .
Second type - Tenth topic: The philosophers' statement on the ranks of intellect. Something external like earth and sky, then we conceive it, so actual (knowledge) is established before multiplicity, and passive (knowledge) after it. That is, actual knowledge is universal from which multiplicity branches off, which are its external individuals; and passive knowledge is universal that branches off from multiplicity, which are its external individuals from which it is derived. And it may be said: We have a universal with multiplicity, but it is not of the class of knowledge, or it is based on the existence of universal natures within external particulars. The philosophers said: God's knowledge of His creations is actual knowledge because it is the cause of the existence of possible things in the external world, but His knowledge being a cause for their existence does not depend on instruments and tools, unlike our knowledge of our actions, and therefore the occurrence of what we know lags behind our knowledge. And they said: His knowledge of the states of possible things in the most perfect order and best existence, relative to the whole insofar as it is a whole, is that upon which their existence in this manner depends, to the exclusion of other possible ways. And this knowledge is called by them eternal care. As for His knowledge of His essence, it is neither actual nor passive, but rather it is identical to His essence in essence, though different by consideration, as will come to you, God willing.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage defines actual and passive knowledge and applies the distinction to God's knowledge. It begins by illustrating actual knowledge as a universal that precedes multiplicity and causes it, like the mental concept of earth or sky before their external instances. Passive knowledge is a universal that is derived from multiplicity after experiencing it. The passage then notes a possible objection: we sometimes have a universal that coexists with multiplicity without being the cause, but such a universal is not considered 'actual' in this technical sense, or it relies on universal natures in particulars. The philosophers assert that God's knowledge of His creations is actual because it is the cause of their existence. Unlike human knowledge of actions, which follows the action and may be delayed, God's knowledge does not require instruments and is the direct cause. His knowledge of possible things in the best possible order, called 'eternal care', is the basis for their existence in that specific way. Finally, God's knowledge of His own essence is neither actual nor passive because it is identical to His essence, though conceptually distinct.

Key Terms
العلم الفعلي
العلم الانفعالي
العناية الأزلية
علم الله تعالى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's knowledge is passive, i.e., derived from external objects.
Correction: God's knowledge is actual: it causes the existence of things, not the other way around.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge of creation is actual because it causes existence, unlike passive knowledge which is derived from things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Abu Hashim's Claim of Knowledge Without a Known Object
Al-Jurjani presents Abu Hashim's claim of knowledge without an object and its proof from the impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu Hashim's argument that knowledge of the impossible is knowledge without a known object, and how it relies on the distinction between 'thing' and 'known'.

Core Idea
Knowledge of the impossible proves that knowledge does not always require an object that is a 'thing'.

Abu Hashim al-Jubbā'ī introduced a puzzling doctrine: that there can be knowledge without a known object. He argued that our knowledge of the impossible (like a square circle) is genuine knowledge, yet the impossible is not a 'thing' and thus not a known object. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and the logical proof he offered.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim
Read the passage and note that Abu Hashim affirms knowledge without an object, using the impossible as an example.
The term 'impossible' refers to logically impossible entities, e.g., a partner for God.
2
Examine the proof
Follow the logical steps: the impossible is not a thing (shay'), while the known (ma'lūm) is a thing. Therefore, knowledge of the impossible has no known object.
This is a simple syllogism: major premise 'the known is a thing', minor premise 'the impossible is not a thing', conclusion 'the impossible is not known'.
3
Consider the objection
Notice the commentator's remark that rational people agree that knowledge without an object is impossible. Yet the proof seems to show that we do have such knowledge.
The 'second figure' syllogism is used to derive that the impossible is not known, but we clearly know it, so there is a contradiction that forces the conclusion of knowledge without an object.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself why this matters for discussions of divine attributes. The doctrine challenges the common assumption that all knowledge must correspond to an external object.
This topic is part of a larger debate about the nature of God's knowledge and attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ المقصد الخامس عشر : علم لا معلوم له ]
( أثبت أبو هاشم علماً لا معلوم له كالعلم بالمستحيل فإنه ) أي المستحيل (ليس بشيء والمعلوم شيء ) فهاهنا علم لا معلوم له، وقد اتفق العقلاء على امتناع علم لا
·
قوله : (فإنه أي المستحيل ليس بشيء إلخ) ينتج من الشكل الثاني أن المستحيل ليس بمعلوم ولا شك في تعلق العلم به أو يحكم والحق عليه فثبت علم لا معلوم له .
[The fifteenth objective: Knowledge without a known object]
(Abu Hashim affirmed knowledge without a known object, such as knowledge of the impossible. For the impossible is not a thing, and the known is a thing.) So here is knowledge without a known object. And the rational people have agreed on the impossibility of knowledge without an object.
·
His saying: (For it, i.e., the impossible, is not a thing, etc.) yields from the second figure that the impossible is not known, and there is no doubt about knowledge being attached to it or judging it, and the truth is against it, so knowledge without a known object is established.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage presents the fifteenth objective of the second type: knowledge without a known object. The author (al-Iji) states that Abu Hashim al-Jubbā'ī affirmed the existence of knowledge that has no known object, giving the example of knowledge of the impossible (al-mustaḥīl). The argument: the impossible is not a thing (shay'), while the known (al-ma'lūm) is a thing; therefore, knowledge of the impossible is knowledge without a known object. The commentator then explains the logical proof: from the second figure of the syllogism, it follows that the impossible is not known. However, there is no doubt that we have knowledge of it (we know it is impossible). Therefore, there is knowledge that has no known object (ma'lūm). The phrase 'the truth is against it' may indicate that the premise 'the impossible is not known' is contradicted by our actual knowledge, so the conclusion is that there is knowledge without a known object.

مستحيل
معلوم
شيء
شكل ثاني

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All knowledge must have an external object that is a 'thing'.
Correction: This passage argues that knowledge of the impossible is an exception: it is genuine knowledge but its object is not a 'thing'.

Checkpoint
How does Abu Hashim's proof establish knowledge without a known object?
A) By showing that the impossible is known but not a thing.
B) By arguing that the intellect can know non-existent entities.
C) By denying that knowledge requires an object.
D) By defining knowledge as independent of objects.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of the impossible is valid knowledge that has no known object, challenging the assumption that all knowledge requires an external thing.

Definition of Will: Belief in Benefit vs. Inclination
Al-Jurjani defines will and presents the Mu'tazilite view and its critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two competing definitions of will (إرادة) as presented by Al-Jurjani: the Mu'tazilite view that it is belief in benefit, and the alternative view that it is an inclination following such belief.

Core Idea
Will is not mere belief in benefit; it is a distinct inclination that follows belief.

This lesson introduces the first goal of the third type of psychic qualities: the definition of will. Al-Jurjani presents two views: the Mu'tazilite claim that will is belief in benefit or its presumption, and the counterview that will is an inclination that arises after such belief. Understanding this distinction is crucial for later discussions on divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument. Note the two definitions and the evidence provided.
The passage includes commentary from the author, so read carefully.
2
Identify the two definitions and their arguments
Find the Mu'tazilite definition (will = belief in benefit) and the opposing definition (will = inclination). Note how each is supported: the Mu'tazilites rely on the equal relation of power, while the opponents cite self-awareness.
The passage also discusses animals to refute the Mu'tazilite definition.
3
Examine the objection and the evidence from self-awareness
The passage concludes with an objection: why must the preponderant factor be will and not something else? Consider how this challenges the second definition.
This shows that the debate is ongoing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثالث - المقصد الأول : تعريف الإرادة. من أنواع الكيفيات النفسانية الإرادة، وفيها أي في الإرادة وفي بعض النسخ وفيه أي في هذا النوع مقاصد سبعة. المقصد الأول : تعريف الإرادة. الأول في تعريفها قيل إنها أي الإرادة اعتقاد النفع أو ظنه، والقائل بذلك كثير من المعتزلة قالوا : إن نسبة القدرة إلى طرفي الفعل على السوية، فإذا حصل اعتقاد النفع أو ظنه في أحد طرفيه ترجح على الآخر عند القادر وأثر فيه قدرته. وقيل : ليس الإرادة ما ذكر من الاعتقاد أو الظن بل هذا هو المسمى بالداعية، وأما الإرادة فهي ميل يتبع ذلك الاعتقاد أو الظن كما أن الكراهة نفرة تتبع اعتقاد الضرر أو ظنه، وليست الإرادة من قبيل الاعتقاد والظن. فإنا نجد من أنفسنا بعد اعتقاد أن الفعل قوله : ( الأول في تعريفهما بعد الاتفاق على أن الإرادة مرجحة لأحد طرفي المقدور عن القادر اختلفوا في حقيقتها وهذا الاتفاق لا بد من مراعاته حتى يتم الاستدلال المذكور من كل فريق. قوله : ( اعتقاد النفع اعتقاداً وهي تخيل اللذة كما في الحيوانات العجم أو تعقلياً يتبع الفكر، كما في الإنسان. قوله : ( إن نسبة القدرة إلخ ) حاصله : إن اعتقاد النفع أو ظنه يرجح أحد طرفي الفعل وكل ما هذا شأنه فهي الإرادة إما الصغرى فلما ذكر الشارح وإما الكبرى فبالاتفاق. قوله ( وليست الإرادة من قبيل الاعتقاد إلخ) يعني أن قوله : فإنا نجد إلخ، دليل على المدعى الضمني وهو أن الإرادة ليست اعتقاد النفع أو ظنه وليست دليل الصريح وهو أنه ميل الاعتقاد لأن حاصله أنا نجد بعد الاعتقاد المذكور ميلاً مترتباً عليه مغايراً له، وإذا كان يتبع قوله : ( اعتقاد النفع أو ظنه للحيوانات العجم أفعال اختيارية فإما أن يقال بالفرق بين الأفعال الاختيارية والإرادية وإما أن يقال بوجود الاعتقاد فيها، وإما أن يخص التفسير بإرادة الإنسان والآخر أقرب لأن الحركة بالإرادة مأخوذة في تعريف مطلق الحيوان، ومن البين انتفاء الاعتقاد والظن في الحيوانات العجم. قوله : ( فإنا نجد من أنفسنا) يعني أنا نجد ميلاً هو مرجح لأحد المقدورين، والمرجح هو الإرادة فيكون الميل هو الإرادة ورد عليه بأنه لم لا يجوز أن يكون المرجح شيئاً آخر لا يعتبر فيه
The third type - the first goal: definition of will. From the types of psychic qualities: will. And in it, i.e. in the will, and in some copies 'in it' meaning in this type there are seven goals. The first goal: definition of will. The first in its definition: it was said that it, i.e. will, is belief in benefit or presumption thereof. And those who say this are many of the Mu'tazila. They said: The relation of power to the two sides of an action is equal. So when belief in benefit or its presumption occurs in one of its two sides, it becomes preponderant over the other for the capable agent and his power affects it. And it was said: Will is not what was mentioned of belief or presumption; rather that is what is called the motive. As for will, it is an inclination that follows that belief or presumption, just as aversion is a repulsion that follows belief in harm or its presumption. And will is not of the category of belief and presumption. For we find in ourselves after belief that the action... His saying: (The first in their definition after agreement that will is a preponderant of one of the two sides of the possible for the capable agent, they differed in its reality and this agreement must be observed so that the aforementioned inference is completed by each group. His saying: (Belief in benefit as a belief, and it is an imagination of pleasure as in dumb animals or a rational one following thought, as in humans. His saying: (The relation of power etc.) Its summary: that belief in benefit or its presumption preponderates one of the two sides of the action and whatever has this property is will, either the minor premise for what the commentator mentioned or the major premise by agreement. His saying: (And will is not of the category of belief etc.) means that his saying: 'For we find etc.' is evidence for the implicit claim that will is not belief in benefit or its presumption and it is not evidence for the explicit claim that it is an inclination of belief, because its summary is that we find after the mentioned belief an inclination consequent upon it different from it. And if it follows his saying: (Belief in benefit or its presumption for dumb animals there are voluntary actions) then it is either said there is a difference between voluntary and willful actions, or said that belief exists in them, or the interpretation is specified to human will and the last is closer because movement by will is taken in the definition of animal in general, and it is evident that belief and presumption are absent in dumb animals. His saying: (For we find from ourselves) means we find an inclination that is a preponderant of one of the two possible things, and the preponderant is will, so the inclination is will. And it is objected: why is it not permissible that the preponderant is something else not taken into account?
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a definition (تعريف) of will (الإرادة) and an exposition of two competing views. It begins by stating that will is one of the psychic qualities and that this type contains seven goals, the first being its definition. The first view, attributed to many Mu'tazila, defines will as belief in benefit or its presumption. They argue that power is equally related to both sides of an action, and when belief in benefit arises on one side, it becomes preponderant and power acts accordingly. The second view rejects this, claiming that belief or presumption is merely the motive (الداعية), while will is an inclination (ميل) that follows that belief or presumption, just as aversion is a repulsion following belief in harm. The passage provides evidence from self-awareness: we find in ourselves after belief a distinct inclination. The commentary then elaborates on the first word of the definition, explaining that belief in benefit can be an imagination of pleasure (as in animals) or a rational thought (as in humans). It clarifies that the argument from the Mu'tazila relies on the premises that belief in benefit preponderates and that whatever preponderates is will. The commentary also addresses the issue of animals: since animals have voluntary actions but lack belief and presumption, the definition of will as belief in benefit cannot apply to them; thus the second view, which defines will as an inclination, is more plausible. Finally, the commentary notes an objection: why must the preponderant factor be will and not something else?

Correction: The second view shows that will is a separate inclination that follows belief, not the belief itself.

The Eternal Will Necessitates the Willed Object
States that the eternal will necessitates the willed object with a distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Ash'ari school holds that God's eternal will necessarily brings about its object when it concerns His own acts, while there is disagreement about acts of others.

Core Idea
God's will for His own acts is always effective and cannot fail.

This lesson examines a foundational Ash'ari doctrine: the relationship between God's eternal will and the occurrence of the willed object. The passage distinguishes between acts God performs Himself and acts performed by others, clarifying where there is agreement and where the Mu'tazila differ.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the key terms like الإرادة القديمة (the eternal will) and المراد (the willed object).
2
Identify the two cases
The passage presents two cases: when God's will attaches to His own act, and when it attaches to another's act. Note the agreement in the first case and the dispute in the second.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila position
The Mu'tazila say that for acts of another, the meaning of the command is by a third preponderator. Reflect on what this might imply about their view on divine will and human action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الثاني : الإرادة القديمة توجب المراد ]
الإرادة القديمة توجب (المراد ) أي إذا تعلقت إرادة الله تعالى بفعل من أفعال نفسه لزم وجود ذلك الفعل وامتنع تخلفه عن إرادته (اتفاقاً من أهل الملة والحكماء أيضاً، وأما إذا تعلقت بفعل غيره ففيه خلاف المعتزلة القائلين بأن معنى الأمر هو بمرجح الثالث .
The second objective: The eternal will necessitates the willed object. The eternal will necessitates (the willed object), meaning: when the will of God Most High attaches to an act of His own, the existence of that act becomes necessary and its failure to occur is impossible, by agreement of the people of the religion and the philosophers as well. But if it attaches to an act of another, then there is disagreement with the Mu'tazila who say that the meaning of the command is by a third preponderator.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is a statement of a key doctrine: the eternal will (al-irada al-qadima) of God necessarily causes the existence of its object (al-murad). It specifies that when God's will attaches to an act that He Himself performs, the existence of that act is necessitated and cannot fail to occur. This is agreed upon by both Muslims (ahl al-milla) and philosophers (al-hukama'). However, when the will attaches to an act of another (i.e., a creature's action), there is a dispute: the Mu'tazila hold that the divine command (al-amr) is something else, implying that the will does not directly necessitate the act of another, but there is a third factor (perhaps the command). The passage is brief but establishes the Ash'ari position on the efficacy of the divine will for God's own acts.

Key Terms
الإرادة القديمة
المراد
الملة
الحكماء
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the Mu'tazila deny that God's will necessitates anything at all, or that the Ash'ari position applies uniformly to all acts.
Correction: The passage clarifies that both parties agree that God's will necessitates His own acts; the disagreement is only about acts of others.

Checkpoint
What does the Ash'ariyya hold regarding God's will when it attaches to an act of His own?
A) The act may or may not occur
B) The act must occur and its failure is impossible
C) The act occurs only if there is a command
D) The act is contingent and can fail

Key Takeaway
God's eternal will necessarily causes the existence of any act He Himself wills, but whether it directly causes acts of others is debated.

The Ancient Will Necessitates the Willed Object
Commentary on the necessity of God's will for its object.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's ancient will necessarily causes the act when it attaches to His own acts, and the Mu'tazilite exception for acts of others.

Core Idea
God's will is not arbitrary; it inherently demands its object.

In this lesson, we explore a foundational doctrine of kalam: the ancient will of God necessitates the existence of its object. This is agreed upon for God's own acts, but the Mu'tazilites introduced a nuance for commands to others. The passage clarifies the reasoning behind this agreement and the point of contention.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Let the author's own words set the foundation.
Pay attention to the distinction between acts of God's self and acts of another.
2
Identify the two scenarios
Notice that the passage presents two cases: when the will attaches to God's own act, and when it attaches to an act of another. Mark the different claims for each.
The first is agreed upon; the second has Mu'tazilite disagreement.
3
Analyze the reasoning
Understand why failure to occur would indicate inability (ʿajz) and possibility (imkān) in God. This is given as the reason for agreement.
The philosophers' view is also explained: God's will is knowledge of the most perfect order.
4
Examine the Mu'tazilite position
The Mu'tazilites identify command (amr) with will, and argue that command does not necessitate the commanded act, as seen with disobedients. This is the point of disagreement.
This shows the importance of distinguishing God's will from His command.
5
Connect to broader theology
Reflect on why this distinction matters: for God's own acts, His will is absolutely effective; for human acts, His command may be disobeyed without implying inability.
This preserves both divine power and human responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الثاني : الإرادة القديمة توجب المراد
الإرادة القديمة توجب (المراد) أي إذا تعلقت إرادة الله تعالى بفعل من أفعال نفسه لزم وجود ذلك الفعل وامتنع تخلفه عن إرادته (اتفاقاً من أهل الملة والحكماء أيضاً، وأما إذا تعلقت بفعل غيره ففيه خلاف المعتزلة القائلين بأن معنى الأمر هو الإرادة فإن الأمر لا يوجب وجود المأمور به كما في العصاة
قوله : (اتفاقاً) لكون التخلف دليل العجز والإمكان . قوله : ( والحكماء) حيث قالوا : إن إرادته تعالى هو لعلم بالنظام الأكمل من يتأخر بحيث تبعه الوجود.
The second aim: The ancient will necessitates the willed object.
The ancient will necessitates the willed object, i.e., if the will of Allah Almighty attaches to an act of His own self, the existence of that act becomes necessary and its failure to occur from His will is impossible, by agreement of the people of the religion and the philosophers as well. But if it attaches to an act of another, then there is the disagreement of the Mu'tazila who say that the meaning of command is will, for the command does not necessitate the existence of the commanded thing, as in the case of disobedients.
His statement: 'By agreement' because failure to occur is evidence of inability and possibility. His statement: 'And the philosophers' where they said: His will, exalted, is knowledge of the most perfect order that is delayed such that existence follows it.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal statement about the ancient will (al-irāda al-qadīma) of God. It begins by declaring that God's ancient will necessitates its object (tūjibu al-murād): if God's will attaches to an act of His own (such as creating the world), that act must come into existence and cannot fail to occur. This is agreed upon by both Muslims (ahl al-milla) and philosophers (al-ḥukamā'). However, if the will attaches to an act of another (i.e., a human being's action), there is disagreement with the Mu'tazilites, who claim that the command (al-amr) itself is will, and command does not necessitate the existence of the commanded thing, as seen in the case of those who disobey. The commentator then explains the glosses: 'by agreement' means that failure to occur would indicate inability (ʿajz) and possibility (imkān) in God, which is impossible. The philosophers' view is that God's will is identical to His knowledge of the most perfect order, which is temporally delayed but inevitably followed by existence.

Key Terms
الإرادة القديمة (al-irāda al-qadīma) – ancient will
توجب المراد (tūjibu al-murād) – necessitates the willed object
أهل الملة (ahl al-milla) – people of the religion (Muslims)
الحكماء (al-ḥukamā') – philosophers
الأمر (al-amr) – command

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's will does not necessarily cause the act; it may be contingent.
Correction: For God's own acts, His will necessitates existence; failure would imply inability. For acts of others (commands), the will is effective but obedience is not forced.

Checkpoint
In the case of God's will attaching to an act of another (e.g., human action), what is the Mu'tazilite view according to this passage?
A) They agree that the will necessitates the act.
B) They say that the command is the will, and command does not necessitate the act.
C) They deny that God has will altogether.
D) They claim that the will necessitates the act only if the act is good.

Key Takeaway
God's ancient will necessarily brings about its object when it concerns His own acts, but commands to others do not necessitate obedience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's will for your actions does not force them to happen, then what are you doing with the free will He has granted you? Will you choose to obey His commands today, knowing that disobedience is possible but a sign of your own weakness?
?
Consider a time you disobeyed God's command. Did you blame fate or think it was God's will? This lesson shows that God's command does not necessitate your obedience — so whose failure was it? How will you respond to His next command differently?
?
God's will for His own acts is always fulfilled. Does this make you feel secure or unsettled? If you truly believed that everything God decrees for your life is necessary and good, would your anxiety about the future dissolve? What is holding you back from that trust?

Eternal and Temporal Will: Necessity and Non-Necessity
Eternal will and temporal will: necessity and views

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the eternal will necessitates its object (unlike a command), while temporal will according to the Ash'aris does not necessitate, though some Mu'tazila allow it only if it is an intention (qasd) rather than a mere resolve (azm).

Core Idea
Eternal will directly necessitates; temporal will only accompanies.

This lesson introduces a key distinction in Islamic theology: the eternal will of Allah necessitates its object, but temporal will (as in humans) does not—except when it is a full intention. Different scholars have differing views, and we'll explore the Ash'ari position and the Mu'tazili exceptions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on eternal and temporal will
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the contrast between eternal will and command, and the discussion of temporal will.
The passage begins with the second objective: the eternal will necessitates.
2
Contrast eternal will with command
The author states that the eternal will necessitates its object, unlike a command which does not ensure obedience, as in the case of sinners.
This highlights that divine will is effective, while command may be disobeyed.
3
Identify the Ash'ari position on temporal will
According to the Ash'aris, temporal will does not necessitate its object, though it is concomitant with the action. Note that al-Jubba'i and later Mu'tazila agreed.
Concomitance (muqārana) means the will exists at the same time as the action but does not cause it necessarily.
4
Understand the Mu'tazili exception
Al-Nazzam, al-‘Allaf, and early Basran Mu'tazila allowed temporal will to necessitate only if it is an intention (qasd) towards the action, defined as what we find in ourselves at the moment of bringing the action into existence, not a resolve (azm).
This will be explained further in the next lesson.

قوله : ( بأن معنى الأمر هو (الإرادة أي إرادة فعل الغير والظاهر بأن إرادة فعل الغير هو الأمر به . قوله : ( عند الأشاعرة فالمراد بالاتفاق اتفاق منا. قوله : ( ما نجده من أنفسنا إلخ ) أي القصد التام المقارن للقدرة المستجمعة. قوله : ( وإن كانت مقارنة له أي على سبيل الوجوب وبهذه المقارنة صح تفسيرها بالصفة المخصصة لأحد طرفي المقدور بالوقوع وإن لم يتحقق الإيجاب . قوله : ( إذا كانت قصداً إلى الفعل قد سبق الآن أن الإرادة عند المعتزلة إما اعتقاد النفع أو ظنه وإما ميل يتبعه، فالظاهر أن القصد والعزم عند بعضهم من مراتب الاعتقاد أو الميل حتى لا يكون جعلهم الإرادة عبارة عن إحداهما مخالفا لما سبق، ولكن في الأول بعد ظاهر فتدبر. قوله : ( وأما الأشاعرة فلم يجعلوا العزم من قبيل (الإرادة تخصيص القول بأن العزم لم قوله : (بل أمراً مغايراً لهما يتقدم على وجود الفعل زماناً لما تقرر عندهم أن فعل المختار حادث وإن ناقش فيه الآمدي كما مر .
The third type - the second objective: The eternal will necessitates its object. This will, for the command does not necessitate the existence of the commanded as in the disobedient. (As for) the temporal will, it does not necessitate it by agreement, meaning that the will of one of us when it is attached to an action of his actions does not necessitate that object according to the Ash'aris, although it is concomitant with it according to them. And al-Jubba'i, his son, and a group of later Mu'tazila agreed with them on that. (And al-Nazzam, al-‘Allaf, Ja'far ibn Harith, and a faction of the early Mu'tazila of Basra permitted) its necessitation, i.e., the necessitation of the temporal will (of the object when that will is an intention (qasd) towards the action, and it) i.e., the intention towards the action (is what we find in ourselves at the moment of bringing into existence) i.e., at the moment of our bringing the action into existence (not a resolve ('azm) towards it) because if the will is a resolve towards the action, it does not necessitate the object, for the resolve may precede the action, so its necessitation of it is inconceivable. And they argued for that by saying that resolve is the soul's settling upon one of two alternatives after preceding hesitation between them. (And resolve) which is the settling (admits of intensity and weakness) and grows gradually (until it reaches the degree of certainty (jazzm)), so hesitation disappears entirely. (Yet despite that, the resolve that has reached the level of certainty may not be) concomitant with the action nor an intention towards it, but rather (is) a certainty that it will intend the action, thus it precedes the action without any necessitating factor. (And perhaps that resolve, i.e., the certainty, disappears) due to the disappearance of a condition of the action or the occurrence of an impediment to it, so the action does not occur after it either. And if the settling that has reached the limit of certainty is not necessitating of the action, then that which has not reached it is more deserving of not necessitating. So these (Mu'tazila) affirmed a will that precedes the action by times, which is the resolve, and they did not deem it necessitating of the object, and they deemed its necessitation permissible. As for the Ash'aris, they did not make resolve a type of will that is concomitant with it, but rather something other than it. He said: (by agreement) because failure (to produce the object) indicates incapability and contingency. He said: (and the philosophers) when they said: His will is His knowledge of the most perfect system that is such that existence follows it. He said: (that the meaning of command is) the will, i.e., willing the action of another, and it is apparent that willing the action of another is commanding it. He said: (according to the Ash'aris, so what is meant by agreement is our agreement.) He said: (what we find in ourselves etc.) i.e., the complete intention concomitant with the fully gathered power. He said: (and although it is concomitant with it) i.e., in the mode of obligation, and by this concomitance its interpretation as the attribute that specifies one of the two sides of the possible for occurrence becomes valid, even if necessitation is not verified. He said: (when it is an intention towards the action, it has already preceded that will according to the Mu'tazila is either belief in benefit or its presumption or an inclination that follows it. So it appears that intention and resolve according to some of them are among the levels of belief or inclination, so that their making will an expression of one of them does not contradict what preceded, but in the first there is apparent distance, so reflect. He said: (and as for the Ash'aris, they did not make resolve a type of will) specifying the statement that resolve did not... He said: (but rather something other than them, preceding the existence of the action in time, because it is established with them that the act of the free agent is temporal, even if al-Amidi objected to it as has passed.)
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is a combined definition and argument. The author begins by stating the second objective (al-maqsad al-thani): the eternal will necessitates its object. He immediately clarifies that this is not like a command, which does not cause obedience. Then he contrasts with temporal (created) will, which according to Ash'aris does not necessitate its object, though it is concomitant with it. He reports that al-Jubba'i and later Mu'tazila agreed, while al-Nazzam, al-‘Allaf, and early Basran Mu'tazila held that temporal will can necessitate if it is an intention (qasd) rather than a mere resolve ('azm). The author then explains their reasoning: resolve is the soul's settling on one of two options after hesitation; it admits degrees and may reach certainty (jazzm), yet even certain resolve may not be concomitant with the action, and it can vanish due to change in conditions. Therefore, if even certain resolve does not necessitate, lesser resolve is even less likely. The Ash'aris respond by denying that resolve is a type of will; rather, will is something distinct that is concomitant with the action. The subsequent 'qawl' statements offer further clarifications: the word 'by agreement' indicates that failure to produce the object would imply incapability; the philosophers identified divine will with knowledge of the best system; the meaning of command is will of another's action; 'according to the Ash'aris' means consensus among us; 'what we find in ourselves' is the complete intention with full power; concomitance in the mode of obligation allows defining will as the attribute specifying one of the two sides of the possible. The last note mentions that intention and resolve are considered levels of belief or inclination by some Mu'tazila, and the Ash'aris treat resolve as distinct from will, preceding the act in time.

Key Terms

الإرادة الحادثة
القصد
العزم
الإيجاب
المقارنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Temporal will always necessitates its object.
Correction: According to the Ash'aris and many Mu'tazila, temporal will does not necessitate; it is only concomitant. Only some Mu'tazila allow it if it is intention (qasd).

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'aris, does the temporal will necessitate its object?
A) Yes, always.
B) No, never.
C) Yes, but only if it is intention.
D) Yes, but only if it is resolve.

Key Takeaway
The eternal will necessitates its object directly, while temporal will (according to the dominant view) does not necessitate, with some exceptions for intention (qasd).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you resolve to do something for Allah but then fail to act, do you attribute that failure to a weak resolve or to the absence of true intention (qasd) at the moment? What does this passage reveal about the gap between your plans and your actions?
?
Consider a specific act you performed recently that you did for Allah's sake. At the moment of doing it, did you find in yourself the qasd described here—the actual intention accompanying the act—or were you merely going through motions? How can you train yourself to ensure your qasd is present?
?
If temporal will does not necessitate action (according to the Ash'aris), then what is your real role in your own deeds? Are you just a passive accompanier, or is there a deeper responsibility? Examine your heart: do you rely on your own resolve or on Allah's enabling?

intermediate
6 min
Resolve vs. Intention: Why Resolve Does Not Necessitate Action
Argument that resolve does not necessitate and Ash'ari distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why resolve (azm) does not necessitate action, even when it reaches certainty, and how Ash'aris distinguish resolve from will.

Core Idea
Resolve is not will; even certain resolve does not guarantee action.

The previous lesson introduced the idea that temporal will does not necessitate action unless it is intention (qasd). Here, we delve deeper into the argument that even a firm resolve (azm) does not produce the action, and why some Mu'tazila still consider intention (qasd) as necessitating. The Ash'aris respond by distinguishing resolve from will entirely.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument that resolve does not necessitate
Read the passage carefully. The author presents the reasoning that if will is a resolve, it cannot necessitate because resolve may precede the action.
Note the key definition: resolve is the soul's settling on one alternative after hesitation.
2
Understand the degrees of resolve
Resolve admits degrees: intensity and weakness, growing gradually until it reaches certainty (jazzm) where hesitation disappears entirely.
Even at the highest level, it is still not guarantee of action.
3
Consider why even certain resolve fails to necessitate
Even the most certain resolve may not be concomitant with the action; it could be a certainty that one will intend, and it can disappear due to changing conditions or obstacles.
This shows resolve is not a sufficient cause.
4
Reflect on the Ash'ari conclusion
The Ash'aris conclude that resolve is not a type of will; rather, will is something distinct that is concomitant with the action.
This resolves the issue by denying that resolve is will.

Why does even the highest degree of resolve (jazzm) not necessitate the action, according to the passage?

?

?

What condition for your worship is currently weak in your life—sincerity, knowledge, or consistency? What one step can you take today to protect that condition so your resolve does not vanish?
?
Ash'aris say that resolve is not will at all; will is something else that accompanies action. How does this distinction challenge your assumption that your resolve is your will? If your resolve is not your will, then what part of you is actually responsible for your actions? Are you ready to stop excusing inaction by saying 'I resolved to do it' and instead check whether your intention is truly present?

advanced
10 min
Will Not Conditioned Upon Belief in Benefit
Author proves will exists without prior belief in benefit or inclination, using the lion example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why will (al-irada) is not necessarily based on belief in benefit or prior inclination, using the example of a person fleeing from a lion.

Core Idea
Will can exist without any belief in benefit or prior inclination.

This passage establishes a key distinction: divine will (and human will in this example) does not depend on perceiving benefit. The author uses the example of a person escaping a lion with two equal paths to demonstrate that choice occurs without any rational preponderance. This refutes the Mu'tazila who tie will to belief or inclination.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the complete Arabic passage once, then read the literal English translation. Get a sense of the argument's flow before deeper analysis.
The Arabic is dense; the translation clarifies the logical steps.
2
Understand the thesis
Identify the author's claim: will is not conditioned upon belief in benefit (i'tiqad al-naf') or an inclination (mayl). Note that this contradicts the Mu'tazila.
3
Analyze the lion example
Focus on the proof: a person fleeing a lion, with two equal paths, chooses one without any belief that one path is more beneficial. This shows will operates independently.
Consider why equality is emphasized: it removes any external preponderance.
4
Consider the objection and answer
Read the objection: if will were conditioned, it could be defined by its necessary concomitant. The answer clarifies that this is a concession, not the main point.
The author's 'at all' (aslan) emphasizes strong negation.
5
Grasp the Mu'tazila clarification
Note that the Mu'tazila actually identify will with belief or inclination, not just conditionality. The author's wording targets those who make it a condition.
This subtlety affects how we understand the disagreement.

The third type - the third objective: Will is not conditioned upon belief in benefit. The third objective: Will is not conditioned upon belief in benefit or an inclination that follows it. And that is because will exists without them, so it is neither identical to one of them nor conditioned by it as well, so not by one of them at all. (Contrary to the Mu'tazila) who interpreted it as one of them. (Our proof) for the existence of will without them (is that the one fleeing from a lion, when) i.e. appears (to him two equal paths) leading to his goal which is escape from it, (he) despite being compelled in fleeing (chooses one of them) by his will, and he does not pause in that choice upon one of them being preponderant due to a benefit he believes (in it nor ...) He said: (Thus it is not correct to interpret it by one of them at all. If you say: The branching of the incorrectness of interpreting will by one of them on the negation of identity and conditionality has no basis, for the negation of conditionality has no bearing on non-existence, so even if it were established, that interpretation would not be correct. I say: After conceding that the branching is based on what was mentioned, not on the existence of will without them, it has a bearing. For if conditionality were realized, one of them would be necessary for will by necessity of the condition for the conditioned, so it would be correct to define it by its necessary concomitant. But it is to be considered that this holds only with the verifiers if the concomitant is predicable. However, it could be said that it would be correct to define it by one of them then as a concession, even if not correct in reality, so the intent is exaggeration in negating that correctness, as the word 'at all' indicates. And know that the apparent meaning of the author's words indicates that the Mu'tazila say that will is conditioned by belief in benefit or an inclination that follows it, but it is not so; rather, it is with them one of them itself. So the commentator, by adding his words 'thus it is not correct to interpret it etc.', pointed to the interpretation of his words. And the author's words have another interpretation closer than what the commentator mentioned: that it is to be understood as that will with us is not conditioned by one of the two things, contrary to the Mu'tazila, i.e., some of them who said that one specific is a condition for it. For whoever makes it the inclination itself that follows belief, for example, makes belief a condition for it.) He said: (contrary to etc.) For they say by the absent one of them, let alone conjunction. He said: (two equal paths) and that it is not hidden that considering equality is merely for clarification. For the hungry, the thirsty, and the thirsty choose one of the two things without conceiving of a preponderant factor or believing it, whether in reality there is a preponderant factor or not, and that is sufficient to establish the desired. He said: (and does not pause etc.) In the dictionary, 'pause' upon the name [؟] is fatigue. They made it a type of will, suggesting that intention is will with them, while it is not so because of what preceded in the discussion of pre-eternity: that intention is concomitant with the absence of the intended, while will interpreted as the specifying attribute is concomitant with the object willed, unless absolute resolve is intended and made to include intention.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof. The author states the third objective: will is not conditioned upon belief in benefit (i'tiqad al-naf') or an inclination (mayl) that follows it. He explains that will exists without them, so it is neither identical to them nor conditioned by them. This contradicts the Mu'tazila who define will as one of these. The proof is the example of a person fleeing from a lion who finds two equal paths to safety; despite being compelled, he chooses one by his will without any belief that one path is more beneficial than the other. This shows that will operates independently of such belief. The subsequent commentary ('qawl' statements) addresses objections: one might argue that the proof only works if equality is assumed, but the commenter says equality is just for illustration; even if no preponderant factor is perceived, choice still occurs. Another objection about conditionality is answered by noting that if will were conditioned on belief, then belief would be necessary for will, allowing definition by that necessary concomitant, but this is a concession and the main point is to negate any necessary connection. A further note clarifies that the Mu'tazila actually say will is identical to belief or inclination, not conditioned by them; the author's wording 'contrary to the Mu'tazila' means against those who made one of them a condition. The last remark distinguishes between intention (qasd) and will: intention is concomitant with the absence of the intended, while will (as divine attribute) is concomitant with the object willed, unless one intends absolute resolve encompassing intention.

Misconception: Will always requires a belief in benefit or an inclination preceding it.
Correction: The lion example shows that will can exist without any belief in benefit or prior inclination; it is an independent specifying attribute.
