The Proof That Created Ability Has No Effect
Proof using two opposing wills against effective created ability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam argument that created ability cannot be effective, based on the impossibility of two opposing wills producing contradictory outcomes.

Core Idea
If created ability were effective, conflicting divine and human wills would lead to impossible consequences.

This passage presents the central proof that created ability has no causal effect. It assumes that if human ability produced acts, then when God wills one thing and a person wills the opposite, both must occur (impossible) or both fail (impossible). The argument is then defended against objections, including the possibility of joint causation and a subtle interpretation of a Qur'anic verse. The discussion reveals the careful logical structure of Ash'ari theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Note the logical steps: assumption, conditional, objection, response.
The passage includes commentary inserts; focus on the main argument chain.
2
Identify the core proof
Find the conditional statement: if both abilities are effective, then opposing wills lead to impossible states (both acts together or both absent). This proves created ability is not effective.
The proof relies on the impossibility of two opposites coexisting or both failing.
3
Examine the first objection and response
Some argue the proof is unnecessary because two causes for one effect is impossible. The response shows joint causation (like two persons carrying a board) is possible, so the contradiction argument is needed.
This aligns with the doctrine of Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini.
4
Understand the second objection regarding Q.81:29
An objection claims the servant cannot will contrary to God's will based on the verse. The response offers a nuanced interpretation: God wills the servant's willing itself, not its object, so no contradiction arises.
This preserves human agency while affirming divine omnipotence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدليل على أن القدرة الحادثة ليست مؤثرة، أنه لو كان فعل العبد بقدرته وتأثيرها فيه، وأنه واقع بقدرة الله تعالى، بل هو واقع بتأثيرها فيه لما سنبرهن على أنه تعالى قادر على جميع الممكنات، بل جميعها صادرة عنه. فلو أراد الله شيئاً من الأفعال المقدورة للعباد وأراد العبد ضده لزم إما وقوعهما معاً فيلزم اجتماع الضدين، أو عدمهما معاً، ولا شك أن المانع من... قوله: (بل هو واقع) إضراب عن قوله واقع بقدرته، لأن الوقوع بقدرته تعالى من غير تأثير في الفعل لا يوجب إمكان التمانع بين القدرتين، فلا يتفرع قوله: فلو أراد إلخ. قوله: (بل جميعها صادرة) بالنصب عطفاً على الضمير المنصوب في أنه وفائدة الإضراب ظاهر، لأن إقامة البرهان على أنه تعالى قادر على جميع الممكنات لا يثبت وقوع فعل العبد بتأثير قدرته تعالى. قوله: ( فلو أراد إلخ ) قيل: لا حاجة إلى هذا الكلام لأن جميع الممكنات إذا كانت واقعة بتأثير قدرته فلو وقع واحد منها بقدرة العبد يلزم اجتماع علتين على معلول واحد بالشخص وأنه محال، كما بين في موضعه وليس بشيء، لأن اللازم مما ذكرنا تأثير القدرة للعبد وقدرته تعالى في أفعاله، فيجوز أن يكون واقعة بمجموع القدرتين بأن يريد بكل منهما ما يريد الآخر، فحينئذ تكون العلة المستقلة مجموعها، وإن كان كل واحدة منهما كافية في وقوعه، كما الخشبة المحمولة لاثنين مع كون كل واحد منهما كافياً حملها، وهو مذهب الأستاذ أبي إسحاق في أفعال العبد فلا بد من اعتبار التمانع المشار إليه بقوله: فلو أراد إلخ. قوله: ( وأراد العبد ضده ) وليس إرادة العبد خلاف ما أراد الله ممتنعة على ما وهم لوقوع خلاف مراد العبد لقوله تعالى: وما تشاؤون إلا أن يشاء الله [التكوير: 29]. قوله: ( لزم إما وقوعهما إلخ ) أي بعد تأثير قدرة كل منهما على وفق الإرادة. قوله: ( بل جميعها صادرة عنه تعالى ) فإن قلت بهذا القدر يتم الكلام ولا حاجة إلى قوله: فلو أراد الله تعالى إلخ، لأن جميع الممكنات إذا كانت صادرة عنه تعالى فلو وقع واحد منها بقدرة العبد يلزم اجتماع علتين على معلول واحد بالشخص، وأنه محال كما بين في موضعه قلت: نعم إلا أن المصنف أراد بقوله: واقع بقدرة الله تعالى أن قدرته تعالى متعلقة بفعل العبد، بدليل قوله: لما سنبرهن على أنه تعالى قادر على جميع الممكنات ولهذا احتاج إلى قوله: فلو أراد الله تعالى إلخ، وأما قول الشارح: بل هو واقع بتأثيرها فيه، وقوله بل جميعها صادرة عنه فهو بيان للواقع من الشارح لا أنه مراد المصنف. قوله: ( وأراد العبد ضده ) قيل: هذا فرض محال يجوز أن يستلزم محالاً آخر وذلك لأن مناقضة إرادة العبد إرادة الله تعالى لا يجوز نقلاً، لقوله تعالى: وما تشاؤون إلا أن يشاء الله [التكوير: 29] والجواب أن ما ذكر إنما يتم إذا كان معنى الآية وما تشاؤون كم شيئاً إلا أن يشاء الله ذلك الشيء، وأما إذا كان معناها وما تشاؤون إلا أن يشاء الله تعالى مشيئتكم، فلا مناقضة حينئذ لجواز أن يشاء تعالى حركة زيد ومشيئة زيد سكون نفسه غايته أن زيد بدون حصول مراده، ولا محذور فيه إلا أن يحمل مشيئة العبد على الصفة الموجبة المقارنة.
The proof that the created ability is not effective is that if the servant's act were by his ability and its effect, and it occurs by the power of God, rather it occurs by its effect because we will prove that He is capable of all possible things, indeed all of them originate from Him. So if God wills something from the acts possible to the servants and the servant wills its opposite, then either both occur together, necessitating the conjunction of two opposites, or both do not occur, and there is no doubt that the preventing... He said: (Rather it occurs) is a digression from his statement 'occurs by His power', because occurrence by His power without effect in the act does not necessitate the possibility of opposition between the two powers, so his statement 'so if He willed etc.' does not follow. He said: (Rather all of them originate) in the accusative as an apposition to the accusative pronoun in 'that' and the benefit of the digression is apparent, because establishing the proof that He is capable of all possible things does not prove the occurrence of the servant's act by the effect of His power. He said: (So if He willed etc.) It was said: There is no need for this statement because if all possible things occur by the effect of His power, then if one of them occurred by the servant's power, it would necessitate two causes on one identical effect, and that is impossible, as established in its place. But it is nothing, because what is necessitated from what we mentioned is the effect of the servant's ability and His power in his acts, so it is possible that it occurs by the sum of the two powers, by each willing what the other wills. In that case, the independent cause is their sum, even if each one of them were sufficient for its occurrence, like a board carried by two persons while each one of them is sufficient to carry it. This is the doctrine of the master Abu Ishaq regarding the acts of the servant, so one must consider the contradiction indicated by his statement: 'So if He willed etc.' He said: (And the servant willed its opposite) and the servant's willing contrary to what God willed is not impossible, contrary to what is imagined, because the opposite of the servant's will occurs, according to His statement: 'And you do not will except that God wills' [81:29]. He said: (Then either both occur etc.) meaning after the effect of each power according to the will. He said: (Rather all of them originate from Him) For if you say: with this amount the speech is complete and there is no need for his statement: 'So if God willed etc.', because if all possible things originate from Him, then if one of them occurred by the servant's power, it would necessitate two causes on one identical effect, and that is impossible as established in its place. I say: Yes, except that the author intended by his statement 'occurs by the power of God' that His power is connected to the servant's act, as indicated by his statement: 'because we will prove that He is capable of all possible things', and therefore he needed his statement: 'So if God willed etc.' As for the commentator's statement: 'Rather it occurs by its effect', and his statement 'rather all of them originate from Him', it is an explanation of reality from the commentator, not what the author intended. He said: (And the servant willed its opposite) It was said: This is an impossible hypothesis that can entail another impossibility, because contradicting the servant's will to God's will is not permissible by transmission, according to His statement: 'And you do not will except that God wills' [81:29]. The answer is that what was mentioned only holds if the meaning of the verse is 'You do not will any thing except that God wills that thing', but if its meaning is 'You do not will except that God wills your willing', then there is no contradiction, because it is permissible that He wills Zayd's movement and Zayd wills his own stillness, the result being that Zayd does not obtain his desired object, and there is no objection except that the servant's will is interpreted as the necessary concomitant attribute.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage presents the central proof that created ability has no effect (ta'thir). The proof assumes that if the servant's act were produced by his own ability, and simultaneously produced by divine power, then if God wills one thing and the servant wills the opposite, both would have to occur (impossible because opposites cannot coexist) or both would fail to occur (impossible because God's will cannot be thwarted). The passage then addresses objections. First, some argue the proof is unnecessary because all acts originate from God, so positing a second cause would entail two causes for one effect, which is impossible. The response counters that one could conceive of the act as produced by both causes together, each sufficient but jointly forming the complete cause, like a board carried by two persons each able to carry it alone. This is the position of Abu Ishaq, and thus the contradiction argument is needed to rule out such joint causation. Second, an objection regarding the verse 'You do not will except that God wills' (81:29) is answered by a nuanced interpretation: the verse may mean God wills your willing itself, not the object of your will, so there is no contradiction if God wills Zayd's movement while Zayd wills his own stillness—Zayd simply does not get what he wants, which is unproblematic. The passage concludes by noting that the commentator's interpretation differs from the author's intent.

Key Terms
قدرة
تأثير
علتين على معلول واحد
تمانع
إرادة
مشيئة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the proof is unnecessary because two causes for one effect is impossible, so the contradiction argument is redundant.
Correction: The passage shows that joint causation is conceivable (like two persons carrying a board), so the stronger contradiction argument is needed to rule out such a scenario.

Checkpoint
What does the proof using two opposing wills aim to demonstrate?
A) That God's will always overrides human will.
B) That if created ability were effective, conflicting wills would lead to impossible consequences.
C) That the servant's will is never contrary to God's will.
D) That human ability can cooperate with divine power.

Key Takeaway
The proof that created ability has no effect hinges on the impossibility of two opposing wills producing their objects, refuting the notion that human power contributes to acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you plan your day, do you truly believe that your own ability is ineffective without God's will? Or do you act as if your success depends solely on your effort? How can you remind yourself of this truth in your daily tasks?
?
The passage shows that your will can oppose God's will, but your will has no effect. Have you ever desired something that you knew was against God's decree? How does this lesson change your attitude towards disappointment?
?
If created ability has no effect, what does it mean for your sense of responsibility? Are you tempted to use this as an excuse for inaction? How can you balance trust in God's power with your duty to strive?

God's Power Does Not Override Human Power
Refutation of the claim that God's power overrides human power due to its comprehensiveness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the generality of God's power does not negate the influence of human power on a specific act, and how the argument against divine overriding is structured.

Core Idea
General power does not give an advantage when both powers target the same act.

This passage addresses a common objection in the debate over free will and divine power: if God's power is more complete and general, doesn't it automatically override human power? The author refutes this by analyzing the relationship between two powers directed at the same object. Understanding this refutation clarifies a key principle in Sunni theology regarding human capacity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector claims that God's power is more complete and general, so it should override human power in any act. Note how they argue it's like a stronger impediment.
The objection is presented in the first part of the passage.
2
Analyze the rebuttal
The commentator responds that the generality of God's power has no effect when both powers are directed to the same specific act. They become equal in relation to that act.
Compare with the proof of mutual impediment (tamānu') for oneness, where deficiency in power is not allowed.
3
Consider the nuance about inequality
The passage acknowledges a possible objection: powers can differ in degree (intensity/weakness), so they might not be equal. This is a point of discussion.
This nuance shows the depth of the argument.
4
Connect to Jahm's view
The same proof used to refute the objection was used by Jahm to deny human power entirely. Understand why Jahm's conclusion is different.
This highlights sectarian differences within early Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال : نختار أنه يقع مقدوراً لله تعالى لأنَّ قدرته أتم من قدرة العبد (ألا ترى أنها أعم ) منها لتعلقها بما لا يتصور تعلق قدرة العبد به ولا يلزم حينئذ عدم تأثير قدرة العبد في فعل أصلاً، بل يلزم تخلف أثرها عنها في هذه الصورة المفروضة لمانع أقوى منها . أعني : قدرة الله تعالى ولا يمكن أن يقال مثل ذلك في دليل التمانع على الوحدانية، لأن تخلف الأثر نقصان في القدرة والناقص لا يكون إلها، ويجوز يكون عبداً ) لأنا نقول : عموم القدرة لا يؤثر فإن تعلق القدرة بغير المقدور المعين لا أثر له في هذا المعين (ضرورة فلما فرض تعلق قدرتهما بمقدور معين كانت القدرتان متساويتين بالقياس إليه فكان تأثيرهما في طرفيه على سواء، فكون تأثير إحداهما مانعاً من تأثير الأخرى دون العكس ترجيح بلا مرجح، وفيه بحث لأنَّ تعلق القدرتين بمقدور معين لا يستلزم تساويهما لجواز أن يكون أحد القادرين أقدر عليه من الآخر مع تشاركهما في كون ذلك المعين مقدوراً لهما، فإن اختلاف مراتب القدرة بحسب الشدة والضعف جائز ) وبهذا الدليل الذي نفينا به تأثير القدرة الحادثة ( بعينه نفى جهم ( القدرة ( الحادثة ) فقال : لو كان للعبد قدرة على فعل مع
It cannot be said: We choose that it occurs as an act of God, because His power is more complete than the power of the servant, do you not see that it is more general, because it is connected to what the power of the servant cannot be connected to? And it does not follow then the non-influence of the power of the servant in the act at all, but rather the failure of its effect follows from it in this supposed situation due to a stronger impediment from it, namely the power of God. And it cannot be said similarly in the proof of mutual impediment for oneness, because the failure of the effect is a deficiency in power, and the deficient cannot be a god, but it is permissible for it to be a servant. We say: Generality of power does not have effect, for the connection of power to that which is not the specific object of power has no effect on this specific object of power. Necessarily, when the connection of both powers to a specific object of power is assumed, the two powers are equal in relation to it, so their influence on both sides is equal. So the influence of one preventing the influence of the other without the opposite is a preference without a preponderator. And there is discussion about this, because the connection of two powers to a specific object of power does not necessitate their equality, for it is possible that one of the two capable beings is more capable of it than the other, with their sharing in that specific object being an object of power for both of them. For indeed, differences in the ranks of power with respect to intensity and weakness are permissible. And with this proof by which we negated the influence of the originated power, Jahm likewise negated the originated power, saying: If the servant had power over an act...
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes an objection: one might argue that the act occurs by God's power alone because God's power is more complete and general (it extends to things beyond human power). The objector claims this does not deny human power any influence; rather, God's power simply overrides it like a stronger impediment. The commentator responds that the generality of God's power does not give it any advantage when both powers are directed at the same specific act, because they are equal in relation to that act. To say one prevents the other without reason is arbitrary. The passage also notes a nuance: one could argue that powers can differ in degree (intensity/weakness), so equality is not necessary. Finally, it mentions that Jahm used the same reasoning (negating human power to avoid two causes) to deny human power entirely, i.e., if a servant had power, it would lead to two causes, so power must be negated from the servant.

Key Terms
أتم
أعم
ترجيح بلا مرجح
قدرة حادثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's power being more general and complete automatically overrides human power in any specific act.
Correction: The generality of God's power does not give it special force when both powers are directed to the same object; they become equal in that relation, so the override claim is arbitrary without a preponderator.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator reject the objection that God's power overrides human power because it is more general?
A) Because God's power is not actually more general.
B) Because generality of power has no effect on a specific object when both powers are directed to it; they become equal in that relation.
C) Because the servant's power is not real.
D) Because the argument only applies to the proof of oneness, not to human acts.

Key Takeaway
The generality of God's power does not negate human power in a specific act because both powers become equal in relation to that act; the argument that one overrides without reason is arbitrary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever catch yourself thinking, 'It was God's will' or 'God made me do it' to avoid full responsibility? After reading this lesson, can you still hide behind God's power?
?
If God's general power does not override your ability to choose a specific act, then your actions are truly yours. How does that change your view of repentance and the need to change?
?
The text warns that the same logic used to affirm human power can be twisted to deny it. Are there beliefs you hold that, taken too far, could undermine other truths you know about Allah and yourself?

Proof That Two Equally Powerful Agents Cannot Share an Act
Reductio proof that two agents cannot share power over one act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reductio proof that two equally powerful agents cannot both have power over the same act.

Core Idea
Shared power over one act leads to logical absurdity.

This lesson presents a classic kalam proof using reductio ad absurdum. The argument assumes two agents with equal power over one act and shows that any conflict of will results in an impossibility. This establishes that power over an act must be exclusive.

Guided Path
1
Understand the assumption
Read the opening sentence that sets up the scenario of two agents each having power over a single act. Note that the act is assumed to be within the power of both.
2
Follow the reductio argument
The argument considers two cases when wills differ: either both acts occur (impossible) or one agent fails (contradicts the assumption). Identify each case in the text.
These are the two impossibilities mentioned.
3
Conclude the impossibility
Since both possibilities lead to absurdity, the initial assumption that two agents can share power over one act must be false.
This is the core of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الرابع - المقصد الأول : تعريف القدرة
ان وقوع مراد كل منهما وقوع مراد الآخر، فإذا لم يقعا وجب وقوعهما معاً، ويلزم ذلك المحال، وأيضاً إذا فرض ضدان لا واسطة بينهما كان عدمهما معاً محالاً (أو كون أحدهما (عاجزاً غير قادر على ما فرض قدرته عليه وتأثيره فيه، وهو أيضاً محال (لا يقال : نختار أنه يقع مقدوراً لله تعالى لأنَّ قدرته أتم من قدرة العبد (ألا ترى أنها أعم ) منها لتعلقها بما لا يتصور تعلق قدرة العبد به ولا يلزم حينئذ عدم تأثير قدرة العبد في فعل أصلاً، بل يلزم تخلف أثرها عنها في هذه الصورة المفروضة لمانع أقوى منها . أعني : قدرة الله تعالى ولا يمكن أن يقال مثل ذلك في دليل التمانع على الوحدانية، لأن تخلف الأثر نقصان في القدرة والناقص لا يكون إلها، ويجوز يكون عبداً ) لأنا نقول : عموم القدرة لا يؤثر فإن تعلق القدرة بغير المقدور المعين لا أثر له في هذا المعين (ضرورة فلما فرض تعلق قدرتهما بمقدور معين كانت القدرتان متساويتين بالقياس إليه فكان تأثيرهما في طرفيه على سواء، فكون تأثير إحداهما مانعاً من تأثير الأخرى دون العكس ترجيح بلا مرجح، وفيه بحث لأنَّ تعلق القدرتين بمقدور معين لا يستلزم تساويهما لجواز أن يكون أحد القادرين أقدر عليه من الآخر مع تشاركهما في كون ذلك المعين مقدوراً لهما، فإن اختلاف مراتب القدرة بحسب الشدة والضعف جائز )
The Fourth Type - First Aim: Definition of Power. That the occurrence of the will of each of them is the occurrence of the will of the other. If they do not occur, then their occurrence together is necessary, and that entails the impossible. Also, if two opposites with no intermediary are assumed, their non-existence together is impossible. Or that one of them is incapable, not having power over what his power was assumed to be upon and his effect in it, and that is also impossible. It cannot be said: we choose that it occurs as something within God's power because His power is more complete than the power of the servant. Do you not see that it is more general than it, because it is attached to what the attachment of the servant's power cannot be conceived, and then the non-effect of the servant's power in any act does not follow; rather, the failure of its effect in this assumed case follows due to a stronger obstacle from it, namely God's power. And it cannot be said similarly in the proof of mutual hindrance for oneness, because failure of effect is a deficiency in power, and the deficient cannot be a god, but it is permissible to be a servant. For we say: generality of power does not affect, for the attachment of power to other than the specific object of power has no effect on this specific [object]. Necessarily, when the attachment of their powers to a specific object of power is assumed, the two powers are equal in relation to it, so their effects on its two sides are equal. Thus, the effect of one of them preventing the effect of the other without the reverse is a preference without a preferrer. There is investigation because the attachment of two powers to a specific object of power does not necessitate their equality, for it is possible that one of the two powerful beings is more powerful over it than the other, while they share that specific object being within their power. For difference in levels of power in terms of intensity and weakness is permissible.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is a reductio proof that two omnipotent agents cannot both have power over the same act. The argument begins by assuming two agents with power over the same act. If both wills coincide, the act occurs; if they differ, two impossibilities follow: either both opposites occur (impossible) or one agent is rendered incapable (contradicting the assumption of shared power). An objection is raised that God's power is greater, so the act occurs by His power alone, nullifying the servant's effect. The response rejects this because the generality of God's power over other objects is irrelevant; regarding the specific act, their powers are equal. If one power prevented the other, it would be arbitrary preference. The passage concludes by noting a possible investigation: equality of power over a specific act is not necessary, as one agent may be stronger, but the proof assumes equality for the sake of argument.

What are the two impossibilities that follow from assuming two agents with power over the same act when their wills conflict?
A) Either both acts occur or both fail to occur
B) Either both acts occur or one agent is incapable
C) Either one act occurs or both agents are incapable
D) Either both acts fail or one agent is omnipotent

Key Takeaway
Two agents cannot both have power over the same act because any conflict of will leads to absurdity.

Objection and Response: Generality of Power and Equality
Objection and response regarding shared power over an act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that God's greater power resolves the conflict and the kalam response that equality is assumed for the specific act.

Core Idea
Generality of power does not affect specific shared power.

After the reductio, an objection is raised: God's power is more general and complete, so when conflict occurs, the act occurs by God's power alone, nullifying the servant's effect. The response argues that generality is irrelevant to the specific act; regarding that act, their powers are equal, so preventing one by the other would be arbitrary.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objector argues that God's power is greater and more general, so when conflict occurs, God's will prevails and the servant's failure is due to a stronger obstacle.
2
Understand the response
The response denies that generality of power matters. For the specific act in question, the powers are equal because both are assumed to have power over it. Thus, preventing one by the other would be arbitrary.
This is the key logical move.
3
Note the final investigation
The passage ends by acknowledging that equality is not necessary in reality; one agent may be stronger. But the proof assumes equality to derive a contradiction.
This shows the argument's structure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The objection assumed that because God is greater, His power should always win. The response says that's not how it works. Is there an area in your life where you assume God's greatness means He doesn't care about the details? Today, commit to believing that God deals justly with every specific act, not just generally.

Power is a Positive Attribute: Consensus and Dissent
States the consensus that power is a positive attribute, with Bishr al-Mu'tamir's dissenting view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the majority view that divine power is a positive attribute and the minority privative view of Bishr al-Mu'tamir.

Core Idea
Power is a real attribute, not merely the absence of defects.

This lesson presents the third objective concerning the nature of power (qudra). The Ash'aris, Mu'tazila, and others agree it is a positive attribute, but Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir dissents, defining it as mere soundness of constitution. Understanding this foundational classification sets the stage for deeper kalam discussions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the main points.
The passage is short but packs two contrasting views.
2
Identify the two positions
Notice the majority consensus (Ash'aris, Mu'tazila, others) that power is a positive attribute, and Bishr's dissenting view that it is merely 'soundness of constitution'.
The passage explicitly lists both.
3
Understand the privative definition
Bishr defines power as a privative attribute (sifa 'adamiyya) – the absence of defects, not a real entity. He challenges proponents of a positive attribute to bring proof.
This is a radical departure from the mainstream.
4
Reflect on al-Razi's adoption
Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi in al-Muhassal chose Bishr's position, showing the view had scholarly weight despite being a minority.
Al-Razi is a major Ash'ari theologian, so this is notable.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الثالث : القدرة صفة وجودية ]
اتفقت الأشاعرة والمعتزلة وغيرهم على أنَّ القدرة صفة وجودية يتأتى معها الفعل بدلاً عن الترك، والترك بدلاً عن الفعل ) وقال بشر بن المعتمر: القدرة الحادثة عبارة عن سلامة البنية عن الآفات ) فجعلها صفة عدمية قال : ( فمن أثبت صفة زائدة ) على سلامة البنية . ( فعليه البرهان) واختار الإمام الرازي في المحصل مذهبه
The third objective: Power is a positive attribute. The Ash'aris, Mu'tazila, and others agreed that power is a positive attribute with which the act is realized instead of omission, and omission instead of the act. And Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir said: The originated power is an expression of the soundness of the constitution from defects. So he made it a privative attribute. He said: So whoever affirms an additional attribute on top of soundness of constitution must provide proof. And Imam al-Razi in al-Muhassal chose his school.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage presents the third objective (al-maqsad al-thalith) concerning the nature of power (qudra). It begins by stating the consensus of the Ash'aris, Mu'tazila, and others that power is a positive attribute (sifa wujudiyya), meaning it is a real entity that enables the agent to perform the act instead of leaving it, and to leave it instead of performing it. Then it notes the dissenting view of Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir, the Mu'tazili theologian, who defined originated power (al-qudra al-haditha) as merely the soundness of the physical constitution (salamat al-baniya) from defects, thus making it a privative attribute (sifa 'adamiyya) rather than a positive one. Bishr reportedly said: 'Whoever affirms an additional attribute beyond soundness of constitution must provide proof.' The passage concludes by mentioning that Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi in his work al-Muhassal adopted Bishr's position, indicating a notable exception to the majority view.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All theologians agree that power is a positive attribute.
Correction: While the majority (Ash'aris, Mu'tazila, etc.) hold it is positive, Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir and al-Razi consider it a privative attribute (soundness of constitution).

Checkpoint

A) It is a privative attribute
B) It is a positive attribute
C) It is the soundness of the constitution
D) It is neither positive nor privative

Key Takeaway
The majority of theologians consider power a positive real attribute, but a minority defines it as merely the soundness of the constitution, making it a privative attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you habitually attribute your accomplishments to a positive power within you, or do you see them as the mere absence of obstacles? How does each perspective shape your gratitude to Allah—and which one is more true to your actual dependence on Him?
?
When you face failure, do you immediately blame a lack of strength or the presence of some defect? Bishr's view suggests power is just soundness from defects. How does that reframe your sense of responsibility for maintaining your physical and spiritual health?
?
If power is only the absence of impediments, then every moment you act is a moment you are free from weakness. That freedom is a trust. When you last sinned, was it because you lacked power or because you misused the soundness Allah gave you? What will you do with the soundness you have right now?

The Impossibility of a Single Act Being Within the Power of Two Agents
Al-Jurjani explains the Ash'ari position on shared power between two agents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'arites consider it impossible for two agents with the same type of power (both effective or both acquiring) to share a single act, and the exception for one effective and one acquiring power.

Core Idea
Two powers of the same kind cannot share one act.

In this passage, al-Jurjani explains the Ash'ari position on whether a single act can be within the power of two agents. He contrasts the Mu'tazila absolute denial with the Ash'ari nuanced view, showing that mutual impediment arises for two effective powers, and the locus problem prevents two acquiring powers from sharing an act. However, an effective (divine) power and an acquiring (human) power can coexist on the same act.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the literal English translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the parentheses in the Arabic – they are explanatory insertions.
2
Identify the three scenarios
The passage presents three combinations of powers on a single act: two effective powers, two acquiring powers, and one effective plus one acquiring. Find each one in the text.
The Mu'tazila only consider effective powers; Ash'arites distinguish types.
3
Understand the reasoning for impossibility
For two effective powers, the reasoning is mutual impediment (tamānuʿ). For two acquiring powers, the reasoning is that each acquiring power can only relate to an act within its own locus (maḥall). A single act cannot have two loci.
The exception is the mixed case, which is permitted as per earlier discussion.
4
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
Some may think Ash'arites allow any two powers to share an act, but the passage clarifies that only one effective and one acquiring can share, not two of the same kind.
This distinction is crucial for understanding the Ash'ari doctrine of kasb.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ومنعه المعتزلة) أي منعوا جواز كون مقدور بين قادرين مطلقاً (بناء على امتناع قدرة غير مؤثرة على رأيهم بل لا تكون القدرة عندهم إلا مؤثرة (فيلزم التمانع على تقدير كون مقدور بين قادرين ) والمجوزون من أصحابنا) لكون مقدور بين قدرة كاسبة وقدرة مؤثرة كما مر (اتفقوا على امتناع) مقدور بين قدرتين مؤثرتين للتمانع ( و ) على امتناع مقدور بين (قدرتين كاسبتين، لأن الكسب أن يخلق الله تعالى فعلاً متعلقاً للقدرة الحادثة، وأنها ) أي القدرة الحادثة (لا تتعلق بفعل خارج عن المحل أي محل تلك القدرة الحادثة ( فلا يقدر زيد على فعل عمرو، ولا يتصور اثنان هما محل لفعل واحد ) بل يكون كل واحد من الاثنين محلاً لفعل مغاير، ولو بالشخص لفعل الآخر، فلا يمكن اجتماع قدرتين كاسبتين على فعل واحد شخصي .
(And the Mu'tazila denied it) meaning they denied the permissibility of an act being within the power of two agents absolutely, (based on the impossibility of a power that is not effective according to their view; rather, power in their view is only effective. (Therefore, mutual impediment (tamānuʿ) would follow on the supposition of an act being within the power of two agents). And those who permit [it] from our companions (al-mujawwizūn min aṣḥābinā), due to an act being within the power of an acquiring power (qudra kāsiba) and an effective power (qudra mu'aththira) as passed earlier, (agreed on the impossibility) of an act being within two effective powers because of mutual impediment, (and) on the impossibility of an act being within (two acquiring powers), because acquisition (al-kasb) is that Allah creates an act related to the originated power (al-qudra al-ḥāditha), and that it (i.e., the originated power) is not related to an act outside of the locus (al-maḥall) i.e., the locus of that originated power. (Thus, Zayd cannot have power over the act of ʿAmr, and two [agents] who are a locus for one single act cannot be conceived). Rather, each of the two is a locus for a distinct act, even if it is individually [the same as] the act of the other. So the coming together of two acquiring powers over one individual act is impossible.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage discusses the Ash'ari position on whether a single act can be within the power of two agents. It begins by stating that the Mu'tazila absolutely deny the possibility of an act being within the power of two agents, because they hold that power must be effective (mu'aththira) and if two effective powers were on one act, mutual impediment (tamānuʿ) would result. The Ash'arites who permit shared power between an acquiring power (qudra kāsiba) and an effective power (qudra mu'aththira) agree on the impossibility of an act being within two effective powers (due to tamānuʿ) and also on the impossibility of an act being within two acquiring powers. The reason is that acquisition (kasb) means Allah creates an act related to the originated power (qudra ḥāditha), and this originated power cannot relate to an act outside its own locus (maḥall). Hence Zayd cannot have power over ʿAmr's act. Two agents cannot be a locus for a single act; each must be a locus for a distinct act, even if that act is individually the same as the other's. Therefore two acquiring powers cannot together produce one individual act.

القدرة الكاسبة (al-qudra al-kāsiba)
التمانع (al-tamānuʿ)
الكسب (al-kasb)
القدرة الحادثة (al-qudra al-ḥāditha)
المحل (al-maḥall)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that Ash'arites allow any two powers to share an act, but they only allow sharing between one creating power and one acquiring power, not between two of the same kind.
Correction: The passage clarifies that two effective or two acquiring powers cannot share an act; only one effective and one acquiring can coexist on the same act.

Checkpoint
Why do the Ash'arites consider it impossible for two acquiring powers (qudra kāsiba) to share a single act?
A) Because two effective powers would cause mutual impediment (tamānuʿ).
B) Because each acquiring power is only related to an act within its own locus, and a single act cannot have two loci.
C) Because God does not create acts for two acquiring powers simultaneously.
D) Because the Mu'tazila deny it.

Key Takeaway
Ash'arites hold that two powers of the same type (both effective or both acquiring) cannot share a single act, but one effective (divine) and one acquiring (human) can coexist on the same act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you accomplish something praiseworthy, do you truly believe that your power is merely an acquisition from Allah, or does your heart secretly attribute the success to your own ability? How can you train yourself to feel that dependence on Allah in the moment of achievement?
?
If every act you perform is created by Allah and you only acquire it, what does that mean for your sense of personal responsibility? Does it make you more grateful? More humble? What concrete change in your daily behavior would show that you live this belief?
?
The passage teaches that two acquiring powers cannot share the same act. In your relationships, do you sometimes compete with others as if your power and their power are both trying to control the same outcome? How would embracing the Ash'ari view on power change how you collaborate and trust in Allah's sole effective power?

Ash'ari Proof: Originated Power Exists with the Act
Ash'ari proof that originated power exists with the act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari proof that originated power exists only simultaneously with its act and why it cannot precede it.

Core Idea
Originated power cannot exist before the act—doing so leads to a logical contradiction.

This passage presents a concise reductio argument for a key Ash'ari doctrine: that originated power (qudra haditha) exists only at the moment of the act, not before. The argument shows that if power preceded the act, the act would have to be possible in that earlier time—and assuming it occurs there collapses the distinction between before and during, yielding an absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text and its English translation carefully. Notice the structure: a statement followed by a proof.
The proof uses a reductio ad absurdum, so pay attention to the conditional 'otherwise'.
2
Identify the reductio
The author assumes for contradiction that power exists before the act. Trace the logical steps from that assumption to the absurd conclusion.
The key move: if power existed before, the act must be possible in that prior time.
3
Reflect on the premise
The argument relies on the premise that power implies the possibility of its object. Consider why this premise is taken as self-evident in kalam.
This premise is crucial; without it, the reductio fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ) وأصحابه: (القدرة ) الحادثة (مع الفعل) أي أنها توجد حال حدوث الفعل، وتتعلق به في هذه الحالة ( ولا توجد ( القدرة الحادثة (قبله) فضلا عن تعلقها به (إذ قبل الفعل لا يمكن (الفعل) . بل يمتنع وجوده فيه ( وإلا ) أي وإن لم يمتنع وجوده قبله، بل أمكن فلنفرض وجوده فيه (فهي) أي فالحالة التي فرضناها أنها حالة سابقة على الفعل ليست كذلك، بل هي حال الفعل، هذا خلف) محال
The Shaykh (Ash'ari) and his companions said: Originated power is with the act, meaning it exists at the moment the act occurs and is related to it in that state, and originated power does not exist before the act, let alone its relation to it, since before the act the act is not possible. Rather, its existence before it is impossible. Otherwise—i.e., if its existence before it were not impossible but possible—then let us assume its existence in it. Then the state we assumed to be prior to the act is not so, but rather it is the state of the act; this is an absurd consequence.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents and proves the Ash'ari doctrine that originated power exists simultaneously with the act, not before it. The author first states the position: originated power exists only at the moment the act occurs and is related to it in that very moment. It does not exist before the act, so it cannot be related before. The proof is a reductio ad absurdum: If power existed before the act, then the act would be possible in that prior time (because power implies the possibility of its object). But if the act were possible before itself, we could assume it occurs in that prior time, which would mean the state we thought was prior to the act is actually the state of the act itself—a contradiction. Therefore, power cannot precede the act.

Key Terms
القدرة الحادثة
مع الفعل
تعلق القدرة
السابقة
الخلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that originated power can exist before the act but only relate at the moment of the act.
Correction: The proof shows that if power existed before, the act would have to be possible in that before-time, leading to contradiction. Therefore, power cannot exist before at all.

Checkpoint
What is the key premise in the Ash'ari proof that originated power cannot exist before the act?
A) Power cannot exist without a possible object.
B) The act must be possible in the time when power exists.
C) Assuming existence of the act in the prior time leads to contradiction.
D) All of the above.

Key Takeaway
Originated power must be simultaneous with its act; otherwise, a logical contradiction follows.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you plan to do a good deed later, do you assume you will have the power to do it at that future moment? This lesson proves that your power to act exists only at the very moment of the act. Are you willing to stop procrastinating and act now, knowing that the power you think you will have tomorrow may never come?
?
Have you ever told yourself that you have the ability to avoid a sin, yet you continued to commit it? This proof shows that power and act are inseparable; if you truly had the power to avoid it, would you not have avoided it? What does this reveal about the gap between your claimed capability and your actual choice?
?
The proof demonstrates that the existence of power makes the act immediately possible. In your life, when you say 'I can do it,' do you actually do it? How can you ensure that your self-perception of power aligns with real action, especially in matters of obedience to Allah?

The Effect Being the Act Itself and the Problem of Infinite Regress
Objection on effecting being the act itself or distinct, leading to impossibility or infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that if the effect of temporal power (iqa'a) is identical to the act itself, it leads to impossibility, and if it is distinct, it leads to infinite regress.

Core Idea
Effecting cannot be the act itself nor something else without causing infinite regress.

This lesson examines a key objection in kalam regarding the relationship between temporal power and its effect. The discussion centers on the concept of 'effecting' (iqa'a) — the power's influence in bringing about the act. The passage argues that whether effecting is identical to the act or distinct, problems arise: either impossibility or infinite regress.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Note the logical structure: an initial clarification, then two scenarios for the nature of effecting.
Pay attention to the terms 'iqa'a' and 'tasalsul' (infinite regress).
2
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two possibilities for what effecting (iqa'a) is: either it is identical to the act itself, or it is something else. For each, understand the consequence drawn.
The first scenario leads to impossibility because the act cannot occur before its time. The second leads to infinite regress because the effecting itself becomes an event requiring another effecting.
3
Reflect on the initial point
The passage begins by stating that the power's connection to the act does not require the act's possibility in the present state. This is important context for the objection.
This sets up that the power exists before the act, and the act's possibility only needs to be at the time of occurrence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجدت في حال كانت متعلقة بوجود الفعل في ذلك الحال حتى يلزم امكان وجوده فيه، بل نقول: (القدرة في الحال) إنما هي (على ايقاع الفعل في ثاني الحال) وهو أي تعلق القدرة السابقة بالفعل على هذا الوجه (لا يستدعي امكانه) أي امكان الفعل (في الحال بل في ثاني الحال) فلا يضرنا ما ذكرتم من أن الفعل ليس بممكن قبل حدوثه في جواز كون القدرة موجودة قبله. قلنا: (الايقاع) الذي هو تأثير القدرة الحادثة في الفعل وايجادها إياه على رأيكم إن كان نفس الفعل على معنى أن التأثير في الفعل هو عين حصول الأثر الذي هو الفعل فمحال أي فالايقاع محال في الحال لما ذكرنا من أن حصول الفعل مستحيل قبل زمان حدوثه. وإن كان غيره عاد الكلام فيه لأن الايقاع ممكن حادث فلا بد له من تأثير القدرة فيه فللايقاع ايقاع آخر ولزم التسلسل بأن يكون بين القدرة والفعل ايقاعات وتأثيرات غير متناهية. لا يقال: الايقاع أمر اعتباري فلا حاجة به إلى ايقاع آخر. لأنا نقول: اتصاف الموقع بصفة الايقاع دون اللا ايقاع يحتاج إلى ترجيح قطعاً، وهو المراد بالتأثير والايقاع. فإن قلت: هذا تسلسل في الأمور الاعتبارية وذا ليس بممتنع؟ قلت: أجيب بعد تسليم جوازه في الجملة في الاعتبايات التي لم تنشأ من الفرق.
You found it in a state where it was connected to the existence of the act in that state until its possibility is necessary. Rather we say: (the power in the state) is only (for effecting the act in the second state) meaning the connection of the prior power to the act in this manner (does not require its possibility) i.e. the possibility of the act (in the state but in the second state). So what you mentioned that the act is not possible before its occurrence does not harm us regarding the permissibility of the power existing before it. We said: (the effecting) which is the influence of the temporal power in the act and its bringing it into existence according to your view, if it is the act itself meaning that the influence in the act is the very occurrence of the effect which is the act, then it is impossible, i.e. the effecting is impossible in the state due to what we mentioned that the occurrence of the act is impossible before the time of its occurrence. And if it is something else, the discussion returns to it because the effecting is a possible temporal thing, so it must have an influence of power in it, so the effecting has another effecting, and infinite regress follows such that there are between power and act infinite effectings and influences. It cannot be said: the effecting is a relative (i'tibari) matter so it does not need another effecting. For we say: the attribution of the agent with the attribute of effecting as opposed to non-effecting absolutely needs a preponderator, and that is what is meant by influence and effecting. If you say: this is an infinite regress in relative matters and that is not impossible? I say: it is answered after conceding its permissibility in general in relative matters that do not arise from difference.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and reply concerning the relationship between temporal power and the act. It first explains that the power's connection to the act in the second state does not require the act's possibility in the present state. Then it considers the nature of 'effecting' (iqa'a), which is the influence of power. If effecting is identical to the act itself, then it is impossible because the act cannot occur before its time. If effecting is something else, then it is itself an event requiring another effecting, leading to an infinite regress. The objector tries to avoid this by claiming effecting is a relative (i'tibari) matter, not needing another effecting. The reply states that even relative matters require a preponderator to determine the attribution of effecting versus non-effecting. The final objection asks why infinite regress in relative matters is impossible, and the answer is that it is conceded only for relative matters that do not arise from a distinction (i.e., are non-substantive).

Key Terms
الايقاع
التسلسل
القدرة الحادثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the effecting (iqa'a) being the act itself lead to impossibility?
A) Because the act would need to occur in two times simultaneously
B) Because the act cannot occur before its time, and effecting would require the act to exist before its time
C) Because effecting is a relative matter and thus cannot be identical to the act
D) Because it would entail an infinite regress

Key Takeaway
Effecting (iqa'a) cannot be the act itself because that would require the act to exist before its time, nor can it be something else without leading to an infinite chain of effects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often assume that your efforts directly produce results. How does the dilemma of effecting (iqa'a) challenge the illusion that you control outcomes? Does this shift your reliance from your own power to the Creator's?
?
When you pray for something, do you expect an immediate answer? How does the lesson's point that power is for effecting in the second state (not the present) affect your patience and trust in God's timing?
?
If infinite regress in causal chains is impossible, what does that imply about your own actions being caused by previous events? Are you merely a link in a chain, or is there a direct connection to the Divine? How does this change how you perceive responsibility for your deeds?

The Nature of I'tibari Matters and Infinite Regress in Kalam
Objection and reply on effecting being i'tibari, and regress in i'tibari matters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that effecting is a relative (i'tibari) matter and why even relative matters require a preponderator, and why infinite regress in relative matters is not automatically accepted.

Core Idea
Even relative (i'tibari) matters need a preponderator to explain their occurrence; not all infinite regresses are impossible.

This lesson continues the discussion on effecting (iqa'a). A counter-argument is raised: perhaps effecting is merely a relative (i'tibari) attribute, so it does not need its own cause and thus avoids infinite regress. The reply insists that even relative attributes require a preponderator to determine their occurrence. The final exchange questions whether infinite regress in relative matters is impossible, with a nuanced answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and reply
Read the Arabic text and translation. Notice the objection: 'al-iqa'a amr i'tibari' (effecting is a relative matter) so no regress. Then the reply: even i'tibari matters need a preponderator (tarjih).
Key term: 'tarjih' (preponderator) is what causes one state over another.
2
Understand the concept of i'tibari
I'tibari matters are mental considerations or relations, not concrete entities. The objection argues they don't need causes, but the reply counters that attributing the quality of 'effecting' rather than 'not effecting' requires a cause.
The reply says the very attribution of the agent as 'effecting' versus 'not effecting' needs a preponderator.
3
Analyze the final objection and answer
The objector asks: why is infinite regress in i'tibari matters impossible? The answer concedes that it is permissible in general for i'tibari matters, but only for those that do not arise from a real distinction (lam tunsha' min al-farq).
This nuance: infinite regress is not automatically impossible; it depends on the nature of the i'tibari matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجدت في حال كانت متعلقة بوجود الفعل في ذلك الحال حتى يلزم امكان وجوده فيه، بل نقول: (القدرة في الحال) إنما هي (على ايقاع الفعل في ثاني الحال) وهو أي تعلق القدرة السابقة بالفعل على هذا الوجه (لا يستدعي امكانه) أي امكان الفعل (في الحال بل في ثاني الحال) فلا يضرنا ما ذكرتم من أن الفعل ليس بممكن قبل حدوثه في جواز كون القدرة موجودة قبله. قلنا: (الايقاع) الذي هو تأثير القدرة الحادثة في الفعل وايجادها إياه على رأيكم إن كان نفس الفعل على معنى أن التأثير في الفعل هو عين حصول الأثر الذي هو الفعل فمحال أي فالايقاع محال في الحال لما ذكرنا من أن حصول الفعل مستحيل قبل زمان حدوثه. وإن كان غيره عاد الكلام فيه لأن الايقاع ممكن حادث فلا بد له من تأثير القدرة فيه فللايقاع ايقاع آخر ولزم التسلسل بأن يكون بين القدرة والفعل ايقاعات وتأثيرات غير متناهية. لا يقال: الايقاع أمر اعتباري فلا حاجة به إلى ايقاع آخر. لأنا نقول: اتصاف الموقع بصفة الايقاع دون اللا ايقاع يحتاج إلى ترجيح قطعاً، وهو المراد بالتأثير والايقاع. فإن قلت: هذا تسلسل في الأمور الاعتبارية وذا ليس بممتنع؟ قلت: أجيب بعد تسليم جوازه في الجملة في الاعتبايات التي لم تنشأ من الفرق.
You found it in a state where it was connected to the existence of the act in that state until its possibility is necessary. Rather we say: (the power in the state) is only (for effecting the act in the second state) meaning the connection of the prior power to the act in this manner (does not require its possibility) i.e. the possibility of the act (in the state but in the second state). So what you mentioned that the act is not possible before its occurrence does not harm us regarding the permissibility of the power existing before it. We said: (the effecting) which is the influence of the temporal power in the act and its bringing it into existence according to your view, if it is the act itself meaning that the influence in the act is the very occurrence of the effect which is the act, then it is impossible, i.e. the effecting is impossible in the state due to what we mentioned that the occurrence of the act is impossible before the time of its occurrence. And if it is something else, the discussion returns to it because the effecting is a possible temporal thing, so it must have an influence of power in it, so the effecting has another effecting, and infinite regress follows such that there are between power and act infinite effectings and influences. It cannot be said: the effecting is a relative (i'tibari) matter so it does not need another effecting. For we say: the attribution of the agent with the attribute of effecting as opposed to non-effecting absolutely needs a preponderator, and that is what is meant by influence and effecting. If you say: this is an infinite regress in relative matters and that is not impossible? I say: it is answered after conceding its permissibility in general in relative matters that do not arise from difference.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and reply concerning the relationship between temporal power and the act. It first explains that the power's connection to the act in the second state does not require the act's possibility in the present state. Then it considers the nature of 'effecting' (iqa'a), which is the influence of power. If effecting is identical to the act itself, then it is impossible because the act cannot occur before its time. If effecting is something else, then it is itself an event requiring another effecting, leading to an infinite regress. The objector tries to avoid this by claiming effecting is a relative (i'tibari) matter, not needing another effecting. The reply states that even relative matters require a preponderator to determine the attribution of effecting versus non-effecting. The final objection asks why infinite regress in relative matters is impossible, and the answer is that it is conceded only for relative matters that do not arise from a distinction (i.e., are non-substantive).

الأمر الاعتباري
الترجيح
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if something is considered 'i'tibari' (relative/mental), it requires no causal explanation at all.
Correction: The passage shows that even i'tibari attributes like 'effecting' require a preponderator to account for their existence, though infinite regress in purely mental matters may be permissible.

Checkpoint
According to the reply in the passage, why does the effecting (iqa'a) being an i'tibari matter not avoid the need for another effecting?
A) Because i'tibari matters are actually real entities
B) Because the attribution of 'effecting' versus 'not effecting' still requires a preponderator
C) Because infinite regress is impossible in i'tibari matters
D) Because the objection is irrelevant

Key Takeaway
Even relative (i'tibari) attributes like effecting require a cause for their attribution, and infinite regress in them is not always allowed—only when they are purely mental without foundation in reality.

The Third Proof: Concurrency and the Objection
Begins the third proof with commentary and an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third Mu'tazilite proof against concurrency, why it requires refinement, and the objection that Mu'tazilites do not affirm eternal divine power.

Core Idea
A proof against concurrency must account for the Mu'tazilite denial of eternal power.

In this passage, the commentator examines the third Mu'tazilite argument that if power were concurrent with the act, it would imply God's power is temporal. However, the argument as stated is problematic because it relies on a similarity between created and divine power, yet the Mu'tazilites do not affirm an eternal divine power. The passage presents the proof, a necessary refinement, and the objection that Mu'tazilites cannot use this reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice the structure: a proof claim, then commentary, then an objection.
The passage is incomplete; the response to the objection is missing.
2
Understand the third proof's core claim
The proof states that if power is with the act (concurrent), it would necessitate the temporality of God's power. Identify the reasoning behind this claim.
3
Analyze the commentator's refinement
The commentator notes two cases: if power is qualified as 'created', concurrency does not lead to the problem; if unqualified, it is not the dispute. The proof must be reformulated: if created power is concurrent, then by similarity eternal power would also be concurrent, which is impossible.
This refinement shifts the argument to rely on the similarity (tamathul) between created and divine power.
4
Consider the objection
The objection states: Mu'tazilites do not affirm an eternal power, so how can they use this argument? Reflect on why this is a challenge and how the proof might need to adapt.
The passage ends here; the response is not included.

Source Text

الوجه ( الثالث : أنه ( أي كون القدرة مع الفعل لا قبله ( يوجب حدوث قدرة الله تعالى قوله : أي) كون القدرة مع الفعل إلخ) لا يخفى أنه إن قيد القدرة بالحادثة فكونها مع الفعل لا يوجب أحد الأمرين. وإن لم يقيد فهو ليس بمحل النزاع، فلا بد في تحرير هذه الوجه من تصرف كان يقال : لو كانت القدرة الحادثة مع الفعل لكانت القديمة كذلك لتماثلهما، والتالي باطل لأنه يستلزم أحد الأمرين المحالين فكذا ،القدم ولا شك أنها ليست بعده فتكون قبله، وهذا تجويز قول الشارح ولو كان ذلك . قوله : (الوجه الثالث) فإن قيل المعتزلة لا يقولون بالقدرة القديمة فكيف يستدلون بهذا
The third aspect: that (i.e., that power is with the act, not before it) would necessitate the temporality of God's power. [Commentary:] His saying: i.e., that power is with the act, etc. It is not hidden that if power is qualified as created, then its being with the act does not necessitate either of the two things. But if it is not qualified, then it is not the subject of dispute. So in formulating this aspect there must be a modification, such as saying: if the created power were with the act, then the eternal power would be likewise because they are similar, and the consequent is false because it necessitates one of two impossible things, so also (the impossibility of) eternity? And there is no doubt that it is not after it, so it is before it. And this is what the commentator (al-sharih) allows. His saying (the third aspect): For if it is said: the Mu'tazilites do not affirm eternal power, so how can they argue with this?
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage begins the third Mu'tazilite proof and includes part of the commentator's (shariḥ's) analysis. The proof appears to be: if created power were concurrent with the act, then by analogy God's eternal power would also be concurrent, which is impossible because it would lead to one of two impossible outcomes (likely that God's power would be temporal or that it would be related to the act in a way that implies temporality). The commentator notes that the proof must be refined: if 'power' is qualified as 'created', then concurrency does not entail the problematic conclusion; if not qualified, it is not the subject of dispute. So the proof should be rephrased to say: if created power were with the act, then by similarity (tamathul) the eternal power would also be with the act, which is impossible. The text then breaks off with an objection that the Mu'tazilites do not believe in an eternal power, so how can they use this argument? The passage ends abruptly, leaving the response unclear.

Eternal Power Precedes the Act: Proof and Rebuttal
Proves that eternal power must precede the act and rebuts the objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why eternal power must be related to its object before the act occurs, and how the author refutes the objection that the act is impossible in pre-eternity.

Core Idea
Eternal power must precede its act; denying its relation leads to absurdity.

In this passage, the author presents a rigorous proof that Allah's eternal power is related to its objects before they come into existence. He then addresses a key objection from theologians who claim that power cannot relate to an impossible thing. The author's analysis reveals that the objection inadvertently supports the very position it seeks to refute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic once, then the literal translation. Notice the logical flow: assumption, two false conclusions, then the correct position.
2
Follow the proof structure
The proof assumes power and object are together. This leads to two impossible consequences: either the object's temporality implies power's temporality, or power's pre-eternity implies object's pre-eternity. Both are false, so the assumption is false.
The conclusion: power is pre-eternal and related before the act.
3
Examine the objection
The objection states: the act in pre-eternity is impossible, so eternal power cannot be related to it. Understand the underlying assumption.
Why does the objector think impossibility precludes relation?
4
Understand the author's critique
The author argues that this answer concedes the opponent's claim that power exists before the act, and merely explains the delay, thus supporting rather than refuting.
The answer is self-defeating.
5
Engage with the dialogue
Read the 'if you say... I say' part. The author denies that total negation of relation is possible, insisting on a mental relation.
This establishes power's precedence with a relation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك أو قدم مقدوره إذ الفرض كون القدرة والمقدور معاً، فيلزم من حدوث مقدوره تعالى حدوث قدرته أو من قدم قدرته قدم مقدوره وكلاهما باطل بل قدرته أزلية اجماعا ومتعلقة في الأزل بمقدوراته، فقد ثبت تعلق القدرة بمقدورها قبل حدوثه ولو كان ذلك ممتنعاً في القدرة الحادثة لكان ممتنعاً في القديمة أيضاً (أجيب) عن ( بأن الفعل في الأزل غير ممكن، فلا تتعلق به القدرة القديمة قال المصنف : ( وفي) أي في هذا الجواب الذي ذكره الآمدي ( نظر إذ فيه التزام) لمذهب الخصم، أعني وجود القدرة قبل الفعل (وما ذكروه ) في الجواب (بيان للسبب الذي به كان المقدور متأخراً عن القدرة فهو تأييد لمذهبه لا دفع له فإن قلت : إن المعتزلة ادعوا وجود القدرة قبل الفعل مع تعلقها به والمجيب سلم وجودها ومنع تعلقها، فلا يكون التزاماً لمقالتهم . قلت : وجود القدرة مع انتفاء التعلق بالكلية مما تأباه البديهة، فلا بد أن يقال : هناك تعلق معنوي غير كاف في وجود المقدور وبذلك تثبت القدرة قبل الفعل مع تعلقها به في الجملة
That or the pre-eternity of its object of power, for the assumption is that power and the object of power are together. So from the temporal origination of His object of power, the temporal origination of His power would follow, or from the pre-eternity of His power, the pre-eternity of His object of power would follow. Both are false. Rather, His power is pre-eternal by consensus and is related in pre-eternity to its objects of power. So it is established that power is related to its object of power before its temporal origination. And if that were impossible for the originated power, it would also be impossible for the eternal (power). It was answered that the act in pre-eternity is not possible, so the eternal power is not related to it. The author said: 'This answer which al-Amidi mentioned is questionable because it involves an admission of the opponent's doctrine, namely the existence of power before the act. What they mentioned in the answer is an explanation of the reason for which the object of power is postponed from power, so it supports his doctrine, not a refutation of it.' If you say: The Mu'tazila claimed the existence of power before the act with its relation to it, and the answerer conceded its existence but denied its relation, so that is not an admission of their statement. I say: The existence of power with total negation of relation is rejected by intuition, so it must be said that there is a mental relation insufficient for the existence of the object of power. By that, power is established before the act with its relation to it in general.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage presents a proof and an objection-and-answer. It begins by assuming that if power and its object are together, then either the object's temporality implies power's temporality or power's pre-eternity implies object's pre-eternity, both false. Therefore, power must be pre-eternal and related to its objects before they exist. The objection states that the act is impossible in pre-eternity, so the eternal power cannot be related to it. The author critiques this answer, arguing that it concedes the opponent's claim that power exists before the act, and merely explains why the act is delayed, thus supporting the opponent. Then a hypothetical dialogue: if the opponent says the answerer conceded power's existence but denied its relation, the author replies that existence without any relation is intuitive impossible, so a mental relation must be admitted, which proves power precedes the act with some relation.

Correction: The author shows that this objection actually concedes power's existence before the act, and the answer only explains the delay, thus not refuting the claim.

Key Takeaway
The eternal power of Allah is related to its objects before they exist, and objections that it cannot relate to an impossible act fail because they inadvertently admit power's existence prior to the act.

Eternal vs. Originated Power: Resolving the Relation Problem
Refutes objection by distinguishing eternal and originated power

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the distinction between eternal and originated power, along with the concept of a mental relation, resolves the objection that divine power cannot relate to temporal acts without implying precedence.

Core Idea
Eternal power differs in essence from created power, solving the puzzle of divine causation.

This lesson presents a key objection against the Ash'ari position on divine power and its relation to acts. The objector argues that if an eternal relation is impossible, a temporal relation still poses a problem. Sanusi's correct answer distinguishes the nature of eternal power from originated power, and introduces the concept of a 'mental relation' (ta'alluq ma'nawi) that does not entail temporal precedence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the first part of the passage where the objection is presented: if eternal relation is impossible, a finite time before the act is possible, which still implies precedent power.
The objection tries to force a contradiction between power's precedence and the act's temporality.
2
Identify the first part of the answer
The answer begins by stating that eternal power differs in essence from originated power. Note the implication: the rules for one do not transfer to the other.
This is a crucial premise: the nature of power determines what is possible for it.
3
Grasp the two relations of eternal power
The passage explains that eternal power has a mental (pre-eternal) relation and an originated (temporal) relation that causes existence. The mental relation does not produce the act.
The mental relation is a conceptual link, not a temporal one.
4
See how the objection is fully repelled
The conclusion: the pre-eternity of power with its mental relation does not entail pre-eternity of effects. The problem is resolved.
The objection fails because it assumes a single type of relation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا ) إن امتنع تعلق القدرة بالفعل في الأزل لامتناع كون الفعل أزلياً ( فالتعلق) أي تعلقها بالفعل (قبله بزمان ) متناه لا يمتنع فيرد الاشكال بحسبه أي بحسب هذا التعلق، إذ حينئذ تكون القدرة موجودة قبل الفعل ومتعلقة به أيضاً قبله بزمان محدود كان الفعل فيه ممكناً، فالصواب في الجواب أن يقال القدرة القديمة الباقية مخالفة في الماهية للقدرة الحادثة التي لا يجوز بقاؤها عندنا . فلا يلزم من جواز تقدمها على الفعل جواز تقدم الحادثة عليه، ثم إن القدرة القديمة متعلقة في الأزل بالفعل تعلقاً معنوياً لا يترتب عليه وجود الفعل ولها تعلق آخر به حال حدوثه تعلقاً حادثاً موجباً، لوجوده فلا يلزم من قدمها مع تعلقها المعنوي قدم آثارها فاندفع الاشكال بحذافيره
And also, if the relation of power to the act in pre-eternity is impossible because the act cannot be pre-eternal, then the relation—i.e., its relation to the act—before it by a finite time is not impossible, so the problem would apply to this relation, because then power would exist before the act and also related to it before it by a limited time during which the act was possible. So the correct answer is to say: The eternal everlasting power differs in essence from the originated power, which cannot endure according to us. So it does not follow from the permissibility of its precedence over the act the permissibility of the originated (power) preceding it. Moreover, the eternal power is related in pre-eternity to the act with a mental relation from which the existence of the act does not follow, and it has another relation to it at the moment of its occurrence, an originated relation that causes its existence. So it does not follow from its pre-eternity along with its mental relation the pre-eternity of its effects. Thus the problem is completely repelled.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection and the correct answer. The objection says: if the relation is impossible in pre-eternity, still a relation a finite time before the act is possible, and that would still entail power existing before the act. The correct answer distinguishes the two powers. First, the eternal power differs in essence from the originated one; the latter cannot persist. Thus permission of precedence for the eternal does not imply permission for the originated. Second, the eternal power has two relations: a pre-eternal mental relation (which does not produce the act) and an originated relation at the act's occurrence that causes it. Therefore, the pre-eternity of power does not entail pre-eternity of the act, and the problem is fully resolved.

Key Terms
قدرة قديمة
قدرة حادثة
مخالفة في الماهية
تعلق معنوي
تعلق حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if power precedes the act, it must always be a temporal precedence.
Correction: The passage explains that eternal power has a mental (non-temporal) relation in pre-eternity that does not cause existence, so precedence can be merely conceptual.

Checkpoint
According to the correct answer, why does the eternal power's precedence not imply the originated power's precedence?
A) The eternal power is stronger.
B) The eternal power differs in essence from the originated.
C) The originated power does not exist.
D) The act is impossible before its time.

Key Takeaway
Eternal power, being essentially different, has a non-causal mental relation in pre-eternity, so its pre-eternity does not require the act to be eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Power Concurrent with Act: Objection to Obligation
Fourth objection: power simultaneous with act voids obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the view that power is simultaneous with the act leads to the impossibility of religious obligation (taklif).

Core Idea
If power is only at the act, obligation before it is impossible.

This passage presents a logical objection against the Ash‘ari position that power (qudra) is simultaneous with the act. If power does not precede the act, then at the moment of command the person has no power, making obligation meaningless. Moreover, disobedience becomes logically impossible. This argument is used to show that power must precede the act if obligation is to be coherent.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection statement
Begin by reading the Arabic text and its translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if power is with the act, then...'.
The objection is a reductio ad absurdum.
2
Identify the two consequences
The argument draws two consequences: (1) the disbeliever is not obligated to faith, and (2) disobedience from anyone is inconceivable. Locate these in the passage.
The first consequence is explicit; the second is derived from the reasoning.
3
Understand the logic behind each
For the first: at the time of command, the disbeliever has no power over faith (since power is only at the act). For the second: if the act is done, there is no disobedience; if not done, there is no power to do it – so disobedience cannot occur.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that obligation becomes impossible. Reflect on how this argument supports the view that power must precede the act.
This is a classic kalam argument used in debates on qadar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه (الرابع:) إن كانت القدرة على الفعل معه لا قبله ( يلزم أن لا يكون الكافر) في زمان كفره ( مكلفاً بالإيمان لأنه غير مقدور له في تلك الحالة المتقدمة عليه، بل نقول يلزم أن لا يتصور عصيان من أحد إذ مع الفعل لا عصيان وبدونه لا قدرة فلا تكليف فلا
The fourth aspect: If power over the act is with it, not before it, then it would follow that the disbeliever, at the time of his disbelief, is not obligated to faith, because it is not within his power in that state preceding it. Rather we say: it would follow that disobedience from anyone is inconceivable, for with the act there is no disobedience, and without it there is no power, so there is no obligation, so...
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth logical objection against the view that power is simultaneous with the act. If power only exists at the moment of the act, then before the act there is no power. Therefore, the disbeliever cannot be commanded to faith because at the time of command (before the act) he lacks power. Moreover, disobedience becomes impossible: if the act is done, there is no disobedience; if it is not done, there is no power. So obligation is void. This is a classic kalam argument used to show that power must precede the act to ground obligation.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that obligation only requires ability at the time of the act, so power could be simultaneous.
Correction: This argument shows that the command precedes the act, so ability must be present at the time of command; otherwise the command is meaningless.

Checkpoint
What does this argument conclude if power is with the act?
A) The disbeliever cannot be obligated to faith.
B) The disbeliever has power at the time of command.
C) Obligation is based on future power.
D) Power and act are simultaneous.

Key Takeaway
If power is only at the act, then command obligations become logically impossible, proving that power must precede the act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
