?
If you truly held the belief that God gives you power only at the exact moment of action, would you still feel responsible for your future sins? How does this argument challenge any complacency you might have about sins you have not yet committed?
?
Consider one command of Allah that you find difficult to obey. When you think about that command, do you assume you will have the power when the time comes, or do you feel you lack the power now? This argument says that power must precede obligation—so where does that leave you? Are you willing to ask Allah for that power now, not later?

The Problem of Temporal Power and the Essential Distinction
The author states the problem of temporal power preceding the act and gives the essential distinction as the first part of the answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the problem of temporal power preceding the act arises and how the Ash'ari school resolves it by distinguishing the essence of eternal power from temporal power.

Core Idea
Eternal power and temporal power differ in essence, so their precedence rules differ.

This lesson introduces a key problem in kalam: if temporal power exists before the act, what prevents it from causing the act earlier? The author first presents the difficulty and then offers the foundational solution: eternal and temporal power are essentially different, so the permissibility of one does not transfer to the other.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
Read the first part of the passage which defines temporal power and its connection to the act. Notice the objection raised.
2
Understand the essential distinction
The author states that eternal power differs in essence from temporal power. Reflect on why this distinction resolves the initial problem.
Temporal power cannot remain, so it cannot precede the act indefinitely.
3
Apply the principle
Consider how this reasoning prevents the conclusion that temporal power could also precede the act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الرابع - المقصد الخامس : القدرة الحادثة وتعلقها بالفعل. تثبت القدرة قبل الفعل مع تعلقها به في الجملة (وأيضا) إن امتنع تعلق القدرة بالفعل في الأزل لامتناع كون الفعل أزلياً (فالتعلق) أي تعلقها بالفعل (قبله بزمان) متناه لا يمتنع فيرد الاشكال بحسبه أي بحسب هذا التعلق، إذ حينئذ تكون القدرة موجودة قبل الفعل ومتعلقة به أيضاً قبله بزمان محدود كان الفعل فيه ممكناً، فالصواب في الجواب أن يقال القدرة القديمة الباقية مخالفة في الماهية للقدرة الحادثة التي لا يجوز بقاؤها عندنا. فلا يلزم من جواز تقدمها على الفعل جواز تقدم الحادثة عليه، ثم إن القدرة القديمة متعلقة في الأزل بالفعل تعلقاً معنوياً لا يترتب عليه وجود الفعل ولها تعلق آخر به حال حدوثه تعلقاً حادثاً موجباً، لوجوده فلا يلزم من قدمها مع تعلقها المعنوي قدم آثارها فاندفع الاشكال بحذافيره.
The fourth type - fifth objective: The temporal power and its connection to the act. Power is established before the act with its connection to it in general. (And also) if the connection of power to the act in pre-eternity is impossible because the act cannot be pre-eternal, (the connection) i.e., its connection to the act (before it by a time) finite is not impossible; so the problem arises with respect to it, i.e., with respect to this connection, because then power exists before the act and is also connected to it before it by a limited time in which the act was possible. The correct answer is to say: The eternal, remaining power is different in essence from the temporal power which, according to us, cannot remain. So the permissibility of its precedence over the act does not imply the permissibility of the temporal (power) preceding it. Then the eternal power is connected in pre-eternity to the act with a conceptual connection (ta'alluq ma'nawī) that does not entail the existence of the act, and it has another connection to it at the time of its occurrence, a temporal connection (ta'alluq ḥādith) that necessitates its existence. So its eternalness along with its conceptual connection does not entail the eternalness of its effects. Thus the problem is repelled entirely.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage defines the problem of the relation of temporal power to the act and provides the correct answer according to the Ash'ari school. It begins by stating that temporal power is generally established before the act, but a problem arises when considering a finite time before the act. The correct answer is that the eternal divine power (al-qudra al-qadīma al-bāqiya) is different in essence (mukhālifa fī al-māhiyya) from temporal power (al-qudra al-ḥāditha), which cannot persist. Therefore, the fact that eternal power can precede the act does not imply that temporal power can also precede it. The author then explains that eternal power has two types of connection to the act: a conceptual connection (ta'alluq ma'nawī) in pre-eternity that does not cause the act's existence, and a temporal connection (ta'alluq ḥādith) at the time of the act that necessitates its existence. Thus, the eternal power's pre-existence does not entail the pre-existence of its effects, resolving the problem entirely.

Key Terms
القدرة الحادثة
القدرة القديمة
الماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if eternal power can precede the act, then temporal power can also precede it.
Correction: The author clarifies that the two types of power differ in essence, so the rule for one does not apply to the other.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does the permissibility of eternal power's precedence not imply the permissibility of temporal power's precedence?
A) Because temporal power does not exist.
B) Because they differ in essence.
C) Because eternal power is not truly power.
D) Because the act is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The essential difference between eternal and temporal power prevents transferring precedence from one to the other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that God's power is fundamentally different in essence from any power you experience. How does this correct any assumptions you have made about how God 'must' operate—especially when you have doubted His ability to fulfill His promises?

?
If God's power is eternal and remains, while your power fades, what does that demand of your reliance on Him versus your own abilities? Be specific: what area of your life do you need to surrender because your power cannot sustain it?

The Two Connections of Eternal Power and the Full Resolution
The author explains the two connections of eternal power and concludes that the problem is fully resolved.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the two connections (conceptual and temporal) of eternal power explain why its effects are not eternal.

Core Idea
Eternal power has a conceptual connection that does not necessitate effects, and a temporal connection that does.

Building on the essential distinction, this lesson examines how eternal power relates to the act. The author introduces two types of connection: a conceptual one in pre-eternity that does not cause the act, and a temporal one at the moment of creation that necessitates the act. This explains why the act does not exist eternally despite the power being eternal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two connections
Read the passage and locate the two types of connection (ta'alluq) described. Note their differences.
2
Understand the conceptual connection
The conceptual connection (ta'alluq ma'nawī) obtains in pre-eternity but does not cause existence. Reflect on why.
It is like a disposition or capacity, not an efficient cause.
3
Understand the temporal connection
The temporal connection (ta'alluq ḥādith) occurs at the moment of the act and necessitates its existence.
4
See the resolution
Combine the two connections to see why the eternalness of power does not make the act eternal.

Commanding the Impossible: The Ash'ari Defense
Refutes the claim that commanding the impossible is absurd and distinguishes two cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari response to the objection that commanding the impossible is absurd, and the key distinction that a command is valid if the opposite act is within one's power.

Core Idea
Commands are valid if the opposite act is within one's power.

This passage addresses a central objection from the Mu'tazilites against the Ash'ari view that commanding the impossible is permissible. The Mu'tazilites argue that if something is impossible, it cannot be commanded. The Ash'aris respond by pointing out that the command to believe is valid because the opposite—abandoning disbelief—is within the person's power, even if the act of believing itself is not. This nuanced distinction resolves the contradiction and clarifies the nature of divine commands.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Mu'tazili objection
Read the beginning of the passage where the Mu'tazili argument is presented: that the strongest excuse is the inability to perform the commanded act, and if one cannot do it, blame must be removed.
This objection would make the command to believe absurd for a disbeliever who cannot currently believe.
2
See the Ash'ari counter
The Ash'ari response uses a reductio: if the objection held, then commanding the disbeliever to believe would be invalid, but the community agrees it is valid. Therefore, the objection must be false.
The consensus of the community (ijma') is invoked as evidence.
3
Grasp the distinction
Focus on the distinction drawn: the abandonment of faith (i.e., not remaining in disbelief) is in the disbeliever's power, even if the attainment of faith is not. The command is enforced because the opposite act is possible.
The command is to abandon disbelief, which is possible, not to acquire faith directly.
4
Apply the general condition
The passage states the condition for a valid command: either the commanded act itself or its opposite must be within one's power. This is satisfied for faith (the opposite is possible) but not for creating substances and accidents (neither act nor opposite is possible).
This distinguishes between two types of impossible commands.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الرابع - المقصد الخامس : القدرة الحادثة وتعلقها بالفعل وأيضاً أقوى أعذار المكلف التي يجب قبولها لدفع المؤاخذة عنه هو كون ما كلف به غير مقدور له فإذا لم يكن قادراً على الفعل قبله وجب دفع المؤاخذة عنه بعدم الفعل المكلف به، وهو باطل بإجماع الأمة ولو جوز تكليف الكافر بالإيمان مع كونه غير مقدور له فليجز تكليفه بخلق الجواهر والأعراض مما ليس مقدوراً له إذ لا مانع من التكليف بهذا الخلق سوى كونه غير مقدور، وقد فرضنا أنه لا يصلح مانعاً قلنا: يجوز تكليف المحال عندنا فيلزم جواز التكليف بالخلق المذكور ولنا الفرق وهو أن ترك الإيمان من الكافر حال كفره إنما هو بقدرته وإن لم يكن وجوده مقدوراً له حينئذ بخلاف عدم الجواهر والأعراض فإنه ليس مقدوراً له أصلاً فلا يلزم من جواز التكليف بالإيمان جواز التكليف بخلقها وبالجملة فكون الشيء مقدوراً الذي هو شرط التكليف عندنا أن يكون هو أي ذلك الشيء متعلقاً للقدرة أو يكون ضده متعلقاً لها، وهذا الشرط حاصل في الإيمان فإنه وإن لم يكن مقدوراً له قبل حدوثه لكن تركه بالتلبس بضده الذي هو الكفر مقدور له حال كونه كافراً بخلاف أحداث الجواهر والأعراض فإنه غير مقدور له فعله ولا تركه فلا يجوز التكليف به وأما ما ذكروه من قصة الأعذار ووجوب قبولها فمبني على قاعدة التحسين والتقبيح العقليين وسيأتي بطلانها.
The fourth type - the fifth purpose: The originated power and its relation to the act. Also, the strongest excuse of the obligated person that must be accepted to remove blame from him is that what he is commanded to do is not in his power. So if he is not able to do the act before it, blame must be removed from him for not doing the commanded act, and that is false by the consensus of the community. And if it is permitted to command the disbeliever with faith while it is not in his power, then let it be permitted to command him with the creation of substances and accidents, which are not in his power, for there is no impediment to commanding this creation except its being not in his power, and we have assumed that it does not qualify as an impediment. We said: commanding the impossible is allowed according to us, so the permissibility of commanding the mentioned creation follows. And we have the distinction: that the abandonment of faith from the disbeliever during his disbelief is by his power, even if its existence is not in his power at that time, unlike the non-existence of substances and accidents, for it is not in his power at all. So the permissibility of commanding faith does not necessitate the permissibility of commanding their creation. In summary, that a thing be in one's power, which is a condition of command according to us, is that that thing be related to power or that its opposite be related to it. And this condition is present in faith, for even if it is not in his power before its occurrence, its abandonment by adhering to its opposite, which is disbelief, is in his power while he is a disbeliever, unlike the production of substances and accidents, for their doing and their abandonment are not in his power at all, so commanding it is not permissible. As for what they mentioned about the story of excuses and the obligation of accepting them, it is based on the principle of rational good and evil, and its falsehood will come later.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection against the Ash'ari position on the possibility of commanding the impossible. The objection states that if commanding the impossible were allowed, then the command to the disbeliever to believe would be valid even though belief is not in his power at the moment, which is absurd. The response uses a distinction: although the act of believing is not in his power, the opposite act (disbelief) is in his power, and since the command is to abandon disbelief, it is a command of something possible. The passage then generalizes that the condition for a valid command is that either the commanded act or its opposite be possible. Here, the opposite (abandoning disbelief) is possible, so the command is valid. The passage also dismisses the Mu'tazili argument based on excuses, noting that it relies on the principle of rational good and evil, which will be refuted later.

Key Terms
تخليف
تكليف المحال
قدرة
ترك الإيمان
خلق الجواهر والأعراض
التحسين والتقبيح العقليين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'aris believe that commanding absolutely impossible things (like creating substances) is permissible.
Correction: Ash'aris only allow commanding the impossible when the opposite act is within the person's power. For creating substances and accidents, neither the act nor its opposite is possible, so such a command is not permissible.

Checkpoint
What is the distinction made in this passage regarding the command to believe for a disbeliever?
A) The command is impossible because belief is not in his power.
B) The command is valid because abandoning disbelief is in his power.
C) The command is valid because belief is always possible.
D) The command is impossible because God does not command what is impossible.

Key Takeaway
A command is valid if either the commanded act or its opposite is within the person's power, resolving the objection about commanding the impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever excused yourself from a religious duty because 'it's too hard' or 'I can't do it'? What is the opposite act—something you can actually do—that would bring you closer to fulfilling that duty? Will you commit to that opposite today?
?
The disbeliever is commanded to abandon disbelief, which is in his power. What sin have you normalized in your life that you could abandon, even if the perfect alternative seems out of reach? Will you honestly name that sin and take one concrete step to leave it?
?
Imagine standing before Allah and pleading weakness as an excuse. The Ash'ari distinction shows that if the opposite of what you were commanded was possible, you have no excuse. What command of Allah do you most resist, and what small opposite action can you start right now—not tomorrow?

Can a Capable Agent Have No Act?
Presents the disagreement among Mu'tazila on whether a capable agent can be free of all possible acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite disagreement over whether a human capable agent can exist in a state with no performed acts.

Core Idea
Human power's relation to acts sparked disagreement among Mu'tazila.

This lesson explores a key branch of Mu'tazilite theology: can a capable agent (a human) ever be completely without any act? The debate hinges on whether power necessarily produces an act if no impediment exists. We'll examine two major positions and the subtle distinctions they draw between direct and indirect acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the two positions discussed. Notice the terms 'قادر' (capable), 'مقدورات' (possible acts), and 'خلو' (emptiness).
The passage is short but dense; take time to parse who said what.
2
Identify the two positions
Identify the view of Abu Hashim and his followers versus that of al-Jubba'i. Note that Abu Hashim allows emptiness absolutely, while al-Jubba'i makes it conditional.
Al-Jubba'i distinguishes between direct (مباشر) and indirect (مولد) acts, and the presence or absence of an impediment (مانع).
3
Understand al-Jubba'i's distinction
Focus on al-Jubba'i's conditions: with an impediment, emptiness is allowed; without impediment, it is prohibited for direct acts but allowed for indirect acts. Ask why indirect acts might differ.
Indirect acts depend on external factors beyond the agent's immediate control.
4
Apply the learning
Summarize the disagreement and explain why this matters for Mu'tazilite theology. Check your understanding with the checkpoint question.
This branch reveals how Mu'tazila viewed the relationship between power and action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فروع للمعتزلة مبنية على مذهبهم في القدرة الحادثة (الأول) هل يخلو القادر عن جميع مقدوراته جوزه أبو هاشم وأتباعه مطلقاً وفصل الجبائي فجوزه أي الخلو عن جميع المقدورات عند وجود المانع ومنعه عند عدمه في المباشر دون المولد أي لم يجوز الخلو عند عدم المانع في الأفعال المباشرة وجوزه في الأفعال المولدة وقد تبين أن القدرة الحادثة لا تخلو عن
Branches for the Mu'tazila based on their doctrine concerning the originated power. The first: can the capable agent be devoid of all his possible acts? Abu Hashim and his followers permitted it absolutely. Al-Jubba'i made a distinction: he permitted it (i.e., being devoid of all possible acts) in the presence of an impediment, and prohibited it in its absence in the direct acts, not the indirect acts; i.e., he did not permit being devoid in the absence of an impediment in direct acts, and he permitted it in indirect acts. And it has been shown that the originated power is not devoid of...
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage classifies a specific point of disagreement among the Mu'tazila regarding the nature of power. It presents the first branch (فرع) which asks whether a capable agent (i.e., a human) can exist in a state where none of his possible acts are performed. Abu Hashim and his followers held that it is absolutely permissible for the agent to have no act. Al-Jubba'i distinguished between cases: if there is an impediment (مانع) preventing the act, then being devoid of acts is permissible; but if there is no impediment, then for direct acts (المباشرة) the agent cannot be devoid because the power necessitates the act, but for indirect or generated acts (المولدة) it is permissible because the generation of the act depends on external conditions. The passage ends by stating that it has been shown that the originated power is not free from (incomplete).

Key Terms
قادر
مقدورات
خلو
مانع
مباشر
مولد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This branch is about God's power and whether He can avoid creating.
Correction: The passage explicitly discusses human 'originated power' (القدرة الحادثة) and its relation to human acts, not divine power.

Checkpoint
According to al-Jubba'i, when can the capable agent be devoid of all possible acts?
A) Absolutely always.
B) Only in direct acts with no impediment.
C) In indirect acts and in direct acts when there is an impediment.
D) Never.

Key Takeaway
Mu'tazilite thinkers disagreed on whether a human agent can exist without performing any act, with al-Jubba'i making nuanced distinctions based on impediment and act type.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Reflect on your own life: Are there areas where you have the ability to do good but you are doing nothing at all? What does this lesson's debate suggest about the state of your heart when you leave your abilities unused?

Are they genuine obstacles, or are they excuses? How does this classification challenge you to examine your excuses?
?
The distinction between direct (مباشر) and indirect (مولد) acts shows that some actions depend on intermediate causes. Think of a good deed you have been postponing because it seems indirect or requires conditions. How Ash‘aris Classify Actions and the Scope of Power
Jurjani classifies actions into two types and states the Ash'ari rule on power's scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash‘ari classification of actions into those requiring an instrument and those that do not, and the principle that originated power only relates to acts within its own locus.

Core Idea
Ash‘ari power is limited to the agent's immediate act, not its external effects.

Jurjani lays out a foundational distinction in Ash‘ari theology: not all acts that appear to be caused by us are directly under our power. Some actions, like moving your hand, are within the locus of power, while others, like moving a stone by moving your hand, are external. The Ash‘ari rule is clear: originated power only attaches to what is within its own locus, not to external effects. This passage clarifies that distinction and explains why the stone's movement is not 'generated' by the hand's movement in the Ash‘ari sense.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get the author's own flow.
Notice the technical terms: al-āla (instrument), al-maḥall (locus), al-mubāshara (directness).
2
Identify the two categories of actions
Look for the division: actions that do not need an instrument (like hand movement) and those that need an instrument (like stone movement via hand). Understand the concept of 'locus of power'.
The first category is 'subsisting in the locus', the second is 'external to the locus'.
3
Grasp the Ash‘ari rule
Find the sentence: 'وعند الأشاعرة أن القدرة الحادثة لا تتعلق بما في غير محلها'. This is the core doctrinal point – originated power does not relate to what is outside its locus.
This means you cannot directly cause something external; you only cause the action in your own limb.
4
Understand the gloss on generation (tawlīd)
Read the explanation of the stone example. Note that both movements occur directly (bi-l-mubāshara), but the stone's movement is not 'generated' from the hand's movement because it requires intention and grasping. The term 'muwallid' is defined as that which necessitates another act, intended or not.
This counters the idea of necessary causal generation between two events.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الرابع - المقصد الخامس : القدرة الحادثة وتعلقها بالفعل
مقدورها عند الأشاعرة . الفرع ( الثاني ( أنهم اتفقوا على أنه ( تنقسم الأفعال المقدورة إلى ما لا يحتاج في وقوعه ( إِلى آلة كالقائمة بالمحل ) أي كالأفعال القائمة بمحل القدرة مثل حركة اليد ( وإلى ما يحتاج في وقوعه إلى آلة ( كالخارجة عنه ) أي كالأفعال الخارجة عن محل القدرة مثل حركة الحجر بتحريك اليد، وعند الأشاعرة أن القدرة الحادثة لا تتعلق بما في غير محلها .
قوله : ( مثل حركة الحجر) فكل واحدة من حركة اليد وحركة الحجر واقعة بالمباشرة إلا أن الأول بلا آلة تعلق القدرة التي في البديهيات لابتوسط اليد وليست الحركة الثانية بمولدة من الأولى إذ لا تتعلق لأنها لا توجد بدون القصد وأخذ الحجر والمولد ما يوجبه فعل آخر سواء كان قصد الفاعل أو لم يقصد .
The fourth type - the fifth objective: The originated power and its relation to the act. Its object of power according to the Ash'aris. The second branch: They agreed that the acts that are objects of power are divided into what does not need an instrument in its occurrence, like those subsisting in the locus, i.e., acts subsisting in the locus of power such as the movement of the hand; and into what needs an instrument in its occurrence, like those external to it, i.e., acts external to the locus of power such as the movement of a stone by moving the hand. And according to the Ash'aris, the originated power does not relate to what is outside its locus. His saying: (like the movement of a stone): Each of the movement of the hand and the movement of the stone occurs directly, except that the first is without an instrument, the power that is in the [؟] attaches to the hand, and the second movement is not generated from the first, indeed it does not attach because it does not exist without intention and taking the stone, and the generator is that which necessitates another act, whether the agent intended it or not.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage defines the classification of actions that are objects of power (al-af‘al al-maqdūra) according to the Ash‘aris. The author begins by stating the second branch of the fifth objective of the fourth type. He explains that there is agreement among the scholars that actions are divided into two categories: those that do not require an instrument (āla) for their occurrence, such as actions that subsist in the same locus as the power (e.g., the movement of the hand, where power is in the hand and the act is in the hand); and those that require an instrument, such as actions that are external to the locus of power (e.g., the movement of a stone caused by moving the hand). The Ash‘ari position is then stated: originated power (al-qudra al-ḥāditha) does not relate to what is outside its own locus. The gloss (qāla) explains the example of the stone's movement: both hand movement and stone movement are direct (bi-l-mubāshara), but the first is without an instrument, while the second is not 'generated' (muwallada) from the first because it does not occur without intention and grasping the stone. The term 'generator' (muwallid) is defined as that which necessitates another act, whether the agent intended it or not.

الأفعال المقدورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that power extends to effects outside its locus directly, so that moving the hand causes the stone to move by necessity.
Correction: The Ash'ari position limits power to the act within the locus itself. The stone's movement is not caused by the hand movement through generation; it is a direct act that requires intention and grasping, and originated power does not attach to it.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'aris, can originated power (al-qudra al-ḥāditha) relate to an act outside its own locus?
A) Yes, it can relate to external acts.
B) No, it only relates to acts within its locus.
C) Sometimes, depending on the instrument.
D) Only if the act is generated.

Key Takeaway
The Ash‘ari position restricts originated power to actions within the agent's own locus; external effects are not directly within its scope.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you plan your day, do you assume you have direct power over all outcomes, or do you acknowledge that many effects are outside your immediate control? How does the Ash'ari teaching on the limitations of power challenge your everyday sense of agency? What would change if you truly internalized that your power only covers your immediate act?
?
In what area of your life do you feel frustrated because you try to generate external results, forgetting that your power only covers your own effort? How can this truth free you from the burden of outcomes and shift your focus to intention and sincere action?
?
Think of a time you held yourself responsible for someone else's reaction to your words. Temporal Relation of Power to the Act: Two Opinions
Jurjani presents the disagreement on the temporal relation of power to its act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main opinions on how originated power relates to its act temporally, and the agreed-upon principle that power cannot exist without a relation.

Core Idea
Originated power must relate to an act; but is it only for the next moment or for all future moments?

After establishing that power cannot exist without being related to its object, scholars disagreed on the temporal modality of that relation. This passage presents two classical opinions: one restricting the relation to the immediate next moment ('uqb), and another extending it to all subsequent moments. Understanding this disagreement is key to deeper debates on divine power and human actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening premise
The author states that all agree power cannot exist unrelated. Understand why this is a necessary starting point.
The phrase 'لا تبقى غير متعلقة' means it is impossible for power to exist without a relation.
2
Identify the first opinion
One group holds that power relates only to the act immediately after it (in the second state). That is, at the moment of its existence, it is tied to the next moment only.
The term 'عقيبها' means 'immediately after it'.
3
Identify the second opinion
Another group holds that power at the moment of its existence is related to all future moments (second, third, etc.), not just the immediate next.
The phrase 'بما بعدها مطلقاً' means 'to what comes after it absolutely'.
4
Note the reference to Al-Āmidī
Al-Āmidī is cited to mention those who specify the relation to the state — but the main text breaks off here. The glosses help clarify the intended meaning.
This shows how the discussion continues in later works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرع ( الثالث : اتفقوا على أنها لا تبقى غير متعلقة ) أي يستحيل أن توجد القدرة مع أنها لا تتعلق بمقدورها أصلا لكنهم اختلفوا في كيفية تعلقها به ( فقيل : القدرة ) الحادثة ( تتعلق بالفعل عقيبها أي هي في وقت وجودها متعلقة بالمقدور في الحالة الثانية فقط، فلا تتعلق في الحالة الثالثة إلا في الحالة الثانية وكذلك المقدور في الحالة الرابعة لا تتعلق به القدرة إلا في الحالة الثالثة وهكذا ( وقيل : ( القدرة حال وجودها متعلقة بما بعدها مطلقاً) أي هي في تلك الحالة متعلقة بوجود الفعل في الحالة الثانية والثالثة وما بعدها وليس يختص تعلقها بوجود الفعل في الحالة الثانية فقط، قال الآمدي : ثم إن المخصصين لتعلقها بالحالة
قوله : ( لا تتعلق بما في غير محلها لأن التعلق مع الفعل والفعل الخارج لو وجد بعد موت هذا ظهر كون الانقسام فرعاً لتقدم القدرة، وقوله أي يستحيل أن يوجد إلخ إشارة إلى أن المراد بعدم البقاء استحالة وجودها مطلقاً. قوله : ( في الحالة الثانية) متعلق المقدور أي حال كونه في الحالة الثانية لا بقوله متعلقة لأن التعلق في وقت وجودها وكذا قوله في الحالة الثانية متعلق بقوله به أي المقدور في الحالة الثالثة . قوله : ( إلا في الحالة الثانية) متعلق بقوله به فلا يتعلق بغيره وهكذا فيما بعده.
The third branch: They agreed that it (i.e., power) does not remain unrelated, meaning it is impossible for power to exist while not relating to its object of power at all. But they disagreed on how it relates to it. One opinion: Originated power relates to the act immediately after it (i.e., at the moment of its existence it is related to the object of power in the second state only; it does not relate in the third state except in the second state; similarly, the object of power in the fourth state is not related by power except in the third state, and so on). Another opinion: Power at the moment of its existence is related to what comes after it absolutely, meaning in that state it is related to the existence of the act in the second, third, and subsequent states; its relation is not restricted to the existence of the act in the second state only. Al-Amidi said: Then those who specify its relation to the state...
His saying: (does not relate to what is outside its locus) because relation is with the act, and if the external act occurred after the death of this one, the division would be a branch of the precedence of power. And his saying: 'meaning it is impossible for it to exist' etc. indicates that what is meant by not remaining is the impossibility of its existence absolutely. His saying: (in the second state) is related to 'the object of power', meaning while it is in the second state, not to his saying 'related', because the relation is at the time of its existence. Similarly, his saying 'in the second state' is related to his saying 'by it', i.e., the object of power in the third state. His saying: (except in the second state) is related to his saying 'by it', so it does not relate to anything else, and so on thereafter.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage presents the third branch of the discussion on the relation of originated power to its effect. The author states the agreed-upon principle: it is impossible for power to exist without being related to its object (maqdūr). However, scholars differed on the modality of this relation. Two opinions are given: (1) Power relates only to the act 'immediately after it' (ʿuqaybahā), meaning at the moment of its existence it is related to the object only in the second state (the immediate future), and then in the third state it relates only to the object in the second state, and so on in a chain of successive moments. (2) Power at the moment of its existence is related to all future moments (mā baʿdahā muṭlaqan), i.e., it encompasses the existence of the act in the second, third, and subsequent states without restriction to only the immediate next. Al-Āmidī is then cited as noting that those who specify its relation to the state... (the text breaks off). The glosses (qāla) then clarify the syntax and meaning: The phrase 'does not relate to what is outside its locus' is explained as referring to relation with the act; if the external act occurred after the death of the agent, the division would be a branch of the precedence of power. The expression 'meaning it is impossible for it to exist' indicates that 'not remaining' (lā tabqā) here means absolute impossibility of existence. Further, the glosses explain the grammatical attachments: 'in the second state' is attached to 'object of power' meaning the object as being in the second state, not to 'related'; similarly, 'in the second state' is attached to 'by it' (i.e., the object in the third state). The phrase 'except in the second state' is attached to 'by it', so it does not relate to anything else, and so forth.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'لا تبقى غير متعلقة' means power can exist without relation for a short while, then later becomes related.
Correction: The phrase actually means it is absolutely impossible for power to exist at all if it is unrelated; the gloss clarifies that 'not remaining' here indicates impossibility of existence.

Checkpoint

B) The first state only.
C) The second state only.
D) The third state only.

Key Takeaway
The two opinions on the temporal relation of power show that theological disagreements often stem from different interpretations of how an attribute relates to its effect in time.

Abu Hudhayl on Power Timing: Heart vs. Limbs
Jurjani reports Abu Hudhayl's distinction and al-Amidi's dismissal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu Hudhayl's distinction between the timing of power for heart actions and limb actions, and al-Amidi's methodological dismissal of such disputes.

Core Idea
Not all theological disputes have solid foundations; some are dismissed at first glance.

This brief passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif presents a disagreement attributed to Abu Hudhayl al-Allaf about the temporal relationship between power and actions of the heart versus limbs. While this might seem like a subtle theological point, al-Amidi's blunt dismissal serves as an important reminder that not every historical disagreement warrants deep study. Let's examine the distinction and the reason for its rejection.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once to get its flow. Notice the structure: first Abu Hudhayl's statement, then al-Amidi's comment. Note the parentheses in the Arabic which indicate editorial gloss.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Understand Abu Hudhayl's two claims
Identify the two rules: power for heart actions is simultaneous with them; power for limb actions must precede them. Ask yourself why this distinction might be proposed.
Consider the nature of heart actions (e.g., beliefs, intentions) versus limb actions (e.g., physical movements).
3
Reflect on al-Amidi's dismissal
Al-Amidi says these disagreements have no basis and their falsity is apparent early on. This tells us that not all historical theological debates are now considered valid. Why might he consider this baseless?
This lesson connects to methodology: how scholars decide which disputes are worth studying.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرع الرابع : قال أبو الهذيل : ( العلاف القدرة على أفعال القلوب (معها ولا يجوز تقدمها عليها (و) القدرة ) على أفعال الجوارح ) يجب تكون (قبلها ) قال الآمدي : هذه وأمثالها من الاختلافات التي لا مستند لها يظهر فسادها بأوائل النظر فيها والاشتغال بها تضييع للزمان في غيرهم فلذلك أعرضنا عنها .
The fourth branch: Abu Hudhayl al-‘Allaf said: Power over acts of the heart (is) with them, and it is not permissible for it to precede them. And power over acts of the limbs must be before them. Al-Amidi said: These and similar disagreements have no basis; their falsity becomes apparent at the very beginning of investigation, and occupying oneself with them is a waste of time for others, so we turned away from them.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth branch of the fifth objective. It reports the opinion of Abu Hudhayl al-‘Allaf concerning the temporal relation of power to two types of acts: acts of the heart (af‘āl al-qulūb) and acts of the limbs (af‘āl al-jawāriḥ). He claims that power for heart acts is simultaneous (ma‘ahā) and cannot precede them, whereas power for limb acts must precede them. The author then quotes al-Āmidī, who dismisses such disagreements as having no basis (lā mustanad laha) and declares their falsity evident upon initial scrutiny. Al-Āmidī further regards engaging with them as a waste of time and therefore chooses not to discuss them. This serves as a methodological note, indicating that not all theological disputes warrant serious consideration.

Misconception: Abu Hudhayl's distinction is a widely accepted theological position.
Correction: Al-Amidi dismisses it as baseless, showing that not all historical disagreements are considered valid.

Key Takeaway
The passage illustrates that some historical theological positions, like Abu Hudhayl's distinction on the timing of power, were considered baseless by later scholars like al-Amidi, reminding us that theological discourse includes both substantive and dismissible arguments.

Is the Prohibited Person Capable?
Jurjani presents the dispute on whether someone prohibited from an act is still capable of it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari and Mu'tazila positions on whether a person prohibited from an act is capable of it, and the distinction between inability and prohibition.

Core Idea
Prohibition does not destroy power; inability does.

This passage addresses a key disagreement between the Ash'aris and Mu'tazila: can a person who is prohibited from doing something still be capable of it? The Ash'aris deny it because power is simultaneous with the act, while the Mu'tazila affirm it by distinguishing between external prohibition and internal inability. The accompanying gloss clarifies what is meant by 'object of power' and provides an example of a person with a tremor.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to get the overall sense.
Notice the structure: question, Ash'ari denial, Mu'tazila affirmation, then glosses.
2
Identify the two positions
Compare the Ash'ari claim (no power without act) with the Mu'tazila claim (prohibition does not negate power). Understand why they differ.
The Ash'ari view is based on the principle 'power is with the act'.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila distinction
The Mu'tazila distinguish inability ('ajz) from prohibition (man'). Inability opposes power itself, but prohibition does not.
This distinction is central to their argument.
4
Examine the gloss and tremor example
The gloss explains 'object of power' (maqdur) as the essence of the act, not as an object of power. The tremor example illustrates true inability.
In the tremor case, the movement occurs involuntarily, so the person lacks power.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this dispute matters for divine attributes and human responsibility. Does prohibition imply inability?
This ties into broader debates on qadar and human capacity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الممنوع عن الفعل هل هو قادر عليه حال كونه ممنوعاً عنه ( منعه الأشاعرة إذ القدرة ) عندهم ( مع الفعل ) فلا يتصور كون الممنوع عن فعل قادر عليه في حالة المنع إذ لا فعل حينئذ فلا قدرة عليه ( وقال به أي بكون الممنوع قادراً على الفعل ( المعتزلة ) وفرقوا بين العجز والمنع حيث ( قالوا : العجز يضاد القدرة دون المقدور
قوله : (المقدور) أي ذات المقدور لا من حيث أنه مقدور فإن المرتعش عاجز أي ليس له قدرة على الحركة مع حصولها اضطراراً .
قوله : ( يضاد) القدرة دون (المقدور أي دون ذات المقدور وإن كان تضاده من حيث أنه مقدور وإنما قالوا العجز لا يضاد ذات المقدور لأن المحرك يده بالاختيار إذا عرض له حالة حركتها ارتعاً فذات المقدور أعني الحركة باقية بالاضطرار .
Is the person prohibited from the act capable of it while he is prohibited from it? The Ash‘aris denied it, because power, according to them, is with the act. So it is inconceivable that a person prohibited from an act is capable of it in the state of prohibition, for there is no act then, hence no power over it. The Mu‘tazila affirmed it, i.e., that the prohibited person is capable of the act, and they distinguished between inability ('ajz) and prohibition (man‘), for they said: Inability opposes power, not the object of power.
His saying: (the object of power) means the essence of the object of power, not insofar as it is an object of power; for the one with a tremor (murti‘ish) is incapable, i.e., he has no power over the movement while it occurs involuntarily.
His saying: (opposes) power, not the object of power, i.e., not the essence of the object of power, even if its opposition is insofar as it is an object of power. They only said that inability does not oppose the essence of the object of power because the one who moves his hand voluntarily, if a state of tremor occurs in it, the essence of the object of power, namely the movement, remains involuntarily.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage addresses the question: Is a person who is prohibited from an act capable of performing it while under prohibition? The Ash‘aris deny this, because for them power is simultaneous with the act (al-qudra ma‘a al-fi‘l). Hence, in a state where there is no act (due to prohibition), there can be no power. The Mu‘tazila affirm that the prohibited person is capable, and they draw a distinction between inability (al-‘ajz) and prohibition (al-man‘). They argue that inability opposes power itself, but prohibition does not; one can have power even if prevented by an external prohibition. The glosses clarify: 'the object of power' (al-maqdūr) means the essence of the act (dhāt al-maqdūr), not considered as an object of power. An example is given of a person with a tremor (al-murti‘ish) who is genuinely incapable ('ājiz) because the movement occurs involuntarily; here there is no power. But prohibition does not negate power. Another gloss explains that inability opposes power itself, not the essence of the act. Even though inability opposes the act insofar as it is an object of power, the essence of the act can still exist involuntarily, as in the tremor example: the movement (essence) remains, but the person has no power over it.

Key Terms
الممنوع عن الفعل
القدرة
العجز
المنع
المقدور
المرتعش

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that external prohibition (man‘) is the same as inability (‘ajz) and both negate power.
Correction: The Mu'tazila insist that prohibition does not negate power; only inability does. A person can be capable even if prohibited, as long as he is not internally incapacitated.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila, what is the relationship between inability (al-‘ajz) and the object of power?
A) Inability opposes the object of power.
B) Inability opposes power, not the object of power.
C) Inability and the object of power are identical.
D) Inability is a form of prohibition.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila distinguish prohibition from inability: external prohibition does not destroy capacity, while internal inability does.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin despite knowing Allah's prohibition, do you ever tell yourself that you were 'forced' or 'unable' to resist? In what area of your life have you mistaken a prohibition for an incapacity, and how might that change your accountability before Allah?
?
The person with a tremor has no control over his hand's movement. Is there a habit or addiction in your life that has moved from voluntary to involuntary, leaving you spiritually incapacitated? What would it take for you to reclaim your power over it?
?
Allah's commands and prohibitions are tests of your faith. If you truly believe you are capable of obeying them, why do you sometimes act as if you are not? What lie about yourself are you believing that makes you think obedience is impossible?

One Power, One Act: The Ash'ari Proof
Al-Iji states the Ash'ari doctrine and proof regarding power not relating to two objects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari argument that a single power (al-qudra al-wāḥida) cannot relate to two objects of power, and why substitution (badal) is also invalid.

Core Idea
One power, one act: power accompanies the act and cannot extend to two.

This passage presents one of the most characteristic Ash'ari doctrines: that power (qudra) is simultaneous with the act and therefore can only be related to one object. Al-Iji states the position and provides a proof based on our immediate experience of performing acts. The commentary clarifies what 'substitution' could mean and why it is not allowed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own wording come through.
Notice the technical terms: الضدين, البدل, المقدور.
2
Identify the main claim
Find the sentence that states the core doctrine: a single power does not relate to two opposites, nor to any two objects, neither together nor on a substitutional basis.
3
Understand the proof
The proof is: because power is with the act, and we find a difference in what we experience when each act occurs. This shows that the power for each act is distinct.
The proof relies on the premise that power is simultaneous with the act, not before it.
4
Note the rejection of substitution
The passage explicitly denies that a single power can relate to two objects even on a substitutional basis. Read the commentary's explanation of the two senses of substitution.
Substitution in the sense of relating to one after the other is rejected because it would imply power before the act.
5
Reflect on the implication
If power were before the act, it could relate to two, but since it is with the act, it cannot. This is a key difference from the Mu'tazilite view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الرابع - المقصد السابع : القدرة مع الفعل لا تتعلق بالضدين
[ المقصد السابع : القدرة مع الفعل لا تتعلق بالضدين ]
( قال الشيخ : ( وأكثر أصحابه ( بناء على كون القدرة ( عندهم ( مع الفعل ) لا قبله (إنها ) أي القدرة الواحدة ( لا تتعلق بالضدين وإلا لزم اجتماعهما لوجوب مقارنتهما لتلك القدرة المتعلقة بهما (بل) قالوا : إِن القدرة الواحدة لا تتعلق بمقدورين (مطلقاً سواء كانا متضادين أو متماثلين أو مختلفين لا معاً، ولا على سبيل البدل بل القدرة الواحدة لا تتعلق إلا بمقدور واحد، وذلك لأنها مع المقدور، ولا شك أن ما نجده عند صدور أحد المقدورين منا مغاير لما نجده عند صدور الآخر
قوله : ( لوجوب إلخ ) ويلزم وقوع المكسوب في محل القدرة.
قوله: (ولا) على سبيل (البدل بأن يتعلق أحدهما ابتداء بدل التعلق بالآخر وأما التعلق بأحدهما عقيب التعلق بالآخر، فلا يقال له على سبيل البدل، بل على التقابل وخلاف المذهب فإنه يستلزم أن يكون القدرة على الثاني قبله.
قوله : ( ولا شك أن ما نجده إلخ فيه أنه إن أراد المغايرة بينهما بالذات فممنوعة، وإن أراد المغايرة بينهما باعتبار الشرائط والآلات فمسلم لكن لا يثبت المدعى وهو عدم تعلق القدرة الواحدة بهما .
قوله : ( وإلا لزم اجتماعهما لوجوب مقارنتهما إلخ) لزوم اجتماعهما بناء على ما سبق من أن القدرة الحادثة عند الأشاعرة بما في محلها ثم إن هذا الدليل بعينه يدل على عدم تعلق القدرة بالمثلين أيضاً وذلك ظاهر.
قوله : ( ولا) على سبيل إلخ) سواء كان معنى البدلية أن كلاً الضدين منفرداً عن الآخر من يجوز تعلق القدرة به ابتداء أو كان معناها جواز تعلقها به بعد تعلقها بالآخر، فإن قوله : ولا شك إلخ، يدل على نفيهما أما تجويز كثير من أصحابنا للتعلق على البدلية مع قولهم : بأن القدرة مع الفعل فهو بالمعنى الأول لا الثاني، وإلا لزم القول ببقاء القدرة وكونها قبل الفعل.
نفسه
The Fourth Type - The Seventh Topic: Power with the act does not relate to opposites.
[The Seventh Topic: Power with the act does not relate to opposites.]
(The Shaykh said: (And most of his companions (based on the fact that power (according to them (is with the act) not before it (that it), i.e., the single power, (does not relate to opposites, otherwise their coexistence would be necessary because of the necessity of their simultaneous occurrence with that power related to them). (Rather) they said: The single power does not relate to two objects of power (absolutely, whether they are opposites or similars or different, neither together nor on the basis of substitution (badal), but the single power does not relate except to one object of power. And that is because it is with the object of power, and there is no doubt that what we find at the occurrence of one of the two objects of power from us is different from what we find at the occurrence of the other.)
His saying: (For the necessity etc.) And it necessitates the occurrence of the acquired (al-maksūb) in the locus of power.
His saying: (Nor) on the basis of (substitution (al-badal): by one of them relating initially instead of relating to the other. As for the relation to one after the relation to the other, it is not called substitution, but rather alternation (al-taqābul) and contrary to the school (madhhab), because it necessitates that the power over the second be before it.
His saying: (And there is no doubt that what we find etc.) In it is that if he intends the difference between them in essence, it is not granted; if he intends the difference between them in terms of conditions and instruments, it is conceded, but it does not prove the claim, which is the non-relation of the single power to both.
His saying: (Otherwise their coexistence would be necessary because of the necessity of their simultaneous occurrence etc.) The necessity of their coexistence is based on what preceded: that the originated power (al-qudra al-ḥāditha) according to the Ash'aris is in its locus. Then indeed this very proof indicates the non-relation of power to two similars also, and that is obvious.
His saying: (Nor) on the basis of substitution etc. Whether the meaning of substitution is that each of the two opposites, individually, it is permissible for power to relate to it initially, or its meaning is the permissibility of relating to it after relating to the other. Indeed his saying: 'And there is no doubt etc.' indicates their negation. As for the permissibility of many of our companions for relation on substitutional basis with their saying that power is with the act, it is in the first sense, not the second. Otherwise it would necessitate the saying that power remains and exists before the act.
Himself (nafsuh)
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage defines the Ash'ari position on the relation of power to opposites and provides a proof. It begins by stating the doctrine: a single power (with the act) does not relate to opposites, because if it did, both opposites would have to coexist simultaneously, which is impossible. Then it extends this to any two objects of power: the single power cannot relate to two objects, whether they are opposites, similars, or different, neither together nor on a substitutional basis. It only relates to one object. The proof is that power is simultaneous with the act, and we perceive a difference in what we experience when we perform one act versus another. The marginal comments then explain and defend this proof. The first comment clarifies that the proof implies the acquired act (maksūb) cannot be in the locus of power before it? Actually it says 'it necessitates the occurrence of the acquired in the locus of power' which might refer to an implication of the proof. The second comment defines substitution (badal) and distinguishes it from alternation (taqābul), explaining that the relation to one after the other is not substitution but alternation and contradicts the school because it would require power before the act. The third comment discusses the nature of the difference between the two experiences: if essence difference, it is not granted; if conditional difference, it is conceded but does not prove the claim. The fourth comment explains that the same proof also works against relating to two similars. The fifth comment discusses the meaning of substitution and distinguishes two interpretations, showing that the permissibility of substitution among some Ash'aris is only in the first sense (initial relation) not the second (sequential), because the second would imply the existence of power before the act. The passage ends with 'nafsuh' indicating the end of the commentary or a reference to the author's own speech.

المقدور
المكسوب
التقابل

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Ash'ari proof only denies power over two opposites, not over two similars or different acts.
Correction: The passage explicitly says 'whether they are opposites or similars or different', so the denial applies to any two objects of power.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari position presented, what is the main proof given that a single power cannot relate to two objects of power?
A) Because power is before the act, so it cannot apply to two at once.
B) Because power is with the act, and we find a difference in what we experience when each act occurs.
C) Because the Mu'tazila said so.
D) Because the two objects would have to be in the same locus.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari argument that a single power cannot relate to two objects is grounded in the simultaneity of power with the act and our direct experience of a distinct power for each act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your power to act is created by Allah at the very moment of each action, what does that mean for your reliance on your own strength? Are you trusting your own abilities or turning to Allah every time you do something? Commit to a specific action today that reminds you that your power is from Allah.
?
The Ash'aris argue that you cannot attribute your success in one deed to the same power that failed you in another. How does this affect your view of your own consistency? Will you stop making excuses for sin by saying 'I just couldn't control myself'?
?
If power is simultaneous with the act, then your past actions are gone and your future actions are not yet powered. How should this reshape your repentance and your hope? Make a concrete plan to seek Allah's power for your next moment of trial.

The Objection that Capacity with the Act Does Not Relate to Both Opposites
Defends the Ash'ari principle by answering the compulsion objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position on capacity with the act, the objection that it leads to compulsion, and the nuanced response involving two senses of compulsion.

Core Idea
Capacity with the act is unique to that act; compulsion limited to impossibility of separation does not negate capacity.

This passage tackles a critical objection to the Ash'ari view of capacity: if the capacity accompanying an act is so tied to that act that the agent cannot do its opposite, then the agent seems compelled. The Ash'ari response carefully distinguishes two meanings of 'compelled' to defend their position without denying divine justice or human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim.
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the opening statement: capacity with the act does not relate to the two opposites. This is the claim being defended.
The passage then presents an objection and a detailed answer.
2
Understand the objection.
The objection is that if capacity is tied to only one act, the agent would be compelled (mudtarr). Ask yourself: why would this be a problem? What does it imply about human responsibility?
The objection aims to show that the Ash'ari view destroys moral accountability.
3
Analyze the Ash'ari response.
The response distinguishes two definitions of 'compelled': (1) the act is not within one's power, and (2) one's power is directed exclusively to this act. The Ash'aris accept the second sense but deny the first. Reflect on how this distinction preserves both capacity and divine sovereignty.
Pay attention to the analogy of the person trapped in a building.
4
Consider al-Amidi's additional argument.
Al-Amidi offers a further point: even if we grant that a capable agent must be able to do the opposite, the capacities might be multiple (not one). This reinforces that the same capacity need not relate to both opposites.
This is a supplementary point, not the main response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الرابع - المقصد السابع : القدرة مع الفعل لا تتعلق بالضدين بحيث لا يقدر على الانفكاك عنه، لا قادر عليه وهو باطل كيف؟ وعليه أي على كون المكلف قادراً متمكناً من الفعل بنيت الدعوة إلى دين الحق والثواب والعقاب على الأفعال القلبية والقالبية، وإذا ثبت تعلقها بالمتضادات فتعلقها بغيرها أولى. وأجيب عن ذلك بأنه إن أريد بكونه مضطراً أن فعله غير مقدور له فهو ممنوع، وإن أريد به أن مقدوره ومتعلق قدرته متعين وأنه لا مقدور له بهذه القدرة سواه فهذا ما ندعيه ونلتزمه ولا منازعة لنا في تسميته مضطراً، فإن الاضطرار بمعنى امتناع الانفكاك لا ينافي القدرة، ألا ترى أن من أحاط به بناء من جميع جوانبه بحيث يعجز التقلب من جهة إلى أخرى، فإنه قادر على الكون في مكانه بإجماع منا ومنهم، أنه لا سبيل له إلى الانفكاك عن مقدوره. قال الآمدي: ولئن سلمنا أن القادر على مع الشيء لا بد أن يكون قادراً على ضده، قلنا جاز أن تكون القدرة المتعلقة بهما متعددة لا واحدة.
The fourth type - the seventh objective: Capacity with the act does not relate to the two opposites, such that he is not able to separate from it. He is not capable of it, and that is invalid, why? And upon it, i.e., upon the mukallaf being capable and able to perform the act, is built the call to the religion of truth, and reward and punishment, upon the acts of the heart and the tongue. And if its relation to opposites is established, its relation to non-opposites is more appropriate. And it was answered to that that if by his being compelled it is meant that his act is not within his power, then it is denied; and if by it is meant that what is within his power and the object of his capacity is determined and that he has no other object of this capacity besides it, then this is what we claim and adhere to, and we have no dispute with naming him compelled. For al-Idtirar (compulsion) in the sense of the impossibility of separation does not contradict capacity. Do you not see that one who is surrounded by a building from all sides such that he is unable to move from one place to another, he is capable of being in his place by consensus among us and them, and he has no way to separate from his object of power? Al-Amidi said: And even if we grant that the one who has power over a thing must be able to do its opposite, we say it is possible that the capacities relating to them are multiple, not one.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage begins by stating a core Ash'ari principle: the capacity that accompanies the act (al-qudra ma'a al-fi'l) does not relate to the two opposites (al-diddayn), meaning that the agent's power is so tied to the specific act that he cannot perform the opposite. The author then raises an objection: if so, the agent would be compelled, not truly capable. The answer distinguishes two meanings of 'compelled': if it means the act is not within his power, that is denied; if it means his capacity is exclusively directed to one act and he has no other power besides it, that is what the Ash'aris claim and they accept the label 'compelled' in this sense. The analogy of a person trapped in a building from all sides is given: he is still capable of being in his place, even though he cannot leave. This shows that compulsion in the sense of inability to separate from the act does not negate capacity. Then al-Amidi adds that even if one accepts that the capable must be able to do the opposite, the capacities could be multiple, so the same power does not have to relate to both opposites.

Key Terms

فعل
ضدان
اضطرار
مكلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think Ash'aris deny human capacity altogether, saying the agent has no power.
Correction: Ash'aris affirm a real capacity, but it is uniquely tied to the act that occurs; the agent is not powerless, but his power is limited to one specific act.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Ash'ari response to the objection that their view entails compulsion?
A) They deny that the agent is compelled in any sense.
B) They accept that the agent is compelled in the sense that his capacity is exclusively directed to one act.
C) They claim the agent has multiple capacities for the same act.
D) They reject the objection as invalid and do not answer it.

Key Takeaway
Capacity with the act is a real, specific power that does not extend to the opposite; compulsion in the sense of exclusive focus does not negate genuine capacity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to be responsible for your actions, but do you really have power to do other than what you did? Read the Ash'ari distinction carefully: your capacity is tied to what you actually did. Does that relieve you of the burden of choice, or does it actually strengthen your accountability? Reflect on your last major decision: was it possible that you could have done otherwise, or does this passage force you to admit that your capacity was uniquely fitted to that act? How does this change the way you think about repentance and regret?
?
The analogy of the person surrounded by walls shows that even without alternatives, one is still capable. Think of a situation in your life where you felt trapped with no good options. Did you still consider yourself capable of the act you performed? According to this lesson, that capacity was real. Does this give you comfort or discomfort? What does it mean for your relationship with Allah when you feel constrained yet still responsible?
?
Al-Amidi suggests that multiple capacities could be involved. Consider your daily acts of worship—do you ever feel that the capacity to pray or give charity comes from a different source than the capacity to sin? How does this perspective affect the way you seek strength for obedience and avoid disobedience? What would change in your spiritual life if you truly believed your capacity for each act is a unique gift from Allah?

Al-Razi's Two Meanings of Qudra: A Terminological Reconciliation
Al-Razi distinguishes two senses of power and uses them to reconcile Ash'ari and Mu'tazili views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's distinction between two senses of power (qudra) and how it resolves the apparent conflict between Ash'ari and Mu'tazili views on the timing and scope of capacity.

Core Idea
Power can mean mere muscle strength or fully conditioned efficacy; the apparent disagreement is only verbal.

Al-Razi offers a brilliant terminological clarification that cuts through a major theological dispute. By distinguishing two different meanings of 'power' (qudra), he shows that the Ash'ari and Mu'tazili positions are not actually contradictory. This passage illustrates how careful definition can resolve deep-seated polemics.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to get the overall argument.
Notice that al-Razi begins by stating two distinct definitions.
2
Identify the two definitions of power
Find where al-Razi defines 'power' as mere strength (quwwa mujarrada) and then as strength that has gathered all conditions (quwwa mustajmi'a). Understand each definition's characteristics.
The first is muscular power; the second is fully conditioned power.
3
Compare the implications of each definition
Notice that the first definition leads to power being before the act and relating to opposites equally, while the second definition leads to power being simultaneous with the act and not relating to opposites.
These are the points of disagreement between Ash'aris and Mu'tazilis.
4
Understand the reconciliation
Al-Razi suggests that al-Ash'ari used the second definition and the Mu'tazila the first, so they are not truly disagreeing.
This shows how linguistic clarification can resolve theological disputes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الإمام الرازي : القدرة تطلق على مجرد القوة التي هي مبدأ للأفعال المختلفة الحيوانية وهي القوة العضلية التي هي بحيث متى انضم إليها إرادة أحد الضدين حصل ذلك الضد، ومتى انضم إليها إرادة الضد الآخر حصل ذلك الآخر ولا شك أن نسبتها أي نسبة هذه القوة إلى الضدين سواء وهي قبل الفعل، والقدرة تطلق أيضا على القوة المستجمعة لشرائط التأثير برمتها، ولا شك أنها أي القوة المستجمعة لا تتعلق بالضدين معاً، وإلا اجتمعا في الوجود، بل هي أي القوة المستجمعة بالنسبة إلى كل مقدور غيرها بالنسبة إلى المقدور الآخر، سواء كانا متضادين أو غير متضادين، وذلك لاختلاف الشرائط المعتبرة في وجود المقدورات المختلفة، فإن خصوصية كل مقدور لها شرط مخصوص به يعين وجودها من بين المقدورات المشتركة في تلك القوة المجردة، ألا ترى أن القصد المتعلق بها شرط لوجودها دون غيرها، وهي مع الفعل لأن وجود المقدور لا يتخلف عن المؤثر التام. ولعل الشيخ الأشعري أراد بالقدرة القوة المستجمعة لشرائط التأثير، فلذلك حكم بأنها مع الفعل وأنها لا تتعلق بالضدين، والمعتزلة أرادوا بالقدرة مجرد القوة العضلية، فلذلك قالوا بوجودها قبل الفعل وتعلقها بالأمور المتضادة، فهذا وجه الجمع بين المذهبين.
Al-Imam al-Razi said: 'Power is used for the mere strength which is the principle of the various animal actions, and it is the muscular strength which is such that when a will for one of the two opposites is joined to it, that opposite occurs, and when the will for the other opposite is joined to it, that other occurs, and there is no doubt that its relation, i.e., the relation of this strength, to the two opposites is equal, and it is before the act. And power is also used for the strength that has gathered all the conditions of efficacy, and there is no doubt that it, i.e., the gathered strength, does not relate to the two opposites together, otherwise they would both exist together. Rather, it, i.e., the gathered strength, in relation to each object of power is other than it in relation to the other object of power, whether they are opposites or not. And that is due to the difference of conditions considered in the existence of different objects of power, for the particularity of each object of power has a condition specific to it that determines its existence from among the objects of power shared in that mere strength. Do you not see that the intent (qasd) pertaining to it is a condition for its existence apart from other than it? And it is with the act, because the existence of the object of power does not lag behind the complete efficient cause. And perhaps al-Shaykh al-Ash'ari intended by 'power' the strength that has gathered the conditions of efficacy, therefore he judged that it is with the act and that it does not relate to the two opposites. And the Mu'tazila intended by 'power' the mere muscular strength, therefore they said that it exists before the act and relates to opposite things. So this is the manner of reconciliation between the two schools.'
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage provides al-Razi's two-tiered definition of power (qudra) which serves to reconcile the Ash'ari and Mu'tazili positions. Al-Razi states that power is used in two senses: first, mere strength (al-quwwa al-mujarrada), which is the muscular power that is a principle of various animal actions. This strength has an equal relation to both opposites before the act; if will for one opposite is added, it occurs; if will for the other, that occurs. Second, power is used for the strength that has gathered all conditions of efficacy (al-quwwa al-mustajmi'a li-shara'it al-ta'thir). This gathered strength does not relate to both opposites simultaneously, otherwise both would coexist. Rather, it relates to each object of power in a way that is exclusive to that object, because different objects of power have different conditions. For example, the specific intent (qasd) for a particular act is a condition for its existence, not for another. This gathered strength is with the act, as the existence of the object of power does not lag behind the complete efficient cause. Al-Razi then suggests that al-Ash'ari meant power in the second sense (gathered), hence his judgment that it is with the act and does not relate to opposites. The Mu'tazila meant power in the first sense (mere muscle power), hence they said it exists before the act and relates to opposites. Thus, the apparent conflict is resolved by recognizing different definitions.

Key Terms
قدرة
قوة عضلية
قوة مستجمعة
شرائط التأثير
ضدان
إرادة
قصد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'aris deny any capacity (qudra) before the act, while the Mu'tazilis affirm it, and these are irreconcilable.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that the two schools are using different definitions of 'power'. Ash'aris refer to fully conditioned power (which is simultaneous), Mu'tazilis refer to mere muscle power (which exists before). They are not contradicting each other.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, what is the reason the gathered strength (al-mustajmi'a) does not relate to both opposites?
A) It is before the act.
B) It requires intention for each specific act.
C) It is mere strength.
D) It is not with the act.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi reconciles Ash'ari and Mu'tazili views on power by showing they use 'qudra' in two different senses: mere muscular strength (Mu'tazili) and fully conditioned power (Ash'ari).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a conflict with someone, do you pause to clarify definitions before arguing? How would your relationships change if you adopted al-Razi's method of looking for different meanings underlying disagreements? Commit this week to ask at least one person, 'What do you mean by that word?' before responding.
?
Al-Razi shows that power in its fullest sense is simultaneous with the act. Does this truth unsettle you? In what area of your life do you still assume you have independent power apart from Allah's enabling? Write down one specific action you plan to do today and consciously say to yourself, 'This only happens if Allah brings all conditions together.'
?
The Mu'tazila focused on mere muscular strength and felt confident before the act. Do you sometimes rely on your own raw abilities without acknowledging that the actual outcome depends on factors beyond your control? What is one situation where you need to shift from trusting your 'muscular strength' to humbly seeking the 'gathered conditions' from Allah?
