Analyzing Doubt about Equating Perceptions
Al-Jurjani analyses the base text's phrase about doubt and equation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Jurjani's analysis of doubt regarding the equation of perceptions of the sleeper and the awake.

Core Idea
Casting doubt on equation implies doubt about the distinction, not the equation itself.

In this final part of the commentary, al-Jurjani addresses a subtle point about the phrase 'casting doubt on equating them' in the case of the awake. He clarifies that the intended meaning is that the doubt is about the distinction between the two states, not about the equation itself. He explains that the equation implies doubt about the distinction, and that taking it at face value would mean allowing the possibility of equality, which is far from reason. This careful linguistic analysis shows the commentator's precision.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on doubt
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the commentator's interpretation of the phrase 'casting doubt on equating them'.
2
Distinguish between equation and distinction
The commentator says the doubt is not about the equation itself but about the distinction. Understand the difference: equation means treating two things as equal; distinction means recognizing a difference.
3
Understand the alternative interpretation
The commentator also considers the possibility of taking the phrase literally, which would mean the doubt is about the equation, but that would be far from reason because the equation is established. He prefers the first interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وإن كان لا علم له بها والإرادة والقدرة ليست تابعة للعلم والإرادة في جميع الأفعال، بل الصادرة عن قصد كما عرفت وما قيل من أنهم لو استدلوا على ثبوت علمه تعالى بأنه قادر وكل قادر عالم، لأن القادر ما يكون فعله من قصد وإرادة، ولا يتصور ذلك بدون العلم، فإنما يدل على أن كل قادر لا بد أن يكون عالماً مريداً، لا أن كل ما يتعلق به القدرة يجب أن يكون معلوماً ومراداً، ولم يثبت بهذا الدليل عموم علمه تعالى فتدبر فإنه قد زل فيه أقدام . قوله : غير مقدورة له بل هو صادرة عن الطبيعة بحسب عروض العوارض وإتقانها اتفاقي أن مكتسبة والأولى مقدورة، لأن الكسب تعلق القدرة على وفق الإرادة ولا إرادة فيها ولا شعور . قوله: لم يبعد عن التشكيك في تسويتهما أي التشكيك في الفرق تجويز ثبوتها فالكلام على حذف وكلمة في التعليل. يقولون بقيام الشعور بجزء وقيام النوم بجزء آخر، كما يقوله الأستاذ، وفيه نظر لأن المفروض أن القدرة قائمة بما يقوم به النوم كما يشهد به السياق والمدعى أن الذي يقوم به القدرة لا بد أن يقوم به العلم بمقدوره الصادر عنه فليتأمل . قوله : وقال الأستاذ أبو سحاق هي غير مقدورة له أي الأفعال الخارجية غير مقدورة له، فلا ينافي ما اختاره بعيد هذا من عدم الفرق بين إدراك النائم وإدراك اليقظان فيكون إدراك النائم أيضاً مقدوراً كإدراك اليقظان، والأظهر في دفع المنافاة أن مراده من عدم الفرق بينهما عدمه في الإدراكية لا عدمه من جميع الوجوه حتى يشمل المقدورية وعدمها . قوله : لم يبعد عنه التشكيك في تسويتهما في حق اليقظان أراد التسوية في الضرورية وعدم المقدورية، كما أنه المراد بالتسوية بينهما في النائم، ثم الأظهر أن متعلق التشكيك المدعى بعده عن المعقول هو الفرق بينهما الثابت بلا شبهة لانفس التسوية، لأن المتبادر حينئذ أن التسوية ثابتة في نفس الأمر، فالتشكيك فيها بعيد عن المعقول، وليس المراد هذا فكان مراده التشكيك الثابت في تسويتهما فإن التسوية متضمنة للتشكيك في الفرق ويمكن أن يحمل على ظاهره ويكون بعده عن المعقول بناء على أن إيقاع الشك في التسوية يتضمن تجويزها لأن الشك تساوي الطرفين فليفهم .
His saying: 'And even if he had no knowledge of them' – will and power are not dependent on knowledge and will in all actions, but only in those originating from intention, as you have known. And what was said that they deduced the existence of His knowledge – exalted is He – from the fact that He is powerful and every powerful being is knowing, because the powerful is one whose action is from intention and will, and that cannot be conceived without knowledge, – that only indicates that every powerful being must be knowing and willing, not that everything connected to power must be known and willed. And this proof does not establish the universality of His knowledge – exalted is He – so reflect, for feet have slipped therein. His saying: 'Not within his power' rather it originates from nature according to the occurrence of accidents, and its perfection is accidental that it is acquired, and the first is within power, because acquisition (kasb) is the attachment of power in accordance with will, and there is no will nor consciousness in it. They say that consciousness stands in one part and sleep stands in another part, as the teacher (al-Ustadh) says. But there is a problem in that, because it is assumed that power resides in that which is the locus of sleep, as the context indicates. And the claim is that that in which power resides must also reside knowledge of its object that originates from it – so let it be considered. His saying: 'And al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq said: They are not within his power' – meaning the external actions are not within his power. So it does not contradict what he (the commentator?) chose earlier, regarding the lack of difference between the perception of the sleeper and the perception of the awake, such that the sleeper's perception would also be within his power as the awake's perception. The more apparent way to repudiate the contradiction is that his intent by the lack of difference between them is lack of difference in perception (idrak), not lack of difference in all respects such that it includes power and its absence. His saying: 'He is not far from casting doubt on equating them in the case of the awake' – he intended equation in necessity and lack of being within power, just as that is the intended meaning of equation between them in the sleeper. Then the more apparent is that the object of the doubt claimed to be far from reason is the distinction between them established without any doubt, not the equation itself, because the immediate understanding then is that the equation is established in itself, so doubting it is far from reason. But this is not the intended meaning. Rather, his intended meaning is the doubt established in equating them, because the equation implies doubt about the distinction. And it can be taken at face value, and its being far from reason is based on the fact that casting doubt on the equation entails allowing it, because doubt is equality of the two sides – so understand.

Can Horizontal Power Lift Vertically? The Bahshamiyya Debate
Debate on whether horizontal power suffices for vertical motion and critique of Bahshamiyya's requirement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate on whether a power that moves horizontally can also cause vertical motion, and why the Bahshamiyya's requirement of exactly one additional power is critiqued as arbitrary.

Core Idea
Vertical motion requires a separate power — but why exactly one?

This passage from the Bahshamiyya school tackles a subtle question about the nature of power (qudra) in physical motion. The debate centers on whether the same power that moves an object right and left can also lift it upward. The Bahshamiyya argue it cannot, and they specify exactly one additional power is needed. The commentator, however, points out the arbitrariness of specifying a precise number.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the English translation. Let the author's argument flow naturally.
Notice the key terms: qudra, quwwa, tas'id, dahrja, Bahshamiyya, tahakkum.
2
Understand the problem
The passage asks: does the power that causes horizontal motion (right/left) also have strength for vertical motion (upward)? Identify the two opposing views mentioned.
Some permit it, some forbid it.
3
Examine the Bahshamiyya's position
The Bahshamiyya (followers of Abu Hashim) forbid it and require an additional single power for ascent. Note their reasoning based on the perceived difference between rolling and lifting.
They consider lifting harder and thus requiring a separate power.
4
Recognize the critique
The commentator calls their requirement 'tahakkum' (arbitrariness). Why? Because there is no rational basis to limit the needed increase to exactly one power; it might require more.
This critique guards against assuming a fixed number of powers without evidence.
5
Reflect on theological methodology
Consider the implications: in kalam, claims about divine attributes must be based on clear evidence, not arbitrary stipulation. How does this principle apply here?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القدرة المحركة يمنة ويسرة هل تقدر وتقوى على التصعيد والرفع إلى جهة الفوق منهم من جوزه ومنهم من منعه للفرق بين الدحرجة والرفع ضرورة فإن كل عاقل يجد تفاوتا بينهما ويعلم أن رفع شيء أشق وأقوى من تحريكه دحرجة وعليه أي على المنع البهشمية أي الطائفة التابعة لرأي أبي هاشم وأوجبوا للتصعيد والرفع زيادة قدرة واحدة على القدرة المحركة يمنة ويسرة ولا يخفى ما فيه من التحكم إذ لا وجه لحصر الزيادة الكافية على القدرة الواحدة لجواز الاحتياج إلى ما يزيد عليها.
The power that moves right and left – does it have strength (quwwa) for ascent and lifting to the upward direction? Some permit it and some forbid it due to the necessary distinction between rolling and lifting, for every rational person finds a difference between them and knows that lifting something is harder and stronger than moving it by rolling. And upon this (i.e., forbiddance) is the Bahshamiyya – the group following the opinion of Abu Hashim – and they required for ascent and lifting an additional single power (qudra) over the power that moves right and left. And it is not hidden what arbitrariness (tahakkum) is in this, for there is no basis for restricting the sufficient increase to one power, since it is possible that more is needed.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage presents a specific discussion within the broader topic of divine power (qudra) as applied to physical motion. The question is whether a power that causes horizontal movement (right/left) can also cause vertical movement (upward). Some say yes, others say no, based on the observable difference between rolling (which involves both horizontal and rotational motion) and lifting. The Bahshamiyya school (followers of Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i) take the negative view and argue that vertical motion requires a separate power. They require exactly one additional power to be added. The commentator criticizes this as arbitrary (tahakkum): there is no rational reason to limit the needed increase to exactly one power; it might require more. This guards against assuming a fixed number of powers without evidence.

تصعيد
دحرجة
بهشمية
تحكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the Bahshamiyya's requirement of exactly one additional power is obviously correct and rationally necessary.
Correction: The commentator argues that specifying exactly one power is arbitrary (tahakkum) because there is no evidence to limit it; more powers could be needed.

Checkpoint

A) No additional power
B) One additional power
C) Two additional powers
D) It cannot be moved upward

Key Takeaway
The Bahshamiyya's claim that exactly one additional power is needed for vertical motion is criticized as arbitrary (tahakkum) because there is no evidence for that specific number.

The commentator calls the Bahshamiyya's exact number arbitrary (tahakkum). Are you willing to hold even respected opinions to the standard of evidence, or do you sometimes accept them without question? First Proof: Power Differs from Temperament in Genus
Sanusi presents the first proof that qudra differs from mizaj in genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite identification of qudra with mizaj is refuted by the fact that qudra is not sensible, while mizaj and its effects are.

Core Idea
Power is not perceived by touch; temperament is — different genus, different reality.

In the debate over the nature of divine attributes, a key question is whether power (qudra) is identical to temperament (mizaj), as some naturalists claimed. Sanusi here offers a crisp argument from sensory classification: if two things belong to different genera of qualities, they cannot be identical.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the logical structure: a premise about temperament, a premise about power, then a conclusion.
Pay attention to the phrase 'من وجهين' — this is only the first proof.
2
Identify the genus argument
The argument hinges on 'genus' (جنس). Temperament is a mean quality among the four tangible qualities. Its effect is also of that genus. Power is not perceived by touch, so it is of a different genus.
The four qualities are heat, cold, moisture, dryness.
3
Examine the conclusion
The text concludes that power is not identical to temperament but rather a subordinate quality (تابع). Reflect on what 'subordinate' means here: it implies dependence, not identity.
This distinction is crucial for Ash'ari theology.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this matters: if power were identical to temperament, then God's power would be a physical quality, which is impossible. The argument shows a fundamental ontological difference.
This is a simple but powerful classification argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القدرة مغايرة للمزاج من وجهين الأول: المزاج وأثره من جنس الكيفيات المحسوسة بالقوة اللامسة وذلك لأن المزاج كيفية متوسطة من الكيفيات الأربع المشهورة وهي في الحقيقة من جنسها إلا أنها منكسرة ضعيفة بالنسبة إليها فيكون أثرها وحكمها من جنس أحكام هذه الكيفيات إلا أنه يكون أضعف من أحكامها ولا شك أن أحكام هذه الأربع وآثارها من جنسها أيضاً فالمزاج وأثره من جنس الكيفيات الملموسة دون القدرة فإنها ليست مدركة باللمس وليس أثرها من جنس هذه الكيفيات فليست القدرة نفس المزاج بل هي كيفية تابعة له
Power is different from temperament in two respects: First: Temperament and its effect are of the genus of sensible qualities by the sense of touch, because temperament is an intermediate quality among the four well-known qualities, and it is in reality of their genus, except that it is broken and weak relative to them, so its effect and judgment are of the genus of the judgments of these qualities, except that it is weaker than their judgments. And there is no doubt that the judgments of these four and their effects are also of their genus. So temperament and its effect are of the genus of tangible qualities, unlike power (qudra), for it is not perceived by touch and its effect is not of the genus of these qualities. Thus power is not identical to temperament; rather it is a quality (kayfiyya) subordinate to it.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage provides the first of two proofs that power (qudra) is distinct from temperament (mizaj). The argument is a classification by genus: Temperament is a quality (kayfiyya) that is a mean between the four primary tangible qualities (heat, cold, moisture, dryness). As such, temperament and its effects are themselves sensible by the sense of touch. Power (qudra), on the other hand, is not perceived by touch, and its effects (e.g., causing motion) are not of the same genus as tangible qualities. Therefore, power cannot be identical to temperament; rather, it is a different kind of quality that is subordinate to temperament (i.e., depends on it but is not the same). This is a simple argument from difference in sensory accessibility.

Key Terms
قدرة
مزاج
كيفية
محسوسة
قوة لامسة
تابع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that because power is 'subordinate' (تابع) to temperament, it is actually the same thing or a part of temperament.
Correction: The text explicitly says 'ليست القدرة نفس المزاج' — power is not the same as temperament. 'تابع' indicates a distinct quality that depends on temperament, not identity.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason power is not identical to temperament?
A) Power is eternal
B) Power is not perceived by touch
C) Temperament is created
D) Power has no effect

Key Takeaway
Power (qudra) and temperament (mizaj) belong to different genera because temperament is sensible while power is not; hence they cannot be identical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you sometimes equate spiritual power (qudra) with your natural temperament or physical strength? In what specific situation today can you consciously rely on divine power rather than your own disposition?
?
If Allah's power is not touched or felt physically, how does that challenge your expectation of how He should act in your life? Are you willing to trust His power even when you cannot sense it?
?
Your temperament (mood, habits) often dominates your actions. What would change if you truly believed that a power of a different genus — not subject to your physical state — is available to you through Allah? What one step can you take to submit your temperament to that power?

Second Proof: Opposition in Fatigue Shows Power Distinct from Temperament
Second proof that qudra differs from mizaj: temperament opposes power in fatigue.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument from opposition that demonstrates created power (qudra) is a distinct attribute from bodily temperament (mizaj).

Core Idea
If power were temperament, it could not oppose itself – fatigue proves they differ.

This lesson presents a concise yet powerful proof from everyday experience: when we are fatigued, our temperament hinders our power. Since a thing cannot oppose itself, power and temperament must be separate realities. Follow the logical steps to see how this argument refutes the identity of qudra and mizaj.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read the source text carefully in both Arabic and English. Note the key terms: قدرة (power), مزاج (temperament), لغوب (fatigue). These are essential for the argument.
2
Identify the phenomenon of fatigue
The author uses the example of a person afflicted with fatigue (لغوب) and weariness (إعياء). Despite being fatigued, the person still acts by his power and choice. However, his temperament opposes his power in those very actions.
Fatigue here is not the power itself but a bodily state that makes action difficult.
3
Understand the logical principle
The argument hinges on the principle that 'a thing does not oppose itself.' If power and temperament were identical, they could not be in opposition. But in fatigue, we observe opposition, so they must be different.
This is a classic kalam argument from opposition (taqābul).
4
Draw the conclusion
Since opposition occurs, power (qudra) cannot be the same as temperament (mizaj). Therefore, they are two distinct realities. This supports the broader theme that qudra is a separate attribute from the body's nature.
This conclusion is stated explicitly at the end: 'So power is other than temperament.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: المزاج قد يمانع القدرة، كما عند اللغوب فإن من أصابه لغوب وإعياء يصدر عنه أفعاله بقدرته واختياره ومزاجه يمانع قدرته في تلك الأفعال والشيء لا يمانع نفسه فالقدرة غير المزاج
Second: Temperament may oppose power, as in fatigue (al-lughub). For one who is afflicted with fatigue and weariness, his actions issue from his power (qudra) and choice, but his temperament opposes his power in those actions. And a thing does not oppose itself, so power is other than temperament.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This is the second short proof that power (qudra) is distinct from temperament (mizaj). The argument is from opposition: When a person is fatigued (لغوب) or weary, he still acts by his own power and free choice, but his temperament (bodily constitution) opposes his power—it makes the actions difficult. Since one and the same thing cannot oppose itself (a thing cannot be its own hindrance), temperament and power cannot be identical. Therefore, they are two distinct realities.

Key Terms
قدرة
مزاج
لغوب
إعياء
مانعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that fatigue is a property or state of power itself, so that when we are tired, our power is diminished.
Correction: The passage uses fatigue to show that temperament (the bodily constitution) actively opposes power, implying temperament is a separate entity that hinders power rather than being identical to it.

Checkpoint
What phenomenon does the author use to show that temperament opposes power?
A) Sleep
B) Fatigue (al-lughub)
C) Eating
D) Thinking

Key Takeaway
Power (qudra) is not the same as temperament (mizaj) because temperament can oppose power, and nothing opposes itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a moment when you were utterly exhausted but still prayed or fulfilled a responsibility. Did you realize that your fatigue was not a sign of weak faith but a natural opposition from your body? How will you now view those moments as proof that your will (power) is distinct from your physical state?
?
If a thing cannot oppose itself, then where do your spiritual and bodily desires conflict? Could it be that your nafs (self) and your body are not one entity? How does this change your approach to mujahada (struggle)?
?
This proof uses a simple experience to show a deep truth. What small, everyday experience have you been ignoring that might reveal a great truth about Allah's attributes? Will you begin to look for signs in your own exhaustion and effort?

Active vs. Passive Power in the Definition of Quwwa
Debate on inclusion of passive power in definition of quwwa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how scholars debated whether the definition of power (quwwa) includes passive power, and how the phrase 'in another' can be interpreted to accommodate both.

Core Idea
The definition of power can include passive power through a broad interpretation of 'change'.

This lesson explores a key debate in Islamic philosophy: does the technical definition of power (quwwa) apply only to active power, or does it also encompass passive power? By examining the commentary on Ibn Sina's definition, you will see how scholars reconciled the wording to include both types.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and initial claim
Read the passage where the commentator states that power technically includes both active and passive, and note the use of 'change' in the definition.
The definition uses 'change' to cover both action and passion.
2
Understand the question about effective power
Consider the question: can 'effective power' be applied to passive power? See the response that scholars explicitly allow it by interpreting 'effect' as change.
This shows a deliberate broadening of the term.
3
Analyze the objection and resolution
Read the objection that 'in another' seems to restrict to active power, then see how 'otherness' can be relative to the agent, preserving inclusivity.
The resolution hinges on the interpretation of 'other'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( مبدأ التغير في آخر إلخ ) قال الشارح في حواشي التجريد : القوة بحسب الاصطلاح يتناول القوة الفعلية والانفعالية أعني التي تهيأ محلها نحو الفعل أو الانفعال فلذلك أخذوا في تعريفها التغير الشامل للفعل والانفعال، وقال أيضاً : فإن قلت : هل يطلق القوة المؤثرة على القوة الانفعالية . قلت : صرح بذلك بعضهم وادعى أنهم أرادوا بالتأثير هاهنا التغير مطلقاً سواء كان تأثيراً أو تأثراً، ثم قال بعد نقل قول الكاتبي : القوة بمعنى الصفة المؤثرة عرفها الشيخ بأنها مبدأ التغير في آخر من حيث هو آخر وهذا الكلام منه يؤيد إطلاق التأثير على المعنى المتناول للتأثير والقبول كما مر فقد ظهر لك من سياق كلامه أنه حمل التغير في تعريف الشيخ على المعنى الشامل للتأثير والتأثر وجعل التعريف شاملاً للقوة الفعلية والانفعالية، وفيه بحث لأن التغير بحسب مفهومه وإن كان أعم، إلا أن اعتبار كون ذلك التغير في آخر يدل على تخصيص التعريف بالقوة الفعلية لأن المتبادر هو المغايرة بين محل المبدأ ومحل التغير ولو بالاعتبار كما لا يخفى، اللهم إلا أن يقال : الآخرية معتبرة بالنسبة إلى الفاعل مطلقاً فحاصل التعريف أنها مبدأ التغير من شيء في آخر .

أن الإنسان هو هذا النوع الرابع - المقصد الثالث عشر : القوة هي جنس القدرة من حيث هو جسم ينفعل عما يلاقيه من الدواء، وهذا مبني على ما يتبادر إلى الجسم، والتحقيق أن المعالج المؤثر هو النفس الناطقة [؟] الأوهام من والمعالج المتأثر هو البدن وهما متغايران بالذات فالأولى أن يمثل بمعالجة الإنسان نفسه في إزالة الأخلاق الرديئة التي هي أمراض نفسانية، وإنما كان هذا القيد موجباً لعموم الحدود ودخول ما كان خارجاً عنه لأن المتبادر من لفظ الآخر هو المغاير بالذات فلما قيد بالحيثية علم أن التغاير بالاعتبار كاف والقوة بهذا المعنى تنقسم إلى أقسام أربعة لأن الصادر من القوة إما فعل واحد، أو أفعال مختلفة، وعلى التقديرين إما أن يكون لها شعور بما يصدر عنها أو لا، فالأول : النفس الفلكية والثاني: الطبيعة العنصرية وما في معناها والثالث : القوة الحيوانية والرابع: النفس النباتية، وقد مرت
His saying: (The principle of change in another, etc.) The commentator said in the glosses of al-Tajrid: Power, according to technical usage, encompasses active power and passive power, i.e., that which prepares its subject for action or passion. Therefore, they included in its definition change that encompasses act and passion. And he also said: If you say: Is 'effective power' applied to passive power? I say: Some have explicitly stated that, and claimed that they intended by 'effect' here change absolutely, whether it is effect or being affected. Then he said after quoting al-Katibi: Power in the sense of the effective attribute was defined by the master (Ibn Sina) as the principle of change in another insofar as it is another. This statement of his supports the application of effect to the meaning that encompasses effect and reception, as has passed. So it has become clear to you from the context of his speech that he interpreted 'change' in the master's definition as the comprehensive meaning encompassing effect and being affected, and made the definition inclusive of active and passive power. But there is discussion, because change in its concept, although more general, nevertheless the consideration that that change is in another indicates a specification of the definition to active power, because the immediate understanding is the difference between the locus of the principle and the locus of change, even if by consideration, as is not hidden. However, it could be said: 'otherness' is considered in relation to the agent absolutely, so the gist of the definition is that it is the principle of change from something in another.

That man is this fourth type – the thirteenth objective: Power is the genus of capability – insofar as he is a body that is acted upon by what meets it from the medicament, and this is based on what is immediately understood about the body. But the verification is that the effecting treater is the rational soul and the erroneous imaginations? and the affected treater is the body, and they are essentially distinct. So it is better to exemplify by a person treating himself in removing bad morals that are psychological diseases. And this qualification necessitated the generality of the definitions and the inclusion of what was outside them, because the immediate understanding of the term 'other' is that which is essentially distinct. So when it was qualified with 'insofar as', it is known that difference by consideration is sufficient. And power in this sense divides into four divisions: because what issues from power is either one act or different acts, and in both cases either it has awareness of what issues from it or not. So the first is the celestial soul, the second is the elemental nature and what is in its meaning, the third is the animal power, and the fourth is the vegetative soul. And they have passed.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage discusses whether Ibn Sina's definition of power (quwwa) includes passive power. The commentator cites glosses on al-Tajrid stating that technically 'quwwa' includes both active and passive. The gloss explains that the definition uses 'change' to cover both. A question is raised: can 'effective power' be applied to passive power? Some scholars accept this. Al-Katibi's quote shows that the definition's wording ('in another') supports inclusivity. However, the commentator notes a problem: 'in another' implies a distinction between principle and subject, which seems to favor active power. A resolution is offered: 'otherness' is relative to the agent, so passive power is not excluded. The passage then shifts to an example: a person treated by himself. The initial example is problematic because the soul and body are distinct; the commentator prefers an example of self-improvement. He explains that the qualification 'insofar as' broadens the definition. Finally, he presents a fourfold classification of power based on the type of act (single or multiple) and presence of awareness: celestial soul, elemental nature, animal power, and vegetative soul. This classification is stated as having 'passed' previously.

Key Terms
قوة فعلية
قوة انفعالية
تغير
آخرية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The definition of power (quwwa) applies only to active power.
Correction: The passage shows that through interpretation, the definition can include passive power as well.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the key term that allows the definition to include both active and passive power?
A) 'Effect' (ta'thir)
B) 'Change' (taghayyur)
C) 'Another' (akhar)
D) 'Principle' (mabda')

Key Takeaway
The debate over passive power hinges on the interpretation of 'change' and 'otherness', ultimately allowing a broad definition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this lesson, you saw that power can include both acting and being acted upon. In your own life, do you consider your ability to be receptive to truth and correction as a form of strength? When was the last time you intentionally embraced passive power—allowing God to change you—rather than striving to control every outcome? What would it look like this week to cultivate that posture in your relationship with your Creator?
?
The commentator argued that 'otherness' (آخرية) is relative. Do you sometimes feel that God is distant or 'other' in a way that prevents you from experiencing His power in your life? How does understanding that His power relates to you as a loving agent (not a disconnected cause) reshape your trust? What specific fear or doubt can you surrender to Him today because of this truth?
?
Words matter in defining divine attributes. Have you ever reduced God's power to only one dimension (e.g., only active control) and missed how He works through your receptivity? Think of a situation where you felt powerless—could it be that God was inviting you to experience His power through your passive trust? The Fourfold Classification of Power
Classification of power into four types based on act and awareness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and explain the four types of power based on the criteria of act multiplicity and awareness.

Core Idea
Power is classified into four types using two criteria: singular vs multiple acts, and presence of awareness.

In this lesson, you will learn a fourfold division of power that organizes different kinds of causal principles. The classification uses two criteria: whether the power produces one or many acts, and whether it has awareness of its acts. This yields four distinct types: celestial soul, elemental nature, animal power, and vegetative soul.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two criteria
Read the passage and find the two criteria used to classify power: (1) whether the power issues one act or multiple acts, and (2) whether it has awareness (shu'ur) of its acts.
These are binary criteria, giving 2x2=4 types.
2
Locate the four types
The passage names the four types: celestial soul, elemental nature, animal power, and vegetative soul. Note which combination of criteria each corresponds to.
Celestial soul: multiple acts with awareness; elemental nature: multiple acts without awareness; animal power: one act with awareness; vegetative soul: one act without awareness.
3
Reflect on the classification's purpose
Consider why this classification is useful in the broader discussion of power and causality in Islamic philosophy.
It shows how different kinds of natural and supernatural agents fit into a single framework.

Contentment (Ridā): Will or Abandonment of Objection?
Mu'tazila and Ash'ari views on contentment as will or abandonment of objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila and Ash'ari definitions of contentment (ridā) and how they affect the relationship between God's will and His contentment with evil.

Core Idea
Contentment is not the same as will — the Ash'aris distinguish them.

In this passage, al-Ījī presents two theological interpretations of contentment (ridā). The Mu'tazila equate it with will, while the Ash'aris define it as the abandonment of objection. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping how divine will relates to moral approval.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that two schools of thought are presented: Mu'tazila and Ash'ari. Pay attention to the key terms used.
The term 'according to us' refers to the Ash'ari position.
2
Identify the two definitions
Find the definition of contentment according to the Mu'tazila and according to the Ash'aris. Write them down or highlight them.
The Mu'tazila definition makes contentment identical to will; the Ash'ari definition makes it the abandonment of objection.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider the consequence: for the Mu'tazila, if God is not content with disbelief, He does not will it. For the Ash'aris, God wills disbelief but is not content with it. Why does this distinction matter?
This relates to the problem of evil and divine sovereignty.
4
Answer the checkpoint
Test your understanding by answering the multiple-choice question based solely on this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( الثاني ) من تلك الأمور ( عند المعتزلة أن الرضاء هو الإرادة فإذا لم يرض الله لعباده الكفر لم يكن مريداً له أيضاً ( وعندنا ) أن الرضاء هو ترك الاعتراض فالكفر مع كونه مراداً له ليس مرضياً عنده لأنه يعترض عليه.
قوله : ( لم يكن مريداً) فالكفر والفسق واقعان من غير إرادته تعالى عندهم. قوله : ( هو ترك الاعتراض أي الإرادة مع ترك الاعتراض لأن الرضى صفة وجودية.
(Second) Among those matters: (According to the Mu'tazila, contentment is the will. So if God is not content with disbelief for His servants, He also does not will it. And according to us (Ash'aris), contentment is the abandonment of objection. So disbelief, despite being willed by Him, is not something He is content with because He objects to it.) His saying: (He does not will it) means disbelief and immorality occur without His will according to them. His saying: (It is abandonment of objection) i.e., will with abandonment of objection, because contentment is an existent attribute.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage defines the second psychological quality: contentment (al-ridā). It presents two theological positions. The Mu'tazila hold that contentment is identical to will (al-irāda); thus if God is not content with disbelief for His servants, He does not will it either. The Ash'ari position (indicated by 'according to us') is that contentment is the abandonment of objection (tark al-i'tirāḍ). Consequently, God can will disbelief—since everything occurs by His will—but He is not content with it because He objects to it. The commentary clarifies: for the Mu'tazila, disbelief and immorality happen without God's will. For the Ash'aris, contentment is not simply the absence of objection but includes will along with the abandonment of objection, because contentment is a positive attribute (ṣifa wujūdiyya).

Key Terms
الرضاء
الإرادة
ترك الاعتراض
المعتزلة
الأشاعرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God wills something, He must also be content with it.
Correction: The Ash'ari view shows that God can will disbelief (since all things occur by His will) but not be content with it, because contentment is the abandonment of objection.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari view presented, what is contentment (ridā)?
A) The same as will
B) The absence of will
C) Abandonment of objection
D) Emotional pleasure

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari distinction between will and contentment allows God to will evil without approving it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God wills your existence and all that happens to you, but He is not content with some of your actions, how does that change the way you view the trials in your life? Can you trust His will while striving for His contentment?
?
The Ash'aris say God is not content with disbelief even though He wills it. Does this distinction make you more aware of your personal accountability? Are there areas where you have confused God's decree (what He wills) with His command (what He is content with)?
?
Consider the Mu'tazilite view: if God cannot will what He is not content with, then everything that happens is exactly what pleases Him. How does that affect your understanding of suffering and evil in the world? Which view drives you deeper into submission to God's wisdom?

Third Objection: Changing Relations and God's Immutability
Third objection: relative categories entail temporal accidents in God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection against the external existence of relative categories and why it entails that God would have temporal accidents, which is impossible.

Core Idea
God's unchanging nature cannot accommodate changing relations.

This lesson presents the third objection to the realist view of relative categories. The objector argues that if relations existed externally, God would be subject to temporal relations that change with events, contradicting His eternal and immutable nature. We will examine the argument in detail.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the three relations mentioned (before, with, after) and how the objector uses them.
The passage uses a tripartite structure to build the argument.
2
Identify the problem with the prior relation
Focus on why the relation of priority before an event is problematic. The objector says it ceases when the event occurs, but the eternal does not cease.
This is the key move that leads to contradiction.
3
Understand the overall objection
See how the objection concludes that if relations were real, God would have temporal accidents, which is impossible, so relations cannot exist externally.
This refutes the realist position on relative categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث: لو وجدت النسب في الخارج لزم اتصاف الباري تعالى بالحوادث لأن له مع كل حادث إضافة ثابتة إليه بأنه موجود معه وله قبله أي قبل كل حادث إضافة أخرى إليه بأنه متقدم عليه وله بعده إضافة ثالثة إليه بأنه متأخر عنه وهذه الإضافة حادثة، أما التي مع الحادث أو بعده فلا شبهة في حدوثها، وأما التي قبله فقد زالت حال وجوده والقديم لا يزول.
The third objection: If relations existed externally, it would necessitate that God Most High be characterized by temporal accidents, because He has with every temporal event a fixed relation to it, namely that He exists with it, and He has before it, i.e., before every temporal event, another relation to it, namely that He is prior to it, and He has after it a third relation to it, namely that He is posterior to it. And this relation is temporal. As for the one that is with the event or after it, there is no doubt about its temporality. As for the one before it, it has ceased at the time of its existence, and the eternal does not cease.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection, which involves God. If relative categories exist externally, then God would have relations to temporal events: He is simultaneous with them, prior to them, and posterior to them. These relations would be temporal accidents (hawadith). The relation that is simultaneous or posterior is clearly temporal because it depends on the event. The relation of being prior, however, would have ceased when the event occurs. But God is eternal (qadim) and immutable; what is eternal cannot cease to exist. Therefore, such changing relations cannot apply to God. Hence, relative categories cannot exist externally.

Key Terms
اتصاف الباري بالحوادث
الإضافة
حادثة
القديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The third objection denies that God has any relations at all.
Correction: The objection only denies that relations exist as external, changing entities. God's creative relation (e.g., being the Creator) is not denied but is seen as an attribute of His essence, not a temporal accident.

Checkpoint
Why is the relation of God being prior to an event considered problematic for the external existence of relative categories?
A) Because God is not prior to time
B) Because that relation would cease when the event occurs, but the eternal cannot cease
C) Because prior relations are impossible
D) Because God has no relations

Key Takeaway
The third objection shows that external relations would entail changing attributes in God, which is impossible because God is eternal and immutable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you genuinely believe that God is utterly unchanging, or do you secretly think your prayers or actions can 'affect' Him in a way that changes His attributes? Examine your heart: do you ever expect God to 'react' like a human?
?
The objection shows that even God's relation of being 'before you' would cease when you were born. If God's perfections are not increased by your existence or decreased by your sins, does that liberate you to worship Him for who He is, not for what He gives?
?
If you truly internalize that God has no temporal accidents, will you stop treating your relationship with Him as one that needs to be 'maintained' or 'restored' through your own efforts, and instead rely solely on His eternal mercy?

Mutakallimun's Admission of al-Ayn and Two Views on Spatial Obtaining
Al-Iji states that mutakallimun admit al-ayn and presents two views on its cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the mutakallimun, despite denying most Aristotelian categories, admitted the category of 'where' (al-ayn) and disagreed over its cause: whether it is the essence itself or an additional attribute.

Core Idea
The mutakallimun agree on the reality of spatial location but differ on its cause.

Al-Iji begins a new section on the mutakallimun's attitude toward the categories. While they reject most relative categories, they make an exception for the category of 'where' (al-ayn), which they call al-kawn. This passage presents two competing explanations among them for why a substance occupies space.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic carefully and then the literal translation. Notice that the passage cuts off mid-sentence; we are working with an incomplete text.
2
Identify the common ground
All mutakallimun admit the category of al-ayn and call it al-kawn. What do they disagree about?
3
Compare the two views
The majority say the obtaining in space is due to the substance's essence itself. The affirmers of states say it is due to an attribute. What does each view imply about the nature of spatial existence?
4
Reflect on the significance
This debate is about whether spatial location is a direct property of the substance or requires an additional attribute. It connects to broader discussions in kalam about attributes and states.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And the majority of them hold that the cause of obtaining in space is the essence of the substance, not an attribute subsisting in it; thus there are two things: the essence of the substance and the obtaining in space, called by them al-kawn. And a group among them—meaning the affirmers of states—hold that the obtaining of the substance in space is caused by an attribute subsisting in the substance, so they called the obtaining in space al-ka'iniyya and the attribute which is...

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on the first aim: the mutakallimun's denial of all other categories except al-ayn (the category of 'where'). They acknowledge al-ayn and call it al-kawn. The passage then presents two opinions among the mutakallimun. The majority opinion is that the cause of a substance's obtaining in space is the substance's essence itself, not an additional attribute. Therefore, there are two things: the essence and the obtaining, which they call al-kawn. The second opinion, held by the affirmers of states (muthbiti al-hal), is that the obtaining in space is caused by an attribute subsisting in the substance. They call the obtaining al-ka'iniyya and the attribute... (the text breaks off). Thus, the passage introduces the key debate within kalam about the nature of spatial existence.

Motion in Quality: The Non-Permanent Single Quality
Argues for a single non-permanent quality in motion, resolving the problem of discrete instants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument for a single non-permanent quality in motion and how it resolves the problem of discrete changes.

Core Idea
Motion in quality involves a single quality that changes at every instant, not a fixed one.

This passage extends the reasoning from motion in substance to motion in quality. The author argues that a single non-permanent quality avoids the problem of discrete instant changes. He rejects interpretations that would lead to contradiction with sensory experience.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through.
2
Identify the two rejected interpretations
The passage considers two interpretations of 'one quality' – specific (naw'iyya) and personal (shakhsiyya). Note why each is rejected.
The personal quality is rejected because sensory experience contradicts it.
3
Grasp the positive claim
The author asserts that the quality is non-permanent, changing at every instant, but without two connected qualities in the same instant.
This avoids the problem of discrete changes.
4
Understand the analogy with time
The analogy with instants in time shows that between any two supposed qualities, other qualities can be supposed, just as between any two instants.
This explains why no undesirable consequences follow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا نقول للمتحرك في الكيف كيفية واحدة غير قارة فيه بحث لأن المراد بالكيفية الواحدة إن كان واحدة نوعية لم يفد لأن الشبهة بالنظر إلى الكيفيات الشخصية بحالها، وإن أراد الواحدة الشخصية كما نقل عنه رحمه الله تعالى فمما يأباه العقل، إذ القول بأن للمتحرك في الألوان لوناً واحداً من أول الحركة إلى آخرها مما يأباه الضرورة الحسية ألا يرى أنهم عدوا الحركة من السواد إلى الصفرة إلى الخضرة إلى النيلية إلى البياض حركة واحدة، مع أن غير قارة، ففي كل آن يفرض يكون له فيه كيفية أخرى مفروضة ولا يمكن أن يفرض في تلك الكيفية غير القارة كيفيتان متصلتان، بل كل كيفيتين يفرض فيها يمكن أن يفرض فيما بينهما كيفيات أخر، كما أنَّ كل آنين يفرض في الزمان يمكن أن يفرض بينهما آنات أخر فلا يلزم شيء من المحذورات
And similarly we say concerning the mover in quality (kayf): one quality that is not permanent. There is discussion about it because if by 'one quality' is meant one specific quality, it is not useful, because the problem (shubha) regarding personal qualities remains as it is. And if he intends the personal one, as is transmitted from him (may God have mercy on him), then the intellect rejects it, since the claim that the mover in colors has one color from the beginning of the motion to its end is rejected by sensory necessity. Do they not see that they counted the motion from black to yellow to green to indigo to white as one motion, although it is not permanent? For in every instant that is supposed, he has another supposed quality in it, and it is impossible to suppose in that non-permanent quality two connected qualities; rather, between any two qualities supposed in it, other qualities can be supposed, just as between any two instants supposed in time, other instants can be supposed. Thus nothing of the undesirable consequences follows.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage extends the same reasoning to motion in the category of quality (kayf). The author argues for a single non-permanent quality (kayfiyya wahida ghayr qarra) to avoid the problem of discrete changes. He first dismisses the interpretation that the one quality is specific (naw'iyya), because the problem remains regarding personal (shakhsiyya) qualities. If it is taken as personal, the intellect and sensory experience reject it, as color changes are perceptible. Instead, the non-permanent quality changes at each instant, but without discrete connected qualities — analogous to instants separated by other instants. Thus no succession of instants or other problems arise.

Correction: The passage argues for a single non-permanent quality that changes at every instant, not a fixed one.

First Proof: Corporeality Cannot Be the Cause of Natural Motion
States the thesis and first proof with objection and response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof that natural motion's cause is not corporeality, and how the objection about a stone held in the air is resolved.

Core Idea
If corporeality were the cause, motion would never cease.

This lesson introduces a key argument from the Fourth Observation: natural motion is not caused by corporeality alone. The proof is simple but powerful—if the essence of a body necessitated motion, rest would be impossible. We will also see how an apparent counterexample (a stone held still in the air) is handled.

Guided Path
1
Read the thesis and proof
Read the opening statement: 'The cause of natural motion is not corporeality, otherwise motion would continue with its continuance.' Notice the logical conditional.
The argument is based on the principle that the requirement of an essence remains constant.
2
Understand the commentator's expansion
The commentator explains that the cause is not corporeality nor nature alone, but nature together with a 'non-conducive state'. This is the key nuance.
The non-conducive state refers to something external that triggers motion.
3
Examine the objection and response
Consider the objection: if nature plus a state causes motion, why does a stone held in the air not move? The response is that the discussion concerns actual existing motion, and in that case motion is prevented by force.
Force (external prevention) explains the lack of motion, not the absence of the cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الرابع : الحركة الطبيعية ليست هي الجسمية. العلة للحركة الطبيعية ليست هي الجسمية، وإلا دامت الحركة بدوامها أي بدوام الجسمية وامتنع السكون على الأجسام، لأن مقتضى ذات الشيء وحدها يبقى. قوله : ( العلة للحركة الطبيعية إلخ ) أي الحركة الطبيعية الموجودة في الأجسام أي حركة كانت لما سيجيء من قوله وهكذا نقول في الكيفيات والكميات لكونها ممكنة موجودة لا بد من علة تامة يقتضي وجودها اقتضاء تاماً فتلك العلة ليست الجسمية ولا الطبيعة فقط، بل مع الحالة الغير الملائمة وما قيل أن الطبيعة مع تلك الحالة للحركة للتخلف في مثل الحجر المسكن في الهواء فمندفع لأن الكلام في الحركة الموجودة .
The Fourth Observation - The Fourth Purpose: Natural motion is not corporeality. The cause of natural motion is not corporeality, otherwise motion would continue with its continuance, i.e., with the continuance of corporeality, and rest would be impossible for bodies, because the requirement of the essence of a thing alone remains. He said (i.e., the commentator): '(The cause of natural motion, etc.)' meaning the natural motion existing in bodies, whatever motion it may be, as will come in his saying: 'And thus we say regarding qualities and quantities' because they are possible, existing, and cannot avoid having a complete cause whose existence necessitates complete necessitation. So that cause is not corporeality nor nature alone, but rather together with the non-conducive state. And what is said that nature with that state is [؟] for motion to be delayed like the stone held in the air is refuted, because the discussion concerns existing motion.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage begins with the heading of the Fourth Purpose, stating that natural motion is not identical to corporeality. The author then gives the first proof: if the cause of natural motion were corporeality itself, then the motion would continue as long as the body exists, because the essence of a thing alone would necessitate it. But we know that bodies come to rest, so the cause must be something else. The commentator explains that the motion in question is actual motion, and its cause is not pure nature alone but nature combined with a non-conducive state (al-ḥāla al-ghayr mulāʾima). He then addresses an objection: if nature with that state causes motion, why does a stone held in the air not move downward? The answer is that the discussion concerns existing motion; in that case, the motion is prevented by force, so it does not exist, and the argument is about actual motion, not potential.

Key Terms

الجسمية
العلة التامة
الحالة الغير الملائمة
مقتضى ذات الشيء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that if nature alone is not the cause, then nature plays no role in motion.
Correction: The passage clarifies that nature combined with a non-conducive state is the cause; nature is still a component.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, why cannot corporeality be the cause of natural motion?
A) Because corporeality is not a real attribute.
B) Because if it were the cause, motion would be perpetual and rest impossible.
C) Because bodies are not subject to natural motion.
D) Because corporeality is common to all bodies.

Key Takeaway
Corporeality cannot be the cause of natural motion because its essence requirement would make motion perpetual, contradicting observed rest.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What one external condition can you set this week to renew your commitment?

What state can you change to align your actions with your true desire?

The proof shows that if corporeality were the cause, motion would never cease. In your life, what 'motion' (a good habit, a relationship with Allah) have you assumed would never stop because it is part of your nature? What if that assumption is false? The Fivefold Classification of Substance (جوهر)
Iji enumerates the five substances with conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five categories of substance as defined by Islamic philosophers: form, matter, body, soul, and intellect.

Core Idea
Substances are classified by whether they indwell, are a locus, are composite, or relate to body.

Before critiquing the classification, the passage first lays out the standard philosophical division of substance (jawhar). This division is based on whether a substance is indwelling in another (form), is a locus for something (matter), composed of both (body), or if neither, then either related to body as a manager (soul) or not (intellect). Understanding this taxonomy is essential for following the critique that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification passage
Read the Arabic passage describing the five categories. Note the conditional structure: if this, then that.
The passage uses parentheses which may be from OCR; ignore them.
2
Identify each category
List the five substances: form (sura), matter (hayula), body (jism), soul (nafs), intellect ('aql). Understand the defining condition for each.
Form is either bodily (jismiyya) or specific (naw'iyya).
3
Trace the decision tree
Visualize the classification as a flow: start with substance; is it hal (indwelling)? yes→form; no→is it mahall (locus)? yes→matter; no→is it murakkab (composite)? yes→body; no→is it related to body by tadbir? yes→soul; else→intellect.
This tree shows how the categories are mutually exclusive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في جوهر آخر (فصورة) إما جسمية أو نوعية وإن كان محلاً لها أي للصورة (فهيولى، وإن كان مركباً منها فجسم إما مطلق أو نوع منه ( وإلا ) أي وإن لم يكن الجوهر حالاً ولا محلاً ولا مركباً منهما فإن كان متعلقاً بالجسم تعلق التدبير والتصرف ) والتحريك ( فنفس وإلا فعقل وإنما قيدوا التعلق بالتدبير والتحريك لأن للعقل عندهم تعلقاً بالجسم على سبيل التأثير ( وهذا ) التقسيم الذي ذكروه ( بناء) أي مبني على نفي الجوهر الفرد) إذ على تقدير ثبوته لا صورة ولا هيولى ولا ما يتركب منهما بل هناك جسم مركب من جواهر فردة ( و ) على تقدير انتفاء الجوهر الفرد ( إنما يتم بعد أن أن الحال يبين في الغير قد يكون جوهراً) وهو ممنوع فإن الظاهر أن الحال في غيره يكون عرضا قائما به فلا يثبت جوهر حال ولا محل ولا ما يتركب من حال ومحل جوهرين ولا جوهر مجل لجوهر آخر ( و ) بعد أن يبين ( أن غير الجسم ) من الجواهر ( لا يتركب من جزأين أحدهما حال في الآخر وإلا لم أن الجوهر المركب من الجوهرين حال ومحل هو الجسم ( ولم يثبت شيء منهما ) أي من هذين البيانين ببرهان مع أن الأول مخالف للظاهر كما عرفت، والثاني مما لا جزم به لجواز وجود جوهر يكون محلاً لجوهر آخر، ولا يكون شيء منهما قابلاً للإشارة الحسية، فلا يكون ذلك المحل هيولى ولا الحال صورة ولا المركب منهما جسماً
Concerning another substance: (a form) either bodily or specific; and if it is a locus for it, i.e., for the form, (then matter); and if it is composed of them, then a body, either absolute or a type thereof; (otherwise) i.e., if the substance is neither indwelling nor a locus nor composed of them, then if it is related to the body by the relation of management and disposal and movement, then a soul; otherwise an intellect. They restricted the relation to management and movement because for them the intellect has a relation to the body by way of influence. And this division that they mentioned is built on the denial of the individual atom; for if it were affirmed, there would be no form, no matter, and no composition from them, but rather a body composed of individual atoms. And on the supposition of the denial of the individual atom, it is only completed after it is shown that that which indwells in another may be a substance – but this is denied, for the apparent is that that which indwells in another is an accident subsisting in it, so neither an indwelling substance nor a locus nor a composition from an indwelling substance and a locus is established, nor a substance that is a locus for another substance. And after it is shown that other than the body among substances does not compose from two parts one of which indwells in the other – otherwise the substance composed of two substances, indwelling and locus, would be the body – and neither of these two demonstrations is proven by a proof, while the first contradicts the apparent as you know, and the second is not certain because it is possible that a substance exists that is a locus for another substance, neither of them being subject to sensory indication, so that locus would not be matter, nor the indwelling a form, nor the composite from them a body.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage presents the standard philosophical classification of substances (jawhar) into five types: form (sura), matter (hayula), body (jism), soul (nafs), and intellect ('aql). It then immediately critiques the foundations of this classification. The author explains that the classification relies on two unproven premises: (1) the denial of the individual atom (jawhar fard), because if atoms existed, there would be no form and matter composing a body, only atoms; (2) the assumption that an indwelling thing (hal) can be a substance – but the apparent view is that what indwells in another is an accident, not a substance. The second premise is that non-corporeal substances do not compose from two parts, one indwelling in the other, else the composite would be a body. Neither premise is proven: the first contradicts evident appearance, the second is uncertain because it is possible for a substance to be a locus for another substance without either being sensible. Thus the classification is not soundly established.

Key Terms
جوهر
صورة
هيولى
جسم
نفس
عقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Form (صورة)
B) Matter (هيولى)
C) Body (جسم)
D) Soul (نفس)

Key Takeaway
Substances are classified into five types based on whether they are indwelling, a locus, composite, or related to body by management.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this classification, the soul is defined by its management and movement of the body. When you consider your own actions, do you experience your soul as actively managing your body, or do you let your body's desires lead? What would change if you recognized your soul's role more consciously?
?
The intellect is described as influencing the body without direct management. Reflect on a time when knowledge or reasoning changed your behavior without you deliberately managing it. How can you cultivate that influence more intentionally in your daily decisions?
?
The classification distinguishes between form, matter, body, soul, and intellect. Which of these do you most identify with your own existence? Are you more aware of your body or your soul? How does this affect your spiritual practice and your relationship with your Creator?

Divine Attributes

The Problematic Foundations of the Classification
Iji criticizes the classification as dependent on unproven premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the standard classification of substances is considered unsound due to its reliance on the denial of the individual atom and two unproven premises.

Core Idea
The classification of substances rests on the denial of atoms and unproven claims about indwelling.

After presenting the classification of substances, the passage immediately critiques its foundations. The author argues that the entire division depends on the denial of the individual atom (jawhar fard) and two additional premises that are themselves unproven: that an indwelling thing can be a substance (rather than an accident) and that non-corporeal substances do not consist of two parts where one indwells in the other. This lesson unpacks that critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the critique passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully. It begins by stating that the classification is built on nafy al-jawhar al-fard (denial of the individual atom).
Note the conditional: if atoms existed, there would be no form, matter, or composite.
2
Identify the two unproven premises
The passage lists two premises: (1) that an indwelling thing (hal) can be a substance; (2) that non-corporeal substances are not composed of two parts with one indwelling in the other.
Both are needed for the classification to hold (if atoms are denied).
3
Understand the objections
The first premise contradicts the apparent: what indwells is usually an accident. The second is uncertain because a substance could be a locus for another without being sensible.
Thus neither premise is proven, so the classification is not established.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في جوهر آخر (فصورة) إما جسمية أو نوعية وإن كان محلاً لها أي للصورة (فهيولى، وإن كان مركباً منها فجسم إما مطلق أو نوع منه ( وإلا ) أي وإن لم يكن الجوهر حالاً ولا محلاً ولا مركباً منهما فإن كان متعلقاً بالجسم تعلق التدبير والتصرف ) والتحريك ( فنفس وإلا فعقل وإنما قيدوا التعلق بالتدبير والتحريك لأن للعقل عندهم تعلقاً بالجسم على سبيل التأثير ( وهذا ) التقسيم الذي ذكروه ( بناء) أي مبني على نفي الجوهر الفرد) إذ على تقدير ثبوته لا صورة ولا هيولى ولا ما يتركب منهما بل هناك جسم مركب من جواهر فردة ( و ) على تقدير انتفاء الجوهر الفرد ( إنما يتم بعد أن أن الحال يبين في الغير قد يكون جوهراً) وهو ممنوع فإن الظاهر أن الحال في غيره يكون عرضا قائما به فلا يثبت جوهر حال ولا محل ولا ما يتركب من حال ومحل جوهرين ولا جوهر مجل لجوهر آخر ( و ) بعد أن يبين ( أن غير الجسم ) من الجواهر ( لا يتركب من جزأين أحدهما حال في الآخر وإلا لم أن الجوهر المركب من الجوهرين حال ومحل هو الجسم ( ولم يثبت شيء منهما ) أي من هذين البيانين ببرهان مع أن الأول مخالف للظاهر كما عرفت، والثاني مما لا جزم به لجواز وجود جوهر يكون محلاً لجوهر آخر، ولا يكون شيء منهما قابلاً للإشارة الحسية، فلا يكون ذلك المحل هيولى ولا الحال صورة ولا المركب منهما جسماً
Concerning another substance: (a form) either bodily or specific; and if it is a locus for it, i.e., for the form, (then matter); and if it is composed of them, then a body, either absolute or a type thereof; (otherwise) i.e., if the substance is neither indwelling nor a locus nor composed of them, then if it is related to the body by the relation of management and disposal and movement, then a soul; otherwise an intellect. They restricted the relation to management and movement because for them the intellect has a relation to the body by way of influence. And this division that they mentioned is built on the denial of the individual atom; for if it were affirmed, there would be no form, no matter, and no composition from them, but rather a body composed of individual atoms. And on the supposition of the denial of the individual atom, it is only completed after it is shown that that which indwells in another may be a substance – but this is denied, for the apparent is that that which indwells in another is an accident subsisting in it, so neither an indwelling substance nor a locus nor a composition from an indwelling substance and a locus is established, nor a substance that is a locus for another substance. And after it is shown that other than the body among substances does not compose from two parts one of which indwells in the other – otherwise the substance composed of two substances, indwelling and locus, would be the body – and neither of these two demonstrations is proven by a proof, while the first contradicts the apparent as you know, and the second is not certain because it is possible that a substance exists that is a locus for another substance, neither of them being subject to sensory indication, so that locus would not be matter, nor the indwelling a form, nor the composite from them a body.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage presents the standard philosophical classification of substances (jawhar) into five types: form (sura), matter (hayula), body (jism), soul (nafs), and intellect ('aql). It then immediately critiques the foundations of this classification. The author explains that the classification relies on two unproven premises: (1) the denial of the individual atom (jawhar fard), because if atoms existed, there would be no form and matter composing a body, only atoms; (2) the assumption that an indwelling thing (hal) can be a substance – but the apparent view is that what indwells in another is an accident, not a substance. The second premise is that non-corporeal substances do not compose from two parts, one indwelling in the other, else the composite would be a body. Neither premise is proven: the first contradicts evident appearance, the second is uncertain because it is possible for a substance to be a locus for another substance without either being sensible. Thus the classification is not soundly established.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume the fivefold classification of substances is an absolute and proven division.
Correction: The passage shows the classification depends on unproven premises: denial of atoms, and the claims that indwelling can be substance and non-bodies not composite.

Checkpoint
What is the first unproven premise required for the classification (assuming denial of atoms)?
A) That atoms exist
B) That an indwelling thing can be a substance
C) That bodies are composed of form and matter
D) That the intellect is a substance

Key Takeaway
The philosophical classification of substances is not firmly established because it depends on the unproven denial of atoms and two additional unproven premises about indwelling and composition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you accept a theological or philosophical claim, do you ever examine whether its foundational premises are actually proven, or do you rely on the authority of the speaker? Think of one belief you hold that might rest on an unexamined assumption.
?
Iji points out that the philosophers' classification contradicts the apparent (zahir). In your own spiritual journey, are there teachings or doctrines you have accepted that seem to conflict with simple, clear truths? What might be preventing you from re-evaluating them?
?
The passage shows that even learned people can build systems on shaky ground. How confident are you that the core beliefs of your faith are built on solid proof (burhan) rather than inherited assumptions? What concrete step can you take this week to strengthen your knowledge on one of those beliefs?
